

2008 WL 762668 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEXTER LANCELOT RODNEY A.K.A. RODNEY DEXTER A.K.A. DEXTER RODNOY
FILE: AXX XX7 175 - YORK, PA
March 6, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandra Greene, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision finding him removable under section 237(a)(2XA)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. He also moves to remand, asserting that his trial attorney rendered ineffective assistance in conceding his removability (Tr. at 20-21). The Department of Homeland Security has not filed an appeal brief or a response to the motion to remand. The motion will be granted, and the case will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º SeeLu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3rd Cir. 2001) (approving the Lozada requirements). In order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance, the Third Circuit has stated that an alien “must demonstrate prejudice, he ‘must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”’ Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3rd Cir. 2002), citing Uspango v. Asheroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3rd Cir. 2002).
 
We conclude that the respondent has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that a competent attorney would have challenged the respondent’s removability based on the aggravated felony charge listed in the Notice to Appear. As the respondent outlines in his appeal brief, there are arguable issues as to whether the DHS met its burden of establishing that the respondent’s bank fraud conviction involved a loss to a victim or victims exceeding $10,000 to constitute an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(M) of the Act. Because the respondent “was prevented from reasonably presenting his case,” we find that he established the requisite prejudice to support a remand for a hearing on removability. See Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision will be vacated, the motion to remand will be granted, and the case remanded to Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision is hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

2008 WL 762667 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IVANIA ZOMORA SOTELO
FILE: AXX XX7 971 - NEW YORK, NY
March 6, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Wishnie, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela Marra
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on December 7, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s August 23, 2007, decision denying her motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2007, denial of her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against her on June 5, 1997. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider and/or reopen with the Board. The motions are denied.
 
Through her current motion to reconsider, the respondent contends that the Board erred in our December 7, 2007, decision dismissing her appeal because (1) the Board did not address her claim of lack of notice for her June 5, 1997, hearing; (2) the Board did not address her ineffective assistance of counsel claim insofar as she claims that she exhibited due diligence in pursuing her motion to reopen and rescind; and, (3) the Board failed to address the denial of her request to file for asylum based upon changed country conditions in her motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. See Respondent’s Motion at 9-21. Further, the respondent contends that a motion to reopen has been granted to pursue asylum in her removal proceedings filed under a different alien number and name.¿FN1º Id. As a result, she alleges that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) is “estopped” from re-litigating the issue in these proceedings. Id. at 10-11.
 
First, we point out that the entirety of the Board’s December 7, 2007, decision in this case was dedicated to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and lack of notice. In fact, we specifically stated that the respondent argued both a lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel based upon her contention that her prior counsel acted ineffectively by failing to provide her notice of her June 5, 1997, hearing and the possibility of the entry of an in absentia removal order should she fail to appear. See Board’s December 7, 2007, Decision at 3. In this regard, we ultimately concluded that the respondent failed to establish that she exercised due diligence in pursuing her case where she waited 10 years to file a motion to reopen after departing the United States for years with the knowledge that her original motion to reopen was granted in 1997. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). As a result, the respondent’s assertion that the Board failed to address her lack of notice and ineffective assistance of counsel claims is wholly without merit.
 
*2 Moreover, the record reflects that the respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision regarding her request to file an application for asylum based upon alleged changed country conditions in Nicaragua. Rather, the respondent’s Notice of Appeal and appellate brief before the Board argued only the denial of her motion to rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against her. Thus, the respondent cannot now argue that the Board committed legal and/or factual error in failing to adjudicate an appellate claim that she never raised. In light of the foregoing, the respondent’s motion for reconsideration will be denied for her failure to sustain her burden in establishing that the Board committed factual and/or legal errors in our December 7, 2007, decision dismissing her prior appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 (2007).
 
We now turn to the respondent’s current motion to reopen to apply for asylum based upon alleged changed country conditions in Nicaragua. Here, we note that there is no time limit on the filing of a motion to reopen if the basis of the motion is to apply for relief under section 208 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158, and is based upon changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality or the country to which removal has been ordered. See section 240(d)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(d)(C)(ii). However, the respondent has not filed an asylum application with her motion to demonstrate that she has a well-founded fear of persecution based upon those changed conditions. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish prima facie eligibility for asylum and withholding of removal, and we see no reason to remand these proceedings.¿FN2º
 
Accordingly, the motion are denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The record reflects that the respondent was ordered removed from the United States in absentia in separate proceedings under a different alien number and name because she reentered the United States in 2001 under a false name.

2

We find it unnecessary to address the respondent’s contentions regarding whether the DHS is estopped from opposing her motion to reopen based upon changed country conditions inasmuch as the DHS did not oppose her motion on this basis. Therefore, the issue is moot.

2008 WL 762682 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARINO CABRAL
FILE: AXX XX7 370 - BOSTON, MA
March 3, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adam S. Elman, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(l)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated November 1, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has appealed that decision. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely because it was filed on September 8, 2005, more than 90 days after the respondent was ordered removed on July 2, 2001. See 8C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Moreover, the Immigration Judge did not find that exceptional circumstances were present, even if such circumstances could justify the untimeliness of the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to demonstrate that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, because he did not satisfy any of the procedural requirements for attorney negligence as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1stCir. 1988), nor has he explained his failure to do so (I.J. at 4, 5). Failure to meet the Lozada requirements will usually be fatal to the claim. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (discussion of policy considerations underlying Lozada requirements); aff’d Rivera v. INS, 122 F. 3d 1062 (4th Cir. 1997). The respondent refers to the Ninth Circuit case, Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000), and contends that strict adherence to the Lozada requirements is not required when the record of ineffective assistance of counsel is legitimate and substantial. At the outset, we note that this case arises in the jurisdiction of the First Circuit, and the respondent has not shown that the First Circuit has adopted the reasoning of the Ninth Circuit in Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra. The First Circuit has held that the Lozada requirements may be applied as long as the court does not arbitrarily demand adherence to them. Saakian v. INS, 252 F.3d 21, 26 (1st Cir. 2001); I.J. at 4.¿FN1º Here, the failure to satisfy any of the Lozada requirements is fatal to this claim. Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9 (1st Cir. 2004). Because the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely, the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion is dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Board has held that attorney negligence is not an exception to a statutory time limit for the filing of a motion to reopen. Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) and Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998).

2008 WL 762745 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ETZHAK NIMNI
FILE: AXX XX3 973 - EL PASO, TX
February 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victoria Calle, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Corine Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated December 6, 2007. Therein, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia on June 29, 1999. The record will be remanded.
 
A motion to reconsider is a “request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of Ramos, 23 I&N Dec. 336, 338 (BIA 2002) (quoting Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.2 (BIA 1991)). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Matter of Cerna, supra, at 402. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, as opposed to a motion to reopen, which seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. Id at 403; see also section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
The respondent argues in his motion that the Board incorrectly concluded that he was notified of the hearing. He states that his former counsel withdrew as his representative on May 19, 1999, and that he did not receive a notice of hearing either from his former counsel or from the court. The respondent further argues that there is no proof that the Immigration Court actually mailed the Notice of Hearing to him. In addition, the respondent argues in his motion that the Board incorrectly determined that he failed to satisfy the procedural requirements for showing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). He contends that former counsel was notified of the complaint filed against him with the Texas Bar Association as a part of the bar complaint process.
 
We find in light of the allegation of non-receipt of the hearing notice that it is appropriate to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to more fully address whether the respondent was adequately notified of the hearing. See Maknojiya v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 588, 589 (5th Cir. 2005) (noting the lower burden required for rebutting the presumption of delivery for correspondence sent by regular mail was met where both alien and his counsel submitted affidavits indicating that they did not receive the notice that reset the hearing from an earlier hearing date than originally scheduled). In addition, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge did not address the issue of whether the respondent was victimized by the negligence of his former counsel, Mr. Rosenbloom. We find it appropriate for the Immigration Judge to render the initial findings of fact on the issue.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is granted, and these proceedings are remanded for further proceedings.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 762745 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 762742 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWIN FRU NGUTI
FILE: AXX XX6 806 - BUFFALO, NY
February 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William H. Berger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel M. Griebel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º -In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Cameroon, appeals an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 21, 2006. On August 4, 2006, the respondent failed to appear for a hearing pursuant to a notice mailed to his address on June 22, 2006, and the Immigration Judge ordered him removed to Cameroon in absentia (I.J. at 2). On September 1, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to rescind the removal order and reopen proceedings with the Immigration Judge alleging that he failed to appear for the Immigration Court hearing due to a lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Following the Immigration Judge’s September 21, 2006, denial of the respondent’s motion, the respondent filed a timely appeal with the Board. The request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The respondent’s appeal will be sustained and the removal proceedings will be reopened.
 
As an initial matter, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion upon concluding that the respondent failed to show — either through an affidavit or other evidence — that he did not receive notice of the August 4, 2006, hearing (I.J. at 2-3). Although the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent did not rebut the presumption of effective delivery, we find that the removal order should be rescinded. First, we note that the respondent initiated correspondence with the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) as a result of his prior submission of his asylum application, which was confirmed by the DHS arresting officer in his notation in the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien. As a result, the respondent arguably had little motive to avoid the scheduled hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 6). See generally Lopes v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 81, 86 (2d Cir. 2006) (remanding, in part, because the Board did not consider all relevant evidence offered to rebut the presumption of delivery, including the fact that the petitioner — through the filing of an application for a labor certification — initiated a proceeding to obtain a benefit, which makes it less likely that he would ignore, or have such motive to, an immigration proceeding for which he had notice); see also Alrefae v. Chertoff, 471 F.3d 353, 359 (2d Cir. 2006). Also, the respondent did not delay in seeking a reopening of the proceedings as he, upon receiving the in absentia removal order, apparently retained Mr. Mark Kenmore as his counsel, who filed the motion on September 1, 2006.
 
*2 In addition, while not discussed in the Immigration Judge’s decision, the DHS in this matter did not oppose the respondent’s motion to rescind the removal order and reopen proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 2). Indeed, as evidenced through the DHS’s statement on appeal, it continues to assert its non-opposition to the respondent’s motion (Respondent’s Br. at 3, 6). Moreover, as indicated in the respondent’s brief, the respondent’s prior attorney, Mr. Kenmore, may have rendered ineffective assistance to the respondent prior to his withdrawal of the case (Respondent’s Br. at 3-4). Although the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel, he suggests that such defective representation is apparent from the record as Mr. Kenmore — despite suffering from cognitive difficulties and memory problems and being under the care of a neurologist since June 2006 — continued to represent the respondent before the Immigration Judge and the Board. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Also, the respondent offers the fact that no supporting documentation was provided with the motion to the Immigration Judge as further evidence of such inadequate representation.¿FN1º
 
Therefore, we will sustain the respondent’s appeal, vacate the Immigration Judge’s decision, and rescind the in absentia order. As we find that the proceedings should be reopened, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings as may be appropriate under the circumstances. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600 (BIA 1978). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is hereby remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent now presents sworn affidavits from himself and his uncle attesting to the non-delivery of the June 22, 2006, notice (Respondent’s Br. at 5-6), we note that the Board may not accept such documents into the record as it is precluded from engaging in factfinding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv).

2008 WL 762758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERTON ALPHONSO MCKENZIE
FILE: AXX XX4 706 - MIAMI, FL
February 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Geoffrey A. Hoffman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David J. Kelly
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
This case was last before us on May 28, 2003, when we remanded the record of proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). On February 13, 2007, the Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 13, 2007, decision. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand in order to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion to remand. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand will be denied. Additionally, the respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
 
On appeal, the respondent does not contest that he was convicted by a jury for the offense of criminal possession of marijuana in the fourth degree and criminally using drug paraphernalia in the second degree. Instead, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge was bound by the law of our May 28, 2003, remand to consider his application for section 212(c) relief. The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge erred by holding that he is ineligible for section 212(c) relief because he was convicted pursuant to a jury trial, rather than pursuant to a guilty plea. Additionally, the respondent raises due process arguments. In his motion to remand, the respondent contends that his proceedings should be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that he can apply for relief under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
Turning first to the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is ineligible for section 212(c) relief (I. J. at 2). Specifically, we find that the respondent’s conviction by jury trial does not fall within the ambit of the United States Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) (holding that the repeal of section 212(c) of the Act by the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act (“IIRIRA”) could not be applied retroactively to aliens who pled guilty or nolo contendere to a crime prior to IIRIRA’s enactment). See Alexandre v. United States Attorney General 452 F.3d 1204, 1207 (11th Cir. 2006) (finding that a section 212(c) waiver was precluded because the alien had proceeded to trial and was convicted by jury). To the extent that the respondent relies on the decisions in other federal courts of appeal that have found that section 212(c) relief may be available to aliens who were convicted after a jury trial, we find that those decisions are not binding on the present matter as they did not arise in the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit (the “Eleventh Circuit’), the jurisdiction in which this case arises. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989) (explaining that the Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit). Moreover, we reject the respondent’s contention on appeal that the Eleventh Circuit has only issued decisions containing non-binding dicta indicating that relief under former section 212(c) of the Act is unavailable to aliens who have been convicted pursuant to a jury trial. See Alexandre v. United States Attorney General, supra, at 1207. We also reject the respondent’s assertion on appeal that the law of the case doctrine forbad the Immigration Judge from pretermitting his application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. Although we remanded the record of proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act in light of INS v. St. Cyr, supra, the respondent concedes on appeal that our remand was based on a conviction document that erroneously indicated that he had been convicted pursuant to a guilty plea. See respondent’s motion to remand at pg. 3, n.1. In this regard, we reject the respondent’s argument on appeal that it was incumbent upon the DHS to determine prior to our remand that the respondent had been convicted pursuant to a jury trial rather than pursuant to a guilty plea. Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent argues that the relevant law as applied to him is unconstitutional, we have no jurisdiction over the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations we administer. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Turning next to the respondent’s motion to remand, we find that remand of proceedings is not warranted. With his motion to remand, the respondent has submitted an application for relief under section 212(h) of the Act, as well as supporting documentation. We find that the respondent has not adequately demonstrated that the proffered evidence with his motion to remand was not previously available or could not have been presented during his hearing before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1002.3(c)(1), (c)(4); see generally Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992) (providing that an alien seeking reopening based on a failure to request relief before the completion of deportation proceedings must meet the general motion requirements). Moreover, we note that, even if the Immigration Judge failed to advise the respondent of his right to apply for relief under section 212(h) of the Act during the initial proceedings, as argued by the respondent in his motion, the respondent was nonetheless aware of the possibility for such relief during the remanded proceedings and still failed to apply at that time (Tr. at 16-17). To the extent that the respondent claims that his failure to request relief under section 212(h) of the Act before the Immigration Judge was the result of ineffective assistance by his counsel, he has made no attempt to comply with the procedural requirements set forth under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 762758 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 762771 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XUAN QUAN
FILE: AXX XX3 565 - SAN ANTONIO, TX
February 27, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Albert Chow, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s May 10, 2007, decision denying her¿FN1º motion to rescind the order of removal entered in her case on an in absentia basis. We have considered the respondent’s appellate arguments as set forth on her Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), her statement attached thereto, and in her subsequently-filed brief. Inasmuch as we agree with the Immigration Judge’s resolution of this case, we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, with the exception of the discussion found on pages 5 and 6 suggesting frivolous behavior on the part of the respondent’s current attorney. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
On appeal, the respondent’s current attorney asserts that the respondent’s supporting declaration sufficiently establishes that she did not receive notice of her March 5, 2007, hearing from her former attorney. See Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26); Respondent’s Brief on Appeal. We disagree. In that declaration, the respondent indicates only that upon her release from detention she provided her new address in New Jersey to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and to the Immigration Court. She makes no specific claims of having provided this address to her former attorney, Ronald Higgins,¿FN2º having kept in touch with attorney Higgins, or that attorney Higgins failed to apprise her that a hearing would be conducted in her case on March 5, 2007. See Exh. 8 (Motion, attached declaration).
 
There also is no indication that prior to the March 5th proceeding attorney Higgins was granted a withdrawal of his representation of the respondent, or that substitute counsel entered an appearance in his stead. Rather, the record reflects that attorney Higgins appeared at the hearing as scheduled, and that the Immigration Judge found he offered no reasonable cause accounting for the respondent’s absence. See Exh. 6 (March 5, 2007, I.J. dec); see also May 10, 2007, I.J. dec at 5.
 
Under the circumstances presented, the respondent was given proper notice of her March 5, 2007, hearing in accordance with the federal regulatory requirements. See I.J. at 5; 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (2007); see also Men Keng Chang v. Jiugni, 669 F.2d 275, 277(5th Cir. 1982); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985); Exh. 3 (Form EOIR-28, filed November 17, 2006, in Immigration Court by attorney Higgins) and Exh. 5 (hearing notice). In arguing otherwise, the respondent’s reliance on case law handed down by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit is misplaced, inasmuch as the instant case arises in the Fifth, not the Ninth, Circuit.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent’s current attorney asserts that the respondent received ineffective assistance by her former attorney. However, none of the requirements for advancing such a claim, as discussed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), have been satisfied. Citing Ninth Circuit case law, the respondent contends that compliance with the Lozada requirements are not necessary in this case. See Brief, pp. 4-5. However, as has already been noted Ninth Circuit case law is not controlling here. In any event, the Fifth Circuit has specifically held that to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien in removal proceedings must fulfillthe Lozada requirements and must demonstrate that counsel’s actions were prejudicial to his case. See Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000). No such showings have been made here.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

1

In his filings, current counsel inconsistently refers to the respondent in both the male and female gender. Compare Exh. 8 (Motion to Reopen (Motion) filed with the San Antonio Immigration Court April 23, 2007) with Respondent’s Brief on Appeal (Brief). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was female. See May 10, 2007, I.J. dec; see also Record of Deportable Alien (Form I-213). 2008 WL 655944 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALINDO GOMES
FILE: AXX XX7 215 - HARTFORD, CT
February 19, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
A. Manuel Nieves, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 This natter was last before the Board on November 19, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s July 30, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen and to rescind therein absentia order of deportation entered against him on October 15, 1992. The respondent has filed with the Board a second motion to reopen, alleging ineffective assistance by his former counsel. The motion will be denied for lack of jurisdiction.
 
The respondent contends on appeal that he did not attend his scheduled deportation hearing on October 15, 1992, due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The respondent further argues that his former counsel’s deficient performance constitutes “reasonable cause” for his failure to appear under former section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
As initial matter we note, as we did in our November 19, 2007, decision, that the respondent’s case is governed by section 242B of the Act, not former section 242(b) of the Act, because the notice of hearing in this case was issued on July 16, 1992, after the June 13, 1992, effective date of section 242B of the Act See Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). Thus, under section 242B(c) of the Act, an in absentia order of deportation may only be rescinded upon a motion to reopen, filed before the Immigration Judge, within 180 days of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that his failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances, or at any time if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive proper notice of the hearing. See section 242B(c) of the Act; Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, supra. The Immigration Judge already has ruled on July 30, 2007, that the respondent had proper notice of his hearing because notice was properly mailed to his counsel of record, which we affirmed in our November 19, 2007, decision. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to an alien’s counsel of record constitutes notice to the alien). If the respondent is seeking now to allege that his former attorney’s ineffective assistance constitutes exceptional circumstances to excuse his failure to appear, he was required to file a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge within 180 days of the in absentia order of deportation. See section 242B(c) of the Act; Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act); Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied
 
Roger A. Pauley

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RODRIGO OCTAVIO GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX8 082 - LOS ANGELES, CA
February 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven E. Comer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on November 14, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order entered on January 17, 2006.
 
On December 26, 2007, the respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, filed a motion to reopen and rescind the January 17, 2006, in absentia removal order. The respondent’s motion is based, in part, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by his two prior attorneys, Mr. David Paz Soldan and Mr. James F. Kajtoch.
 
We will reopen these proceedings on our own motion because we failed to consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims against his prior attorneys, Mr. Paz Soldan and Mr. Kajtoch, which he raised on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent’s appeal brief, filed on September 25, 2006, detailed the allegations of his prior attorneys’ wrongdoing. When the respondent presented these claims on appeal, he proffered documentation that established his compliance with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4-9, Exhibit 4. Yet, we did not consider these claims. See Board’s November 14, 2006, decision at 5.
 
The ineffective assistance of counsel claims raised by the respondent on appeal are the same as those he presents in the instant motion. He claims that on January 17, 2006, he arrived at 8:15 a.m. for his scheduled 8:30 A.M. removal hearing. When he did not find his attorney, Mr. Paz Soldan, he called the law office and was instructed to wait outside the courtroom until his attorney arrived. Mr. Paz Soldan did not arrive until 9:30 A.M. By the time they entered the courtroom together it was empty. When Mr. Paz Soldan failed to file a promised motion to reopen, the respondent retained Mr. Kajtoch.
 
According to the respondent’s August 31, 2006, statement, which was submitted on appeal, when Mr. Kajtoch assisted him in filing a motion to reopen he insisted that the respondent state that he understood that the hearing was scheduled for 9:30 A.M. and that he arrived at the courtroom at 9:15 A.M. The respondent claims that he did not want to tell a lie, but Mr. Kajtoch insisted that it was necessary to obtain reopening. Mr. Kajtoch also advised the respondent not to file a Lozada claim against Mr. Paz Soldan. The respondent also asserted that Mr. Kajtoch erred by failing to support the respondent’s motions to reopen with any applications for relief from removal. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 5-9, Attachment 4 at 2-3. Notably, there is no evidence to establish that Mr. Paz Soldan or Mr. Kajtoch ever responded to the respondent’s allegations.
 
*2 Had we adjudicated the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims which he raised on appeal, we would have remanded this case for further proceedings. The respondent followed the directives of both his prior attorneys, Mr. Paz Soldan and Mr. Kajtoch. Had the respondent not relied on his attorneys’ bad advice, he would have appeared for his hearing or obtained reopening. See Monjaraz-Munoz, 327 F.3d 892 (9th Cir. 2003)(if an alien fails to appear because of his actual and reasonable reliance on counsel’s erroneous advice, it can constitute an exceptional circumstance beyond the control of the alien). Based on the foregoing, we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings on our own motion. Because we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings, we will not address the remaining issues raised by the respondent in this instant motion.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE SOSA
FILE: AXX XX7 661 - NEWARK, NJ
February 7, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sigang Li, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Certification
 
*1 On September 19, 2007, the respondent filed this appeal from an Immigration Judge’s July 2, 2007, decision. It is undisputed that the appeal, which was due on or before August 1, 2007, is untimely. With the Notice of Appeal, the respondent has submitted a motion in which he asserts that his former attorney’s mishandling of his case resulted in the untimeliness of the appeal. We will construe the respondent’s motion as a motion for certification to the Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c). The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
In his motion, the respondent merely asserts that “(h)is counsel reserved the appeal …(h)owever, such appeal was never filed.” He has not, however, provided evidence that he entered into an agreement with his former attorney to filed an appeal. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (specifying the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in the context of motions filed before the Board). Although he has submitted a copy of a complaint against his former attorney to the Disciplinary Board of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, the respondent has not submitted proof that the complaint has been properly filed, and he has not submitted a statement of the alleged misrepresentations. The evidence submitted with the motion simply fails to show that ineffective assistance of counsel caused the untimeliness of the appeal. Accordingly, the motion for certification will be denied, and the untimely appeal will be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, and 1240.15. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court.¿FN1º See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.39 and 1240.14.
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion for certification to the Board is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The untimely appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s appeal has been dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. Either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely must be filed with the Board. However, a motion challenging any other finding or seeking reopening of the proceedings must be filed with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). Strict time and number limits apply to motions to reconsider and motions to reopen before the Immigration Judge and this Board. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and 1003.23(b)(1).

2008 WL 655771 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI ABDO MOHSEN MOHAMED QAYED ALNAHHAM
FILE: AXX XX1 368 - PHOENIX, AZ
February 6, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ronald G. Finch, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John R. Holya
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º -In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 22, 2007, decision denying her “Motion to Reconsider.” The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on June 29, 2004, because she failed to appear for a hearing. On March 31, 2006, she filed a motion to reopen alleging that she did not receive notice of the hearing, which was granted by the Immigration Judge on April 17, 2006. The respondent was given until January 24, 2007, to file an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). When the respondent failed to timely file her application for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge found that her application had been abandoned and granted her voluntary departure in a decision dated February 7, 2007. On February 26, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which the Immigration Judge denied on March 6, 2007. The respondent then filed a “Motion to Reconsider,” which the Immigration Judge denied on March 22, 2007. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her “Motion to Reconsider.” On appeal, the respondent seeks reopening of proceedings because she was the victim of ineffective assistance. Specifically, the respondent contends that she failed to timely file her application for cancellation of removal because her current counsel failed to inform her in writing of her filing deadline.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s “Motion to Reconsider.” To the extent that the respondent’s “Motion to Reconsider”challenged the merits of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen, we find that she failed to establish any material error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Specifically, we find that the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent failed to file a copy of her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act with her motion to reopen, as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Because we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for the above mentioned reason, we need not address the Immigration Judge’s additional finding that the respondent’s motion to reopen was number-barred. To the extent that the respondent sought to submit a copy of her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act with her “Motion to Reconsider,” we construe her motion as a motion to reopen rather than as a motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (providing that a motion to reopen is granted upon a proffer of new, material evidence); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (providing that a motion to reconsider challenges determinations of law and fact). Since this was the respondent’s second motion to reopen filed after her in absentia removal order had been reopened, we find that it was number-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*2 Moreover, to the extent that the respondent seeks to submit new evidence on appeal in the form of a self-reporting letter to the Arizona State Bar from her current counsel alleging that he engaged in ineffective assistance, we are precluded from engaging in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(iv) (providing that, subject to certain exceptions not applicable to the present case, the Board will not engage in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals and that a party asserting that the Board cannot properly resolve an appeal without further fact-finding must file a motion to remand). Furthermore, we find that remand of proceedings based on this new evidence is not warranted. The letter does not concede that he and his assistants failed orally to provide the respondent with notice of the application deadline but merely contends that he should have done so in writing. Moreover, the respondent has not met the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $ 1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
Roger A Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 486927 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOVANNA DE LOS ANGELES NUNEZ A.K.A. SANDRA SANTIAGO
FILE: AXX XX5 211 - BOSTON, MA
January 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lidia Sanchez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Rescission
 
*1 ORDER: PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia in a December 4, 2006, Immigration Judge’s decision. She filed a motion to reopen proceedings on January 3, 2007, alleging that former counsel provided her with ineffective assistance as he misrepresented his handling of the case, and failed to inform her of her hearing date and time. In a February 14, 2007, decision an Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding the respondent had not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent’s appeal of this decision is dismissed.
 
The Board has held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). Matter of Lozada requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639; see also Betouche v. Ashcroft, 357F.3d 147, 149 (1st Cir. 2004) (citing the above-noted requirements).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found the respondent failed to comply with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, supra, as she did not show that she had notified former counsel of her claim, and filed a complaint against him with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. We find no merit to the respondent’s vague and generalized allegations that the Immigration Judge’s decision was “arbitrary and capricious,” an “abuse of discretion,” and “against the law” (Notice of Appeal).
 
*2 The respondent has not expounded on these ambiguous and unsupported assertions, and we find no support for the same.
 
On appeal the respondent submitted a letter which she reportedly filed with the bar authorities complaining about former counsel’s actions. However, we normally do not consider new evidence submitted on appeal as the record we review on appeal is the record before the Immigration Judge. Matter of H-A-, 20 I&N Dec. 529 (BIA 1992); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (the Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.). Moreover, we do not find that a remand in this regard is warranted. There is no evidence that this letter was, in fact, filed with the authorities as alleged. Further, there is no evidence that the respondent’s former counsel has been informed of the allegations against him and afforded an opportunity to respond.¿FN1º See Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 10-12 (1st Cir. 2004) (finding that counsel targeted by an ineffective assistance claim must “be provided an opportunity to respond before filing the motion to reopen”(emphasis added)). Therefore, as the respondent to date has yet to comply with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, a remand in this regard is not appropriate. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992).
 
In sum, as the respondent has not established that she failed to appear at her hearing due to her former representative’s ineffective assistance, the Immigration Judge did not err in denying her motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The circuit where this case arises has declined to find that the mere lodging of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with the bar authorities functionally satisfies the notice requirement. Betouche v. Ashcroft, 357 F.3d at 151 (holding that the filing “simply established that ¿ the petitionerº had notified the Board of Bar Overseers of his complaint, not that either he or the Board had notified ¿prior counselº.”). Moreover, as noted, there is no objective evidence that such a claim has, in fact, been filed.

2008 WL 486949 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONINA SURGANOVA A.K.A. ANTONINA BEAUDOIN
FILE: AXX XX4 919 - CHICAGO, IL
January 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James C. Ten Broeck, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ellen J. Krupp
Assistant Chief Counsel, ICE
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2006, decision dismissing the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native of Lithuania who asserts that she is a citizen of the Ukraine, entered the United States as a visitor in April 2000 as a visitor, and adjusted her status to that of immigrant in November 2003, based upon the approval of a Petition for Alien Relative, filed by the respondent’s husband. After interviewing the respondent’s husband about the bona fides of the relationship, the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) initiated removal proceedings against the respondent, charging her with having engaged in marriage fraud.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), because she procured her adjustment of status by marriage fraud under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. (I.J. at 3-4, 16-17). Section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act states: “Any alien who, by fraud or willfully misrepresenting a material fact, seeks to procure (or has sought to procure or has procured) a visa, other documentation, or entry into the United States or other benefit provided under this Act is excludable.”
 
The central question in determining the good faith of a marriage is whether the bride and groom intended to establish a life together at the time they were married. See, e.g., Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764, 765 (BIA 1988); Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1, 2-3 (BIA 1983). Evidence of such intent may include, but is not limited to, proof that the spouse has been listed on insurance policies, proof of joint property leases, income tax forms, bank accounts, a period of courtship, testimony of mutual experiences and a shared life, and the like. It also may include documentation relating to the degree to which the financial assets and liabilities of the parties were combined; documentation concerning the length of time during which the parties cohabited after the marriage and after the alien obtained permanent residence; and any other evidence supporting the bona fides of the marriage. See Matter of Laureano, supra; Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1216.4(a)(5) and 1216.5 (e)(2).
 
*2 In the present case, the respondent’s husband (a United States citizen petitioner) signed a statement in August 2004 in which he indicated that his marriage with the respondent was not consummated, and that he just wanted to help her become an American citizen (Exh. 2). He also described how the respondent’s daughter and son-in-law paid him, and helped arrange the marriage (Exh. 2). The petitioner now testifies that he signed under duress, and that his statement should not be considered (Tr. at 53, 73-74). We agree with the Immigration Judge that more weight should be given to the testimony of the special agents who interviewed the petitioner than to the testimony of the petitioner (I.J. at 10, 15), and that the petitioner’s statement should be considered.
 
The respondent has argued that in a number of instances, the testimony of the special agents was irrelevant, speculative, lacked foundation, and called for conclusions of law and/or opinions, and that because it was admitted into evidence, it rendered the proceedings unfair and was in violation of the due process clause of the Constitution (R brief at 6-9). We point out that in administrative removal proceedings, the Federal Rules of Evidence are not controlling. See Matter of Grijalva, 19 I&N Dec. 713 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Rather, under the applicable federal regulations, the Immigration Judge may receive into evidence any oral or written statement which is material and relevant to any issue in the case previously made by the alien or any other person during any investigation or examination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1236.1 (2005); see generally 8 C.F.R. § 1242.14. The testimony of the special agents was certainly probative as to the circumstances surrounding the petitioner’s signing his statement. Likewise, the witness testimony permitted by the Immigration Judge was subject to cross examination by the respondent pursuant to section 240(b)(4)(B), and we thus find the reliance on such testimony by the Immigration Judge to be fundamentally fair. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge did not state that he was assigning more weight to the testimony of the special agents because of the disputed testimony that the respondent referred to.
 
Regarding the disputed testimony, the respondent argues that her first counsel of record should have cross-examined the special agents, and that her failure to do so constituted ineffective assistance of counsel (R brief at 8-9). The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge unreasonably held that the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) could not be deviated from (Tr. at 231-34). We note that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by: 1) an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; 2) a showing that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and 3) a showing that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, or if not, why not. See id. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not complied with the first requirement of Lozada, i.e., submitting an affidavit detailing the agreement that she had with her first attorney (Tr. at 231-34). Although several circuits have held that aliens only need to show substantial compliance with Lozada, the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit (the “Seventh Circuit”), under whose jurisdiction the current case arises, is not one of those circuits. See Patel v. Gonzales, 496 F. 3d 829, 831 (7th Cir. 2007) (Court notes that while several Circuits have held that aliens only need to show substantial compliance with the requirements of Lozada, the Seventh Circuit has not expressly decided whether the Board abuses its discretion by requiring strict compliance with Lozada).
 
*3 Furthermore, even if is conceded that the requirements of Lozada were substantially complied with, the actions of counsel that are in question, i.e., allowing the aforementioned testimony of special agents to be admitted into evidence, allowing said agents to testify from memory without reference to their I-213 report, and not using said report as a cross-examination device, do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. Even if it is assumed that counsel should have performed all or some of the aforementioned actions, these actions (or inactions) strike us as tactical decisions, and it is not considered ineffective assistance for counsel to make tactical decisions that are not successful and operate to the client’s detriment. See Away v. Ashcroft, 328 F.3d 336, 343 (7th Cir. 2003) (hindsight applied to unwise tactical decision, to withdrawal of application for asylum, does not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge violated her due process rights by denying her “Motion for Issuance of Subpoenas for Officers’ Materials & to Recall Officers as Witnesses” and a renewed “Motion for Issuance of Subpoenas for Officers’ Materials & to Recall Officers as Witnesses” (R brief at 9-10). In particular, she argues that the Immigration Judge should not have denied these motions summarily, and that the Immigration Judge should have reviewed the officer’s materials “in camera.” We point out that the Immigration Judge did not deny the aforementioned motions summarily (Tr. at 234-39). Rather, he denied them because the respondent did not contact the special agents to see if they would attend the hearing pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1287.4(a)(2)(ii)(B) (Tr. at 235). Furthermore, the respondent’s first attorney testified that after filing a FOIA request, she had been given a compact disc from the Office of Immigration and Customs Enforcement which contained a reprint of the respondent’s file, albeit with much redaction and no notes from investigators (Tr. at 237-38). If the respondent wished to see if there was more information than was presented to her first attorney, she had a duty to try to obtain that information pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1287.4(a)(2)(ii)(B) before asking the Immigration Judge to issue a subpoena.
 
The respondent next argues that the Government did not meet its burden by clear and convincing evidence that the respondent committed marriage fraud under § 212(a)(6)(C)(I) (R at 10-13). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the Government did meet said burden (I.J. at 4). The Immigration Judge specifically found that the testimony of the special agents who interviewed the petitioner should be fully credited over the equivocal testimony of the petitioner and his roommate (I.J. at 10). The Immigration Judge gave reasons for his conclusion, and found that contrary to the testimony of the petitioner and his roommate, the special agents testified clearly and convincingly (I.J. at 15). These factual determinations are not “clearly erroneous.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i)(2005) (requiring this Board to review factual determinations of an Immigration Judge for clear error); Anderson v. City of Bessemer City, North Carolina, 470 U.S. 564, 575 (1985) (emphasizing the great deference accorded to findings regarding the credibility of witnesses under the “clearly erroneous” standard of review).
 
*4 The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should not have given more weight to the agents than to the petitioner regarding whether the petitioner signed his statement on August 6, 2004, under duress by the special agents (R brief at 10-11). We find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to accord greater weight to the special agent’s testimony than to the petitioner and his roommate (I.J. at 10, 15). There was no indication that the special agents’ testimony was in any way unreliable regarding whether the petitioner signed his statement under duress. Although the petitioner testified that the special agents told him that they would take him to jail if he didn’t talk and that he felt forced to give testimony (Tr. at 53, 73-74), one of the special agents testified that the interview was not uncomfortable, and that he wanted to impress upon the petitioner that he wasn’t being hostile (Tr. at 114-15). Furthermore, both special agents did not testify that they told the petitioner he would go to jail if he did not talk, but testified that they informed him of the consequences of marriage fraud and perjury(Tr. at 134, 164). We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the petitioner has not shown that he did not understand what he was signing in August 2004 (I.J. at 14-15). Although a physician expresses concern about the petitioner’s ability to internalize stress in a letter dated April 2006, (Exh. 3-2), the letter does not call into question the petitioner’s ability to understand what he was signing in August 2004 (I.J. at 14). The petitioner submitted an affidavit dated April 2006 (Exh. 3-1), in which he did not deny that his signature and initials appeared on the August 2004 statement, but that he was under great emotional and physical stress, and did not know what he was signing. However, we point out that one of the questions asked on the respondent’s statement (Exh. 2) was whether he understood he was giving a sworn statement and whether he swore that his statements were the truth.
 
The respondent also argues that the special agents testified strictly from memory without referring to their I-213, and that they did not enter the I-213 into evidence (R brief at 12-13). While this is true, it does not strike us as meaningful. Although the respondent argues that the government usually enters the I-213s into evidence (R brief at 12), the respondent has not provided any proof that this is true. The respondent has attempted to describe the agents’ testimony as riddled with so many inconsistencies (R brief at 11-13) that it cannot be believed regarding the crucial question as to whether her husband signed his statement under duress. However, the inconsistencies that the respondent points to (the time that the agents arrived at the apartment, the school that the petitioner’s roommate attended, and whether or not the petitioner and his roommate jointly bought the apartment that they shared together), even if true, are not material. They certainly do not render the agents’ testimony less credible regarding the petitioner’s statement (Exh. 2), and in particular, whether or not there was any duress in his signing it.
 
*5 As to the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge ignored as a matter of law that parties need not necessarily live together to be considered legitimately married (R brief at 13), this is not true. While the Immigration Judge pointed out that the petitioner and the respondent did not live together (I.J. at 19), this was not the main reason for the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent had engaged in marriage fraud. Furthermore, 8 C.F.R. § 1216.5(e)(2) specifically lists “documentation concerning the length of time during which the parties cohabited after the marriage and after the alien obtained permanent residence” as an element to consider when considering whether an alien entered into a qualifying marriage in good faith.
 
In conclusion, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision dismissing the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings, and finding the respondent removable for procuring her admission to the United States through adjustment of status by marriage fraud under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486949 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 486900 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED ABDUL SATER
FILE: AXX XX7 725 - DETROIT, MI
January 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL and MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rosario S. Shoudy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice; Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Asylum, withholding of removal, Convention Against Torture; reopening
 
This case is presently before us on the respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, order denying his motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal that was entered on August 2, 2006. Along with his appeal brief, the respondent moved to reopen this matter based upon changed country conditions and also based on a then-pending visa petition, filed by his United States citizen wife after their July 2003 marriage. The visa petition has since been approved, and a visa is immediately available to the respondent. See August 13, 2007, Supplement to Motion to Reopen.¿FN1º However, because the motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status was not filed within 90 days of the entry of the original in absentia order of removal, it is untimely and will be denied.
 
In his brief in support of his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal, the respondent argues that reopening is appropriate because ineffective assistance of prior counsel caused him to miss his scheduled hearing on August 2, 2006. Although, as the Immigration Judge indicated, the respondent was present when a hearing notice was provided to his prior counsel, he does not speak English and attests that he was never given a copy of the notice. See Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, Order; Respondent’s January 11, 2007, Brief at Exh. 3. Indeed, there appears to be some question about how (or if) the respondent was notified of the August 2, 2006, hearing, in that the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) indicated below that an evidentiary hearing should be held to determine if the former attorney had, as he claimed, “called the respondent” to notify him of the August 2, 2006, hearing. See DHS’s September 18, 2006, Answer to Motion to Reopen. The respondent’s wife provided an affidavit in support of the motion to reopen below that states that the couple was unaware of the date of the August 2, 2006, hearing and that she repeatedly called the attorney’s office to find out the hearing date but was never told about it. See Respondent’s September 14, 2006, Motion to Reopen. She supports this affidavit with telephone records that purportedly indicate telephone calls to former counsel. Id. Additionally, the respondent’s wife indicated in the same affidavit that the couple did not receive notice of a prior rescheduled hearing from former counsel, and instead only learned of the new hearing date by calling the Immigration Court. Id.
 
*2 Because the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for setting forth ineffective assistance of counsel claims, has set forth facts that, if found by the Immigration Judge, would tend to indicate ineffective assistance of counsel, and has shown prejudice from that ineffective assistance in the form of his apparent eligibility for adjustment of status, we will sustain the respondent’s appeal. Further fact- finding is necessary to determine if the respondent has set forth an actionable claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. Because we will sustain the appeal and remand this record, we need not address the respondent’s arguments regarding changed country conditions in Lebanon.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status is denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
Board Member Patricia A. Cole concurs with the majority’s decision to deny respondent’s motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status. However, the evidence of record does not support a conclusion that the respondent did not receive proper notice to appear at his removal hearing. I would not grant the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal and dissent from this portion of the majority’s decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The motion to reopen, which has since been supplemented and updated, is supported by evidence of the bona fides of the marriage, including the fact that as of the last filing in this matter, the couple was expecting the birth of their first child.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUSSAN NG LIN XIE
FILES: AXX XX6 997 - SEATTLE, WA
January 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS MOTION
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro Se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 On October 19, 2007, the respondents¿FN1º timely filed a motion to reconsider our September 27, 2007, decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The respondents contend that we erred in finding them removable on the charge under section 237(a)(1)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B), for remaining in the United States longer than permitted. The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider shall specify the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1). We have reviewed the contentions raised in the respondents’ motion, but find that our previous decision was correct. On motion, the respondents raise various arguments, including their eligibility for adjustment of status as “arriving aliens” pursuant to 71 Fed. Reg. 27585 (2006), deficiencies in the Immigration Judge’s decision, as well as their attorneys’ ineffective assistance of counsel. These are all arguments which we either considered at the time of our prior decision in September 2007, or that could have been argued in the context of the direct appeal. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 58 (BIA 2006) (indicating that “¿aº motion to reconsider based on a legal argument that could have been raised earlier in the proceedings will be denied.”)
 
In our September 2007 decision, we agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondents had not submitted any evidence to show that they extended or changed their status in a timely manner, prior to expiration of their nonimmigrant visas on June 30, 2005. We pointed out that a November 14, 2005, I-797C (Notice of Action) stated that the fee for the Form I-129 application (petition for a nonimmigrant worker) had been received, and that the filing date had been adjusted to reflect a “received date” of July 27, 2005. The respondents have now submitted a copy of a I-797C dated July 5, 2005, showing that the male respondent’s petition for a nonimmigrant worker was received on June 27, 2005. We point out that this new evidence would not show an error of fact or law in our prior decision based on the previous factual record. See id at 57. Furthermore, we point out that the later I-797C, i.e., the one dated November 14, 2005, shows that the male respondent’s petition was actually received on July 27, 2005. Both of these I-797Cs have the same receipt number, and the one dated November 14, 2005, states that the filing date was adjusted to July 27, 2005, to reflect receipt of payment. Inasmuch as the November 14, 2005, I-797C shows an adjusted filing date of July 27, 2005 (after the visa expiration date of June 30, 2005), we find that the submission of the I-797C showing “receipt” of petition on June 27, 2005, is not enough for us to reconsider our decision. There was not an error of fact in the September 2007 decision.
 
*2 The respondents also submitted a copy of a I-797C, dated August 21, 2006, showing that a Form I-526 application (immigrant petition by alien entrepreneur) was received on August 14, 2006. We note that this was information that the respondents could have provided at the time of the September 2007 decision. Moreover, even if this information had been submitted prior to said decision, the visa petition would not have been filed prior to the expiration of the respondents’ nonimmigrant visas on June 30, 2005. Again, there was not an error of fact in the September 2007 decision.
 
In conclusion, we cannot find based upon the record which was before us when we last considered these proceedings that an error of fact or law exists in the September 2007 decision. Accordingly, we decline to reconsider our decision, and the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondents are husband and wife.
