2008 WL 486878 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DAMIAN MCAUTHUR MCKENZIE
FILE: AXX XX3 198 - NEW YORK, NY
January 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Douglas Rosenthal, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Moira Arsenault
Appellate Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination of removal proceedings
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 31, 2007, decision ordering him removed from the United States to Jamaica as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony and a controlled substance violation, and moves for a remand to the Immigration Judge, a stay of removal, and termination of proceedings. The motions will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
  
I. FACTS AND PROCEDURAL HISTORY
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Jamaica. On or about July 11, 1985, the respondent was admitted to the United States in New York, New York, as a lawful permanent resident. On October 3, 2006, the respondent pled guilty and was convicted of criminal possession of a controlled substance (cocaine) with intent to sell in violation of New York Penal Law § 220.16(1). On January 31, 2007, the respondent was charged as deportable pursuant to sections 237(a)(2)(B)(i) and 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. After being placed in removal proceedings, the respondent moved the New York State court to vacate his conviction (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge issued his oral decision on July 31, 2007, ordering the respondent removed and noting that the respondent’s motion in state court was merely a collateral attack (I.J. at 2-3). The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent was not eligible for cancellation of removal, adjustment of status, asylum, or withholding of removal because he had been convicted of an aggravated felony (I.J. at 2). The respondent did not request deferral of removal pursuant to the Convention Against Torture (I.J. at 2). The respondent then moved the State court to accept a delayed appeal pursuant to section 460.30 of the New York Criminal Procedure Law, apparently based on a claim that his waiver of appeal rights was due to ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Brief Exh.; Respondent’s Motion Exh.). On August 6, 2007, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. On August 23, 2007, the respondent filed with the Board a motion to remand proceedings and requested a stay of removal, arguing that his conviction was not final by nature of his request for a delayed appeal. On September 14, 2007, the state appellate court granted the respondent’s motion for a late appeal, causing the respondent’s conviction now to be pending on direct appeal as of right (Respondent’s Brief Exh.; Respondent’s Motion Exh.). See N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 460.30. Consequently, on September 18, 2007, the respondent filed with the Board a motion to terminate proceedings because of the alleged lack of finality of his predicate conviction, and filed an appeal brief containing the same argument. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) did not respond to the motion or file a responsive brief on appeal, but at our request, both parties presented oral argument on December 6, 2007. At oral argument, DHS argued that a conviction on direct appeal is a valid factual predicate for a charge of deportability and, in the alternative, that the respondent’s appeal should be deemed a collateral attack.¿FN1º
  
II. ISSUE
 
*2 The issue on appeal is whether the definition of the term “conviction” in section 101 (a)(48)(A) of the Act, enacted as part of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (“IIRIRA”), in pertinent part defined as “a formal judgment of guilt of the alien entered by a court,” supersedes prior Board and judicial interpretations that a “conviction” under the Act requires waiver or exhaustion of direct appeal rights to serve as a valid factual predicate for a charge of deportability.
  
III. BACKGROUND
 
Until the 1996 enactment of IIRIRA no definition of the term “conviction” existed in the immigration laws. For several decades, the United States Courts of Appeals and the Board had determined that a criminal judgment against an alien did not qualify as a “conviction” for immigration purposes unless the judgment was “final” in the sense that the alien’s right to direct appellate review had been exhausted or waived. See, e.g., White v. INS, 17 F.3d 475,479 (1st Cir. 1994); Martinez-Montoya v. INS, 904 F.2d 1018, 1025 (5th Cir. 1990); Morales-Alvarado v. INS, 655 F.2d 172, 175 (9th Cir. 1981); Marino v. INS, 537 F.2d 686, 691-92 (2d Cir. 1976); Aguilera-Enriquez v. INS, 516 F.2d 565, 570 (6th Cir. 1975); Will v. INS, 447 F.2d 529, 532-33 (7th Cir. 1971); Matter of Thomas, 21 I&N Dec. 20, 21 n.1 (BIA 1995); Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894, 895-96 (BIA 1994); Matter of Ozkok, 19 I&N Dec. 546, 551-52 n.7 (BIA 1988). The origin of this “finality” requirement is unclear¿FN2º and may have derived, at least in part, from a terse 1955 opinion of the United States Supreme Court in which it held, in its entirety, that: “On the record here we are unable to say that the conviction has attained such finality as to support an Order of deportation within the contemplation of § 241 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.A. § 1251. The judgment is reversed.” Pino v. Landon, 349 U.S. 901 (1955) (per curiam), rev’g Pino v. Nicolls, 215 F.2d 237 (1st Cir. 1954) (holding that an alien was convicted under the Act under a Massachusetts procedure permitting the alien’s sentence to be revoked and the case placed “on file” after a previous finding of guilt). The issue before us in this case is whether this judicially-created requirement of “finality” through exhaustion or waiver of direct appeal rights survives the 1996 enactment of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, which set forth, for the first time in the Act, the precise meaning of a “conviction” for immigration purposes. For the following reasons, we conclude that the respondent’s conviction remains a valid predicate for the charge of deportability because a “conviction,”as defined in the Act, does not require the exhaustion or waiver of direct appeal rights.
  
DISCUSSION
 
*3 To determine whether the respondent has been “convicted,” the touchstone of our analysis is the plain language of the Act. Lamie v. United States Trustee, 540 U.S. 526, 534 (2004) (citing Hughes Aircraft Co. v. Jacobson, 525 U.S. 432, 438 (1999)). It is presumed that Congress “says in a statute what it means and means in a statute what it says there.” Hartford Underwriters Ins. Co. v. Union Planters Bank, N.A., 530 U.S. 1, 6 (2000) (quoting Connecticut Nat’l Bank v. Germain, 503 U.S. 249, 254 (1992)); see also Chevron U.S.A., Inc. v. Natural Resources Defense Council, Inc., 467 U.S. 837, 842-43 (1994). We are mindful that the words of a statute cannot meaningfully be understood in isolation; “¿tºhe meaning-or ambiguity-of certain words or phrases may only become evident when placed in context.” Zuni Public Schools Dist. No. 89 v. Dep’t of Educ., 127 S.Ct. 1534, 1545-46 (2007) (citing and quoting FDA v. Brown & Williamson Tobacco Corp., 529 U.S. 120, 132-33 (2000), and Brown v. Gardner, 513 U.S. 115, 118 (1994) ( “¿a ºmbiguity is a creature not ¿justº of definitional possibilities but ¿alsoº of statutory context.”)). However, once the unambiguous meaning of the statutory language has been determined, it must be given effect except in those rare circumstances where strict adherence to the text would lead to an absurd or “bizarre” result that is “‘demonstrably at odds with the intentions of its drafters.”’ Demarest v. Manspeaker, 498 U.S. 184, 190-91 (1991) (quoting Griffin v. Oceanic Contractors, Inc., 458 U.S. 564, 571 (1982)).¿FN3º
 
For purposes of the Act, Congress has defined the term “conviction” as follows:
§ 1101. Definitions
(a) As used in this Act—
 
....
 
(48)(A) The term ‘conviction’ means, with respect to an alien, a formal judgment of guilt of the alien entered by a court or, if adjudication of guilt has been withheld, where—
(i) a judge or jury has found the alien guilty or the alien has entered a plea of guilty or nolo contendere or has admitted sufficient facts to warrant a finding of guilt, and
 
(ii) the judge has ordered some form of punishment, penalty, or restraint on the alien’s liberty to be imposed
 
 
 
Section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A) (codified pursuant to section 322(a)(1) of IIRIRA, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-628 (effective April 1, 1997)). Thus, under current law a “conviction” exists for immigration purposes if either: (1) “a formal judgment of guilt of the alien ¿has beenº entered by a court;” or (2) a judicial finding of guilt has been “withheld,” but the alien’s guilt has nonetheless been established by other means, resulting in the imposition of a penal sanction, “or restraint on the alien’s liberty.” Id.
 
*4 Section 101(a)(48)(A) does not state that a conviction must be made “final” by exhaustion or waiver of appeal rights, nor does the term “conviction” implicitly denote the existence of such a requirement. As the Supreme Court explained in Deal v. United States, 508 U.S. 129 (1993), “the word ‘conviction’ can mean either the finding of guilt or the entry of a final judgment on that finding,” among other things. Id. at 131. In Deal, for purposes of the recidivist enhancement provision codified at 18 U.S.C. § 924(e)(1), the Court held that “conviction” meant the former. Id. at 132-35. We will not read an exhaustion or waiver requirement into section 101(a)(48)(A) absent a basis for such a reading in the plain language of the statute. See Braswell v. United States, 224 F.2d 706, 710 (10th Cir. 1955) (refusing to infer a “finality” requirement for predicate convictions under former section 2(e) of the Federal Firearms Act where the statutory language contained no such requirement). Indeed, the silence of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act with regard to any finality requirement has persuaded those United States Courts of Appeals that have analyzed the issue that the finality requirement has been superseded. See United States v. Saenz-Gomez, 472 F.3d 791,794 (10th Cir. 2007) (holding that the literal language of section 101(a)(48)(A) was controlling as to the meaning of “conviction,” and declining to infer the continued existence of the finality requirement where the plain language of the statute was clear and did not lead to an irrational result);¿FN4º Montenegro v. Ashcroft, 355 F.3d 1035, 1037 (7th Cir. 2004) (per curiam) (holding that “IIRIRA eliminated the finality requirement for a conviction”); Moosa v. INS, 171 F.3d 994, 1009 (5th Cir. 1999) (dicta that “¿tºhere is no indication that the finality requirement imposed … prior to 1996¿º survives the new definition of ‘conviction’ found in IIRIRA § 322(a)”); Puello v. Bureau of Citizenship and Immigration Servs., 511 F.3d 324, 2007 WL 4440916, at *8 (2d Cir. 2007) (dicta that “IIRIRA … eliminate¿dº the requirement that all direct appeals be exhausted or waived before a conviction is considered final”); see also Griffiths v. INS, 243 F.3d 45, 50 (1st Cir. 2001) (reserving the question but citing Moosa with approval). But see United States v. Garcia-Echaverria, 374 F.3d 440, 445 (6th Cir. 2004) (adopting in dicta, without analysis, pre-IIRIRA law requiring the finality of convictions).
 
*5 Although Congress is presumed to be aware of an administrative or judicial interpretation of a statute and to adopt that preexisting interpretation when it reenacts a statute without change, Lorillard v. Pons, 434 U.S. 575, 580 (1978), we deem the presumption to have been rebutted in the present context because IIRIRA embodied an unequivocal break with the prior judicial and administrative requirement of finality of convictions in immigration proceedings in that it included, for the first time, a statutory definition of “conviction” under the Act. Section 101 (a)(48)(A) defines two types of convictions, those arising from “a formal judgment of guilt,” and those in which the “adjudication of guilt has been withheld.” With respect to the latter, deferred adjudication, Congress adopted almost verbatim two prongs of the test enumerated in Matter of Ozkok, supra, at 551, but notably omitted the third prong of that test, which considered a deferred judgment to be a conviction only when “a judgment or adjudication of guilt may be entered if the person violates the terms of his probation or fails to comply with the requirements of the court’s order, without availability of further proceedings regarding the person’s guilt or innocence of the original charge.” See Matter of Ozkok, supra, at 552. Notably, the third prong in Matter of Ozkok, supra, was supported in a footnote by a reference to the finality rule at issue here. Id. at 552 n.7. However, the language used in IIRIRA to define a conviction arising from “a formal judgment of guilt” does not enjoy a similar pedigree. Therefore, we will ascribe no specific intent to Congress with regard to the issue presented, beyond Congress’s intent to enact into law the definition of a conviction arising from “a formal judgment of guilt” now contained in section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act.
 
DHS contends that the legislative history of section 101(a)(48)(A) also lends support to the conclusion that exhaustion or waiver of appeal rights is not required to constitute a conviction under the Act. We disagree, and decline to rely on legislative history absent ambiguity in the plain language of the statute. The Congressional conference report explains the nature and purpose of section 101(a)(48)(A) as bringing more adjudicative processes within the definition of “conviction” and states that section 101(a)(48)(A) “deliberately broadens the scope of the definition of ‘conviction’ beyond that adopted by the Board of Immigration Appeals in Matter of Ozkok, 19 I&N Dec. 546 (BIA 1988).” H.R. Rep. No. 104-828, at 224 (1996). See Matter of Punu, 22 I&N Dec. 224, 227 (BIA 1998) (discussing the legislative history underlying the definition of “conviction”). The conference report also states that “¿tºhis new provision, by removing the third prong of Ozkok, clarifies Congressional intent that even in cases where adjudication is ‘deferred,’ the original finding or confession of guilt is sufficient to establish a ‘conviction’ for purposes of the immigration laws.” Id. (emphasis added). DHS argues that this latter statement suggests, by using the language “even in,” that likewise in cases other than those where adjudication of guilt is deferred, Congress intended a “formal judgment of guilt” to constitute an “original finding or confession of guilt … sufficient to establish a ‘conviction.”’ Nonetheless, we are unable to find that this single, opaque and unexplained statement constitutes an expression of Congressional intent to dismantle the finality doctrine.¿FN5º
 
*6 DHS also contends that Congress’s inclusion of a requirement for finality of convictions or judgments in certain other provisions of the Act provides support for DHS’s interpretation of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act. See Clay v. United States, 537 U.S. 522, 528-29 (2003) (reiterating the maxim that “¿wºhen Congress includes particular language in one section of a statute but omits it in another section of the same Act … it is generally presumed that Congress acts intentionally and purposely in the disparate inclusion or exclusion”) (citations and internal quotation marks omitted). However, we have examined these provisions and observe that their unique historical and statutory contexts make comparisons inapt and too far removed to provide meaningful direction.¿FN6º Thus, we find that these provisions provide no clear guidance regarding the proper construction of the term “conviction” enacted in IIRIRA. Instead, we rely on the plain language of section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Act, reinforced by the decisions of the courts of appeals that have reached this issue, to conclude that “finality,” in the sense of the necessity for waiver or exhaustion of a direct appeal, was eliminated by IIRIRA and is no longer required.¿FN7º
  
IV. CONCLUSION
 
We acknowledge that, even after the 1996 enactment of the controlling definition of “conviction,” many of our unpublished orders have repeated the pre-IIRIRA rule requiring that a conviction has attained “finality.” In part, such statements to date have reflected the practice of the former Immigration and Naturalization Service, continued under its successor agency DHS, of even after IIRIRA instituting proceedings only after an alien’s direct appeal rights were exhausted, with rare exceptions. At oral argument, DHS counsel indicated that this was, and is, done for prudential reasons. We point out that nothing in this opinion precludes DHS from continuing its policy of exercising prosecutorial discretion by charging the vast majority of convicted aliens only after their criminal convictions have attained a certain degree of finality. DHS has exclusive authority over when to bring charges. We hold only that, for the foregoing reasons, prior judicial and administrative interpretations that required a conviction under the Act to be “final” through exhaustion or waiver of direct appeal rights were superseded when IIRIRA expressly defined a “conviction” under the Act in section 101(a)(48)(A), 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A). Therefore, we conclude that the respondent’s conviction, pending on direct appeal, is a valid predicate for the charge of deportability.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motions are denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

Here, the New York procedure is one that has restored the respondent’s direct appeal. DHS has made no showing that the grounds available to the respondent are limited to those grounds available only on collateral attack or are narrower than those available to defendants filing a timely direct appeal in the first instance. There is nothing absurd about treating the text of section 101 (a)(48)(A) as negating a requirement that a direct appeal be pending, nor does the respondent so contend. Compare Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), where we noted that one court of appeals found the text of section 101(a)(43)(A) so clear as to compel a finding that even a conviction reversed on the merits remained a “conviction” for immigration purposes. However, we declined to adopt this interpretation, deeming it to lead to bizarre or absurd consequences not in keeping with the intent of Congress. Id. at 624n.2. See also Lewis v. United States, 445 U.S. 55, 61n.2 (1980) (rejecting the “extreme” argument that a reversed conviction remained a conviction for purposes of the statute punishing possession of a firearm by a convicted felon, but noting that the statute would apply where, as here, possession occurred while a conviction was pending on appeal).

Saenz-Gomez involved the penalty provisions of section 276(b) and found that an alien who was removed nine days following his conviction on drug trafficking charges had been removed “subsequent to a conviction for commission of an aggravated felony.”

In Matter of Punu, supra, we addressed the deferred adjudication part of the newly-enacted definition of “conviction,” holding that an alien had been convicted for immigration purposes where he was subject to a Texas procedure that permitted a trial court to defer an adjudication of his criminal charge upon the entry of a guilty plea and to place him on rehabilitative probation with an eye to the eventual dismissal of the charges. The alien in Matter of Punu, supra at 227-28, argued that such a deferred adjudication was not a conviction for immigration purposes because, among other things, Texas law allowed for the possibility of further appellate review on the question of guilt or innocence in the event that he violated probation. However, we held that the possibility of such further review was “not determinative” in view of the plain language of section 101(a)(48)(A) and its legislative history, which reflected an unequivocal legislative intent to treat such deferred adjudications as convictions. However, in Matter of Punu, supra, we had no occasion to decide the question at issue here: whether a “formal judgment of guilt entered by a court” under the first part of the statutory definition of “conviction” qualifies as a valid factual predicate for a charge of deportability where the alien has not waived or exhausted his direct appeal rights. 2008 WL 486887 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS HANNA DAWOUD
FILE: AXX XX9 680 - BOSTON, MA
January 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Saher Joseph Macarius, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 8, 2006, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The motion was denied as untimely. On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel, and that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Lebanon who entered the United States on April 17, 2002, as a nonimmigrant visitor for business with authorization to remain in this country for a temporary period not to exceed June 16, 2002. He remained in the United States beyond this date without authorization, and was served with a Notice to Appear on January 9, 2003. The respondent appeared pro se at a hearing before an Immigration Judge on November 12, 2003, and explained that he married a United States citizen on June 2, 2003, and that a Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) was filed on his behalf. The Immigration Judge advised the respondent to submit evidence in support of his claim. The respondent subsequently retained counsel, who appeared on his behalf at a March 24, 2004, hearing. At that time, the respondent requested a continuance to await the approval of the Form I-130, and, in the alternative, sought voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge denied the request for a continuance because the respondent had failed to present evidence that a Form I-130 had been filed on his behalf, and denied voluntary departure in the exercise of discretion. The respondent filed a motion to reconsider on May 6, 2004, which was denied as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In an August 22, 2006, motion to reopen, the respondent argued that his removal proceedings should be reopened because his former attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel. In particular, he alleged that his prior counsel failed to file documents requested by the Immigration Judge, discouraged him from appealing the Immigration Judge’s decision, failed to timely file his motion to reconsider, and did not advise him of his right to file a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely. A motion to reopen before an Immigration Judge must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order of removal. See 8 C.F.R, § 1003.23(b). See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). In the instant case, the respondent’s motion would have been due on or before June 24, 2004. As noted above, the respondent did not file his motion to reopen until August 22, 2006.
 
*2 For an alien raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the untimeliness of the motion may possibly be excused under the doctrine of equitable tolling if the alien establishes that he exercised due diligence. See Cai Xing Chen v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 151, 154 n. 3 (1st Cir. 2005) (noting the issue remains unresolved). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not exercised due diligence in this case. The respondent was aware of the evidence the Immigration Judge requested from him in November 2003, and that his former attorney failed to submit this evidence in support of his claim. He knew that the Immigration Judge denied his request for a continuance on March 24, 2004, and that his prior counsel did not appeal this decision. In addition, the respondent knew that the Immigration Judge denied his untimely motion to reconsider on May 6, 2004. Consequently, the conduct of his prior attorney has been known by him since mid-2004. Yet the respondent did not file the motion until nearly 2 1/2 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision. We find that these facts do not support a finding that the respondent has acted with due diligence.
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he did not know that his former counsel was ineffective until he obtained the services of his present attorney. This argument fails. While the respondent may not have characterized his former counsel’s actions as “ineffective” as that term is used by legal practitioners in immigration proceedings, he was well aware of his counsel’s failure to submit evidence to the Immigration Court, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 24, 2004, decision, or file a timely motion to reconsider. Thus, he was aware of the deficiencies in his former attorney’s conduct but did not timely pursue any action regarding his conduct. Moreover, the impetus for the filing of the motion to reopen appears to be less related to any actions or conduct by the respondent’s former counsel and more related to the fact that the Form I-130 filed on his behalf was approved in September 2005. Because we find that the respondent did not act with due diligence, we agree with the Immigration Judge that his motion is not subject to equitable tolling and was properly denied as untimely. Thus, we need not address the issues of whether the respondent complied with the 1requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), or whether his counsel was ineffective and he was prejudiced thereby.
 
During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record for consideration of his application for adjustment of status. A motion to remand filed with the Board must, among other things, state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and must be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). In support of his motion, the respondent submitted a duplicate copy of a Notice of Approval of Relative Immigrant Visa Petition from the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services indicating that the Form I-130 filed on his behalf by his wife was approved on September 23, 2005. While the respondent also provided a copy of his application for adjustment of status (Form I-485), his marriage certificate, and evidence of the bona fides of his marriage, he has failed to explain why this evidence was “not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Thus, we cannot conclude that these proceedings should be remanded on the basis of “circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the bearing.” Id. Further, we note that granting the respondent’s motion would undermine the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his request for a continuance pending the adjudication of the Form I-130 filed on his behalf, and would reward him for his delay in filing evidence previously requested by the Immigration Judge. In order to avoid this unjust result, we deny the respondent’s motion in the exercise of discretion. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion is denied.
 
*3 Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XIAN SHUN SHEN
FILE: AXX XX8 045 - HARLINGEN, TX
January 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lauren H. Mason, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mike Ochoa
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the February 16, 2007, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s request for reopening of the August 30, 2006, in absentia order. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).¿FN1º
 
The respondent, a citizen and native of China, was arrested near McAllen, Texas, on June 11, 2006. Exh. 1 at 3. She admitted to being present in the United States without having been admitted or paroled after inspection. Exh. 2 at 2. The next day, she was given several documents, including a Notice to Appear at a hearing before an Immigration Judge in Harlingen, Texas, on August 30, 2006. Id.; Exh. 1 at 1. Through a Mandarin interpreter, an immigration officer also advised the respondent that she should find a good lawyer to handle her case. Exh. B at 2. The immigration officer also gave the respondent a list of free or low-cost legal service providers in the area of Harlingen-Brownsville, Texas. Id. Instead of contacting these providers, the respondent traveled to Los Angeles (where she apparently had a few friends and acquaintances). Id On or about June 23, 2006, she went to the offices of a two-attorney law firm in Los Angeles. Exh. E. While there, she spoke to an “American looking man” through a “Korean speaking lady” who served as an interpreter (the respondent’s best language is Korean). Id. The interpreter apparently first told the respondent that the documents she had indicated that she should go to court in Texas. Id. The respondent apparently asked if someone from the firm could go with her to court (although she has never indicated what the reply to this question was). Id.; Exh. B at 2. The respondent was then apparently told that she should not go to the Texas immigration court and that if she did, she would be arrested and sent back to China. Id. On appeal, the respondent contends that “¿aºlthough it is not clear that either attorney actually gave ¿meº the wrong advice, their employee did and they should be held responsible.” Respondent’s Brief at 7.
 
Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him or her and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). It is questionable as to whether an ineffective assistance of counsel claim can be made based on representations made by nonattorney interpreters of a law firm. Regardless, the Lozada requirements have not been met in this case: the respondent has failed to identify the nonattorney employee in question, she has not shown that the “American looking man” (or any attorney affiliated with this law firm) actually agreed to represent her, and she has failed to establish that an agreement was entered into with respect to actions to be taken on her behalf.
 
*2 In short, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s decision to rely upon the alleged instructions to not attend the immigration hearing in Texas on August 30, 2006, is not an “exceptional circumstance” within the meaning of section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. I.J. at 2. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s request for a waiver of filing fees is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1), 1003.8.

2008 WL 486872 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE SERGIO PEDRAZA-GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX8 139 - PHOENIX, AZ
January 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John M. Pope, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda I. Spencer-Walters
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 In a decision dated December 13, 2006, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal to Mexico, should he fail to avail himself of voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge noted that any claims for relief were deemed abandoned, since no applications had been filed by the due date of August 8, 2005. The respondent has, through counsel, filed an appeal and a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal but has not addressed the motion. Since the appeal will be sustained, the motion to remand is moot.
 
The basis for the appeal and motion, in its essence, is that the respondent was prejudiced by ineffective assistance, not of counsel, but of a paralegal employed by counsel. It is maintained that the paralegal, having been assigned to do so, failed to file the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal in a timely manner.
 
A review of the record reveals that current counsel has represented the respondent as least since May 2004. Counsel, or his partner, was present at every hearing held in the respondent’s case. At the hearing held on April 6, 2005, counsel’s partner, Mr. Stender, appeared with the respondent and was informed by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 25) that the respondent’s application for cancellation was due no later than August 8, 2005. The respondent indicated his understanding of his duty to cooperate with counsel in the preparation of his application. The application was not timely filed.
 
In an affidavit, counsel has stated that the paralegal who was responsible for filing the application was subsequently terminated after making “many mistakes,” including failing to meet deadlines.
 
The counsel of record, not an employee with no direct representational relationship with the respondent, was ultimately responsible for the submission of any and all applications in a timely manner. Under the circumstances, the Immigration Judge’s action in this matter was not unreasonable. Rules of procedure for Immigration Courts exist, in part, to ensure the timely submission of evidence and applications in proceedings. Neither the alien nor the administrative process is well-served by the piecemeal or untimely submission of pertinent documents in a particular case, especially applications for relief.
 
Ineffective assistance cases are generally governed by the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. Immigration and Naturalization Service, 204 F.3d 1241 (9th Cir. 2000). Insufficient compliance with those guidelines has been demonstrated in this case. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, has stated that the Lozada requirements “are not sacrosanct,” and that a case for ineffective assistance of counsel can be made, even without strict Lozada compliance, where the record shows a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” See, e.g., Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993 (9th Cir. 2006); Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582 (9th Cir. 2006); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 In our view, this case presents such a situation. The respondent was prevented a reasonable opportunity to present his case, since he was not even able to have his application for cancellation of removal considered, due to counsel’s failure to ensure that it was timely filed. Accordingly, we deem it appropriate to sustain the appeal and remand the case for the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the respondent’s cancellation application and render a decision, either granting or denying that relief.
 
In remanding this case, we intimate no opinion as to the respondent’s eligibility or worthiness for the discretionary relief he seeks.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the Immigration Judge’s decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 486852 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEXANDER SHAROGORODSKY
FILE: AXX XX3 068 - MIAMI, FL
January 24, 2008
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
 
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda Osberg-Braun, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility; remand
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2007, decision denying his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), and ordering him removed from the United States as charged. In addition, the respondent has filed a motion to remand in which he claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney and in which he requests an opportunity to apply for protection as a refugee and also to seek cancellation of removal and a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion to remand will be denied.
 
The respondent was born in 1957 in Kharkov, Ukraine, when Ukraine was still part of the former Union of Soviet Socialist Republics (“Soviet Union”). The respondent, who is Jewish, entered the United States as a refugee in 1980 after fleeing from religious persecution in the former Soviet Union. The respondent became a lawful permanent resident of the United States in 1983. In late 1990 the respondent knowingly entered into a criminal conspiracy to obstruct commerce by means of robbery in violation of 18 U.S.C. § 1951 (1988), and in June 1993 he was convicted of that offense pursuant to a guilty plea in the United States District Court for the Eastern District of New York. In early 2003 the respondent left the United States and traveled abroad, returning to this country on January 17, 2003, at which time he presented himself for inspection before an immigration officer and requested entry to the United States as a returning lawful permanent resident. On the basis of the aforementioned conviction, however, the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) declined the respondent’s request to reenter the United States and placed him in the present removal proceedings, in which he is charged as an arriving alien who is inadmissible by virtue of having been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. Sections 101(a)(13)(C)(v) and 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1101(a)(13)(C)(v) and 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). The respondent does not dispute the charge of inadmissibility on appeal.
 
During his proceedings before the Immigration Judge the respondent applied for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act pursuant to INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001), but the Immigration Judge denied the application in the exercise of discretion. On appeal, the respondent does not explicitly challenge the Immigration Judge’s discretionary decision. Instead, he argues that the denial of his application was attributable to the ineffective assistance of his former attorney, who failed to provide the Immigration Judge with sufficient evidence of his favorable equities. In his appellate brief and his motion to remand, moreover, the respondent argues that former counsel was ineffective by accepting the Immigration Court’s designation of Ukraine as his country of removal and by failing to seek various other forms of relief on the respondent’s behalf, such as asylum, withholding of removal, cancellation of removal, and a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
*2 At the outset, we find no basis for disturbing the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver. The respondent’s 1993 conviction for conspiracy to obstruct commerce by robbery is a compelling adverse discretionary factor that can be overcome, if at all, only upon a showing of unusual and outstanding countervailing equities. The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had failed to demonstrate such equities, and the respondent does not argue to the contrary on appeal. Instead, he claims that prior counsel was ineffective by failing to obtain more evidence in support of the respondent’s application and by failing to elicit more persuasive testimony of hardship from the respondent’s wife and child.
 
To justify plenary rehearing on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must demonstrate that he suffered substantial prejudice arising from counsel’s deficient performance; that is, he must demonstrate a reasonable probability that the outcome of the proceedings would have been different but for prior counsel’s errors. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005). In this instance the respondent has done nothing more than to assert that prior counsel failed to obtain strong documentary and testimonial evidence in support of his application for section 212(c) relief. He makes no proffer of additional evidence relevant to section 212(c) relief on appeal, and does not explain with particularity what evidence he would have submitted to meet his burden had counsel been more effective. Under the circumstances, the respondent has not demonstrated that prior counsel’s ineffectiveness deprived him of a meaningful opportunity to present his section 212(c) request to the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, we find no due process violation.
 
The respondent’s other claims arising from the alleged ineffectiveness of former counsel suffer from a similar defect. For example, the respondent claims that counsel should have sought cancellation of removal and a section 212(h) waiver on his behalf, but he has failed to offer any evidence to suggest the existence of any previously undisclosed equitable considerations that would have caused the Immigration Judge to resolve those discretionary applications differently from the aforementioned application for section 212(c) relief. We note in this regard that the discretionary calculus followed in cases arising under former section 212(c) of the Act is not materially different from that which would have been applied in connection with any request for cancellation of removal or a section 212(h) waiver. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7, 11 (BIA 1998) (holding that the general standards and considerations for the exercise of discretion outlined in Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978), were “equally appropriate in considering requests for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.”); Matter of Mendez, 21 I&N Dec. 296, 300 (BIA 1996) 2 (providing that the discretionary analysis set forth in Matter of Marin, supra, is an appropriate guide in section 212(h) cases). Absent a proffer of evidence reflecting the existence of equitable considerations that were not previously presented to the Immigration Judge because of prior counsel’s ineffectiveness, we find no substantial prejudice arising from prior counsel’s “failure” to request cancellation of removal and a section 212(h) waiver on the respondent’s behalf.
 
*3 The respondent also argues that former counsel should have requested refugee protection on his behalf, presumably under the asylum and withholding of removal provisions of sections 208 and 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1158 and 1231(b)(3).¿FN1º Again, we find no substantial prejudice arising from prior counsel’s failure to seek these forms of relief because the respondent’s 1993 conviction for conspiracy to commit robbery is prima facie a “particularly serious crime” barring both forms of relief, Matter of Carballe, 19 I&N Dec. 357 (BIA 1986), and the respondent has proffered no evidence to suggest that there are specific mitigating facts that would militate against such a designation in his case.¿FN2º See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16(d)(2) and 1240.8(d) (indicating that where a ground for mandatory denial of an application for relief may apply, the applicant bears the burden of proving by a preponderance of the evidence that the ground is inapplicable).
 
Finally, the respondent was not prejudiced by prior counsel’s designation of Ukraine as his country of removal. Specifically, had the respondent declined to designate a country of removal, the Attorney General could not have directed his removal to the country of citizenship, i.e., the Soviet Union, because that country no longer exists, and therefore the proper designation would have been to “the country from which the alien was admitted to the United States.” See section 241 (b)(2)(E)(i) of the Act. We recognize that Ukraine was not an independent “country” at the time of the respondent’s admission to the United States in 1980; however, the word ‘country’ is construed in this context to include not just the political entity of which the alien may have been a subject at the time of his entrance into the United States, but also the geographical area or territory from which the alien came. See, e.g., Ademi v. INS, 31 F.3d 517, 521 n.7 (7th Cir. 1994); Bajalieh v. Beechie, 309 F.2d 386, 389 & n.5 (9th Cir. 1962); United States ex rel. Tom We Shung v. Murff, 176 F. Supp. 253 (S.D.N.Y. 1959), aff’d, 274 NF.2d 667(2d Cir. 1960); Seif v. Nagle, 14F.2d 416, 417(9th Cir. 1926), cert.denied, 273 U.S. 737 (1926); United States ex rel. Mensevich v. Tod, 264 U.S. 134, 136 (1924). While the respondent argues that he cannot be removed to Ukraine without evidence that Ukraine will accept him, the Supreme Court has found that prior consent of the country designated for removal is not a precondition for entry of a removal order under section 241 (b)(2)(E)(i) of the Act. Jama v. Immigration & Customs Enforcement, 543 U.S. 335, 345 n.5, 346-48 (2005).
 
*4 In conclusion, the respondent has failed to identify any reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his application for section 212(c) relief in the exercise of discretion, and he has also failed to demonstrate that he has been prejudiced by the allegedly ineffective assistance of his former attorney, so as to warrant a remand for plenary rehearing. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent does not request nonrefoulement under Article 3 of the Convention Against Torture, nor does he allege that it is more likely than not that he will tortured by or with the consent or acquiescence of the present Ukrainian Government.

2

We note that an offense need not be an “aggravated felony” to qualify as a “particularly serious crime” within the meaning of sections 208(b)(2)(A)(ii) and 241 (b)(3)(B)(ii) of the Act. Matter of N-A-M-, 24 I&N Dec. 336, 338-341 (BIA 2007). Moreover, factors that are subsequent and unrelated to the commission of the offense, such as cooperation with law enforcement authorities, bear only on sentencing and are irrelevant to a determination of whether a crime is “particularly serious.” Matter of N-A-M-, supra, at 343 (citing Matter of Y-L-, A-G-, & R-S-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 270, 273-74, 277-78 (A.G. 2002)).

2008 WL 486858 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HERBERT ALEXANDER FLORES-TORRES A.K.A. HERBERT TORRES A.K.A. HERBERT ALEXANDER TORRES A.K.A. HERBERT TORRES-FLORES A.K.A. GEORGE FLORES A.K.A. DEADEYE FLORES
FILE: AXX XX4 242 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
January 24, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Holly S. Cooper, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patricia Spaletta
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before this Board on July 24, 2007. On October 22, 2007, 90 days after our decision, the respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, filed a motion to reopen with this Board. Due to a clerical error, this Board mistakenly rejected the respondent’s motion for failure to include a fee or a fee waiver request. On October 31, 2007, we acknowledged receipt of the respondent’s motion filed on October 22, 2007. The applicable regulations provide that motions to reopen be filed no later than 90 days after the date on which the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings sought to be reopened. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Therefore, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen is timely filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent makes an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against his prior counsel and claims that he derived United States citizenship from his mother. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes reopening. The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
We will first review the procedural history of this case. The respondent is a native and citizen of El Salvador and has been a lawful permanent resident since 1990. In August 2004, the DHS charged him with removability under section 237(a)(2)(B)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(I), as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation. An Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), on December 9, 2004 (Exh. 2). No appeal was taken from that decision. On June 2, 2005, the respondent was convicted of possession of a firearm by a felon with two convictions in violation of section 12021(a) of the California Penal Code. As a result, he was again placed in removal proceedings and charged with having been convicted of an aggravated felony, to wit, a firearms offense. See section 101(a)(43)(E) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(E). On April 20, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to terminate removal proceedings based on his claim of derivative citizenship under former section 321 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432, and ordered the respondent removed from the United States as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. On July 24, 2007, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. We held that the respondent did not derive United States citizenship from his mother, who naturalized in 1995 when the respondent was 17 years old. Under former section 321 (a)(3) of the Act, a child born outside the United States of alien parents becomes a citizen of the United States upon “the naturalization of the mother if the child was born out of wedlock and the paternity of the child has not been established by legitimation,” provided that both the mother’s naturalization and the child’s admission to lawful permanent residence occurred prior to the child’s 18th birthday. See former section 321 (a)(3) of the Act.
 
*2 The respondent claims that he is not considered legitimated under Salvadoran law. In Matter of Moraga, 23 I&N Dec. 195, 198 (BIA 2001), which involved a visa petition filed on behalf of a child beneficiary who was born out of wedlock, the Board observed that El Salvador eliminated the legal distinction between legitimate and illegitimate children on December 16, 1983, thereby placing children born out of wedlock in the same legal position as those born in wedlock. We concluded that El Salvador’s removal of that legal distinction meant that “all natural children are deemed to be the legitimate or legitimated offspring of their natural father from the time that country’s law changed,” as long as they were under 18 years of age on the date of enactment. Matter of Moraga, supra, at 199.
 
However, in Matter of Rowe, 23 I&N Dec. 962, 967 (BIA 2006), this Board dealt with a similar statute under Guyanese law. The alien in Matter of Rowe, supra, was born out of wedlock in Guyana and claimed that he derived United States citizenship through his mother under former section 321(a)(3) of the Act. The Immigration Judge rejected Rowe’s citizenship claim, concluding that the Guyanese Removal of Discrimination Act of 1983 had eliminated all legal distinctions between legitimate and illegitimate children, thereby “legitimating” Rowe by operation of law and precluding him from deriving United States citizenship solely through his mother. In arriving at that conclusion, the Immigration Judge relied on this Board’s decision in Matter of Goorahoo, 20 I&N Dec. 782 (BIA 1994), where we held that children born out of wedlock in Guyana after the effective date of the Removal of Discrimination Act were to be considered the “legitimate” children of their biological fathers for preference allocation purposes.
 
The Board in Matter of Rowe reversed the Immigration Judge’s decision and held that, for purposes of the immigration laws of the United States, Guyana’s statute forbidding discrimination against “illegitimate” children was not sufficient to effect the automatic “legitimation” of children born out of wedlock. Matter of Rowe, supra, at 966-67. Instead, we noted that under Guyana’s Legitimacy Ordinance the only means by which a child born out of wedlock could be truly “legitimated” was by the marriage of his or her parents. Id. at 967. Accordingly, we overruled Matter of Goorahoo, supra, and held, subject to future changes in Guyanese law, that a Guyanese child born out of wedlock could only be legitimated within the meaning of the immigration laws—whether for preference allocation or derivative citizenship purposes—by the marriage of his biological parents. Id. And since Rowe’s parents had never married, we concluded that he had derived United States citizenship under former section 321(a)(3) of the Act upon the naturalization of his mother. Id.
 
*3 In the instant case, the record is insufficient for us to determine whether the changes brought about by the modifications in the law in 1983 in El Salvador regarding children born out of wedlock not only served to eliminate discrimination between legitimate and illegitimate children but also automatically legitimated children born out of wedlock. We note that in Matter of Moroga, supra, at 198, the Board referred to article 279 of the Civil Code of El Salvador, which provided a means of legitimation for children born out of wedlock. However, it is unclear if this law remains in effect. This Board has previously determined that the law of a foreign country is a question of fact. See Matter of Annang, 14 I&N Dec. 502, 503 (BIA 1973); see also Matter of Kumah, 19 I&N Dec. 290, 292 (BIA 1985); Matter of Khatoon, 19 I&N Dec. 153, 154 (BIA 1984) (relying upon an expert memorandum of foreign law from the Library of Congress to establish the validity of a Moslem adoption under the Hindu Code in India). In light of the Board’s inability to make findings of fact in the course of deciding appeals, the obvious significance of the respondent’s citizenship claim, and lack of clarity regarding Salvadoran law concerning legitimation, we find it appropriate to sua sponte reopen proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i); 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent should present evidence, including citations and copies of Salvadoran law, regarding whether the elimination of the legal distinction between legitimate and illegitimate children automatically legitimated children born out of wedlock or merely served as an anti-discrimination measure. The respondent should also submit evidence regarding whether or not there are means by which legitimation may be accomplished in El Salvador. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge, through the submission of evidence from the parties, shall determine whether article 279 of the Civil Code of El Salvador, which provided a means of legitimation for children born out of wedlock, remains in effect. See Matter of Moraga, supra, at 198. Upon remand, the DHS shall also be given the opportunity to present additional evidence and address any evidence submitted by the respondent. Therefore, we will not address the other arguments at this time.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s July 24, 2007, decision is hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*4 Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486858 (BIA)

    1  Case Reopened by IN RE: ANDRES JOSE CERON-ECHEVERRY, BIA, June 24, 2009

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDRES JOSE CERON-ECHEVERRY
FILE: AXX XX7 897 - ELOY, AZ
January 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 27, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision that, among other things, denied his motion to terminate the removal proceedings based on his claim of derivative citizenship and ordered him removed to his native Colombia. In that decision, we concluded that the respondent failed to derive United States citizenship from his mother under former section 321(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432, upon her naturalization on May 16, 1996, because the respondent had failed to demonstrate that his mother had actual uncontested custody of him at the time of her naturalization. On November 26, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The motion will be denied.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent initially argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his claim of derivative citizenship and deprived him of due process during his removal proceedings by hampering his ability to present his claim of derivative citizenship. However, in that regard, his motion is more in the nature of a motion to reconsider than a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (providing that a motion to reconsider is a challenge to errors of fact or law in the Board’s decision); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 2001) (stating that motions to reopen and motions to reconsider are separate and distinct motions with different requirements). The motion is untimely because it was not filed within 30 days of our August 27, 2007, order, and the respondent has offered no explanation for his untimely filing to justify the equitable tolling of that deadline. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); cf. Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993, 999-1000 (9th Cir. 2007) (noting that an alien seeking equitable tolling of the period for filing a motion to reopen must show that he exercised due diligence in vindicating his rights). Moreover, upon review of the respondent’s arguments and all relevant portions of the record, we conclude that the respondent has failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances that would warrant sua sponte reconsideration of our August 27, 2007, order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent also argues that reopening of his proceedings is warranted based on new evidence that was previously unavailable to him that demonstrates his eligibility for derivative citizenship. While such evidence, if present, would warrant reopening, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c)(2), the respondent has presented no such evidence here. In that regard, most of the evidence accompanying the respondent’s motion to reopen was either already presented to the Immigration Court (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. G-K, M; see also Record of Proceedings Exh. 3, Tab D; Exh. 5, Tabs 1-7, Exh. 11, Tabs 1-7) or substantially identical in form and substance to evidence that was submitted before the Immigration Court (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. B, D; Record of Proceedings, Exh. 9). Moreover, the respondent has not shown that the “new” affidavits prepared by himself, his mother, and his godfather after our August 27, 2007, decision could not have been discovered or presented at his May 17, 2007, hearing before the Immigration Judge (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. A, C, F). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent also has not shown that the evidence regarding his arrival and departure dates from Colombia between 1993 and 2000 could not have been discovered or presented at his May 17, 2007, removal proceedings (Motion to Reopen, Exh. E). See id.
 
*2 Finally, we reject the respondent’s contention that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. As the respondent himself acknowledges, he has failed to comply with any of the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988), for claims alleging ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Reopen, Affidavit of the Respondent). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (BIA 2000) (recognizing that the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and that under ordinary circumstances the Board does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra). Moreover, we do not find plain evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel in the record that would excuse the respondent’s noncompliance with Matter of Lozada, supra. Cf. id. at 526 (compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, not required where the record was undisputed that the alien’s lawyer failed, without any reason, to file an application for suspension of deportation in spite of having told the alien that he did file it); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir.) (compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, not required where it was plain from the administrative record that counsel was being represented by an attorney whom she had never met and who had no understanding of her case), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000). An alien who raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must demonstrate that counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence, and must establish that he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance. See Grigoryan v. Keisler, 507 F.3d 1206, 1210 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent has failed to make that showing. Specifically, given the lack of any evidence that the respondent’s mother was awarded legal custody of him from the Colombian government following her divorce from the respondent’s father, the respondent cannot show that he sustained prejudice from his former counsel’s alleged failure to inquire whether his mother was awarded foreign legal custody of him and to advise him that a foreign court’s grant of legal custody may have changed the outcome of these proceedings. We also find no support in the record for the respondent’s general allegations that counsel failed to “keep herself abreast of relevant and significant applications and proper understanding of ¿iºmmigration ¿s ºtatutes, (Motion to Reopen at 22), that counsel “was very untruthful, unprofessional and had a serious problem with procrastinating (Motion at 23),” or that counsel hid her record of prior Florida Bar suspensions from the respondent and the Immigration Court (Motion to 23).¿FN1º
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that reopening of these proceedings is warranted. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the record shows that the respondent’s counsel did, in fact, admit to her prior suspensions at the respondent’s immigration proceedings and that the Immigration Judge rejected the Department of Homeland Security’s assertion that she had improperly failed to indicate that she was “subject” to an order of suspension by an administrative agency in her Form EOIR-28 (Notice of Entry of Appearance or Representative Before the Immigration Court) in light of those previous suspensions (Tr. at 32-34).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EYTAN IZYAGUYEV A.K.A. EYTAN ISIAGUEV
FILE: AXX XX7 059 - ATLANTA, GA
January 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William H. Oltarsh, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decisions dated January 3, 2007, and January 9, 2007, denying the respondent’s motions to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on December 6, 2006. The appeals will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was scheduled to appear for a hearing on December 6, 2006, in Atlanta, Georgia. On December 4, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to change venue. The respondent failed to appear for the hearing on December 6, 2006, and was ordered removed in absentia, The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen, in which his attorney of record, William H. Oltarsh, Esquire, stated that his own spouse was suffering from terminal cancer, and that he failed to advise the respondent that he had to appear for his hearing on December 6, 2006. With the motion to reopen, the respondent submitted a sworn statement requesting that venue be changed to New York, New York. However, the respondent failed to provide any explanation for his failure to appear at the hearing on December 6, 2006. The Immigration Judge found that the attorney’s filings admit actual notice of the hearing, and failure to notify the respondent is not a basis for reopening absent a Lozada affidavit. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). On appeal, Mr. Oltarsh has submitted a sworn statement in which he indicates that he believes he informed the respondent that he was required to appear for a master calendar hearing in Atlanta, Georgia, but Mr. Oltarsh cannot remember the exact details due to his wife’s terminal illness.
 
The Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). Furthermore, we have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). The respondent has not asserted that he failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel, nor has he submitted an affidavit in support of such a claim. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had proper notice of his hearing, and an attorney’s failure to notify a client is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada. As the respondent failed to submit an affidavit in support of his motion to reopen pursuant to Lozada, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that reopening is not warranted in this matter.
 
*2 The respondent also appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 9, 2007, denying the respondent’s second motion to reopen filed on January 4, 2007. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the exceptional circumstances must relate to the respondent, such that the illness of counsel’s spouse does not constitute an exceptional circumstance. Furthermore, the respondent’s second motion to reopen is number-barred pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeals are dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486840 (BIA)
