2008 WL 486823 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARELLA LUZ VILLANUEVA
FILE: AXX XX5 999 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
January 18, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jon Wu, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Admission as a returning resident
 
In a decision dated August 4, 2006, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and denied her application for admission as a returning resident. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s finding that she abandoned her lawful permanent residence. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of the Phillippines who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident with her family in 1986 when she was 13 years of age (Tr. at 16-17). After spending 1 month in the United States, the respondent and her family returned to the Philippines to allow the respondent to continue her education there (Tr. at 17-18). The respondent visited the United States for an additional 3 months in 1987 before returning to the Phillippines to continue her schooling (Tr. at 18-19). The respondent continued her studies and ultimately graduated from college in the Phillippines in 1995 when she was 22 years of age (Tr. at 22-23, 25). At that time, the respondent wanted to return to the United States, but her parents told her she needed to remain in the Phillippines to take care of her maternal grandmother, who had become terminally ill and was expected to live only until 1996 (Tr. at 22-23, 26-27). Accordingly, the respondent remained in the Phillippines. She obtained a full-time job and helped take care of her grandmother when she was not working (Tr. at 55).
 
The respondent’s maternal grandmother actually lived longer than 1 year and passed away on April 17, 1998 (Tr. at 28; Exh. 4, Tab E). At that point, the respondent did not immediately return to the United States; rather, in accordance with Filipino cultural norms, she remained in mourning for 1 year in the Phillippines following her maternal grandmother’s death (Tr. at 28-29). The respondent continued to work full-time during that period, however (Tr. at 55). After her year of mourning passed, she again decided to return to the United States (Tr. at 29-31). Nevertheless, she did not try to travel to the United States until she obtained her family’s approval to do so sometime in 2000.
 
In July 2000, the respondent attempted to board an airplane for the United States and learned, allegedly for the first time, that she could not reenter the United States without a reentry permit (Tr. at 32). The respondent attempted to obtain more information about obtaining a reentry permit from an Immigration and Naturalization Service (“INS”) office in the Phillippines (Tr. at 32-33). She was told she would need certain documents to obtain a reentry permit and to contact an attorney for further assistance (Tr. at 33). The respondent chose to obtain these documents before even contacting an attorney (Tr. at 34-36). Sometime in August 2001, she retained an attorney named Dr. Solomon Burgess to help her obtain a reentry permit (Tr. at 34). According to the respondent, under Burgess’s direction she executed an application for a reentry permit (Form I- 131) (Tr. at 37-38). The record reflects that the respondent signed her Form 1-131 on August 8, 2001; however, for some reason, it was not submitted until January 24, 2002 (Exh. 2A).¿FN1º At some point, the application was evidently returned, and the respondent later refiled the Form I- 131 on September 26, 2002 (Exh. 2A; Exh. 3, Tab D). However, the agency did not ultimately issue the respondent a reentry permit (Form I-327) until January 29, 2004 (Exh. 7).¿FN2º The DHS sent the Form 1-327 to the respondent’s paternal grandmother’s home in Seattle sometime in February 2004 (Exh. 4, Tab B). The respondent’s paternal grandmother promptly forwarded that document to the respondent (Exh. 4, Tab B; Exh. 6).
 
*2 On April 13, 2004, the respondent returned to the United States with her passport, green card, and the Form I-327. Her inspection was deferred, she was interviewed, and a sworn statement was taken (Exh. 2B). She stated in the interview that she had always believed her green card allowed her to reside permanently in the United States, that she was not aware until 2000 that she would need a Form I- 327 to reenter the United States, and that she intended to reside permanently in the United States (Exh. 2B). The respondent was placed in removal proceedings and charged with removability under section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), for not being in possession of a valid unexpired immigrant visa, reentry permit, or other valid entry document at the port of entry.¿FN3º
 
Section 211 (b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1181 (b), allows a waiver of the document requirements in section 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) of the Act for returning resident immigrants. However, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent was not entitled to admission because she had abandoned her lawful permanent resident status. First, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent “most likely” lost her permanent resident status while she was still a minor when her family returned to the Phillippines without any intent to return permanently to the United States for at least 6 to 10 years while she was attending school in the Phillippines (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge concluded in the alternative that, even if the respondent’s parents’ likely abandonment of status was not imputed to her, she herself abandoned her lawful permanent resident status when she remained in the Phillippines after she finished college in 1995 but made no effort to come to the United States until 2000 (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge was unpersuaded that the respondent was a “necessary part of the family plan for her grandmother’s care” following her graduation from college in light of the evidence showing that the respondent worked full-time as a professional while both she and her mother were taking care of her grandmother (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge concluded that any cultural norms that allegedly prevented the respondent from acting independently of her family did not negate her responsibility to demonstrate her true intent to reside permanently in the United States (I.J. at 5). The Immigration Judge was particularly troubled by the fact that the respondent did not make any effort to contact the American embassy when her reason for delaying her return to the United States changed, and that she had made no effort to change or update her immigration file until 2000 (I.J. at 5-6). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for admission and ordered her removal from the United States (I.J. at 6).
 
*3 On appeal, the respondent argues that her study abroad did not establish an abandonment of her permanent residency status. Likewise, she asserts that her decision to stay in the Phillippines to care for her maternal grandmother for a fixed period of time did not constitute an abandonment of her lawful permanent residence. The respondent further contends that her delay in seeking a reentry permit was due to Burgess’s ineffective assistance of counsel, and that she should not be deemed to have abandoned her permanent residency status based on his acts.
 
Where, as here, the respondent has a colorable claim of lawful permanent resident status, the burden is on the DHS to demonstrate by clear, unequivocal, and convincing evidence that she abandoned that status. See Matter of Kane, 15 I&N Dec. 258, 264 (BIA 1975). To qualify as a returning resident immigrant, an alien must be an immigrant lawfully admitted for permanent residence who has retained that status from the time she acquired it and must be returning to an unrelinquished lawful permanent residence from a temporary visit abroad. See section 101 (a)(27)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 211.1(b); Singh v. Reno, 113 F.3d 1512, 1514 (9th Cir. 1997); Matter of Huang, 19 I&N Dec. 749, 753 (BIA 1988). A permanent resident returns from a temporary visit abroad only when (1) her visit is for a period relatively short, fixed by some early event, or (2) her visit will terminate upon the occurrence of an event having a reasonable possibility of occurring within a relatively short period of time. See Khodhagholian v. Ashcroft, 335 F.3d 1003, 1007 (9th Cir. 2003) (internal citation and quotation omitted). Where the length of the visit is contingent upon the occurrence of an event and is not fixed in time and the event does not occur within a relatively short period of time, the visit will only be considered a “temporary visit abroad” if the alien has a continuous, uninterrupted intention to return to the United States during the entirety of her visit. Id. (emphasis added). The relevant intent is not the desire to return ultimately to the United States; rather, it is the intent to retum to the United States within a relatively short period of relevant foreign events. Singh v. Reno, supra, at 1514. A trip abroad may also be extended beyond a relatively short period so long as the alien intends to return to the United States as soon as possible thereafter. Id.
 
We will assume for the sake of argument that the respondent’s parents did not abandon their lawful permanent status while she was a minor such that their abandonment of status could be imputed to her. Cf. Matter ofZamora, 17 I&N Dec. 395 (BIA 1980) (holding that the voluntary and intended abandonment of a lawful permanent resident status by the mother was imputed to a child who was an unemancipated minor at the time his mother abandoned her lawful permanent resident status).¿FN4º Even so, we concur with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent abandoned her lawful permanent resident status and, thus, is ineligible for admission as a returning resident. We agree with the respondent that her trip abroad to complete her studies, as well as her immediate extension of that trip to care for her ailing grandmother and then mourn for her grandmother in accordance with Fillipino tradition, did not constitute an abandonment of status. Singh v. Reno, supra, at 1514. Nonetheless, we share the Immigration Judge’s concerns that the respondent did not even attempt to contact the American embassy when her reasons for being in the Phillippines changed in 1995 from her original plan of acquiring an education to her new plan of caring for and then mourning her grandmother, despite her having spent virtually the entire period of her lawful permanent residence outside the United States.¿FN5º
 
*4 Moreover, the respondent’s subsequent actions negated that she had a continuous, uninterrupted intent to return to the United States. Specifically, we deem significant that the respondent waited more than 1 fullyear after the end of her mourning period before even attempting to return to the United States. Then, even after being expressly notified that she would not be able to return to the United States without a reentry permit, the respondent waited another full year before contacting an attorney who could assist her with her return. The respondent’s decision to gather documents during that period that might help her in returning to the country does not excuse her delay in contacting an attorney or in otherwise failing to evidence an intent to return to the United States between 2000 and 2001. Burgess’s alleged ineffective assistance is nondispositive in this case because by the time that alleged conduct transpired the respondent had already abandoned her lawful resident status.
 
For the foregoing reasons, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s application for admission as a returning lawful permanent resident. We also reject the respondent’s remaining arguments. The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge’s decision deprived her of due process because, prior to all relevant events, she had never received notice that she could abandon her lawful permanent resident status. However, the law regarding abandonment of lawful permanent residence was already well-settled when the respondent left the United States. See, e.g., Santos v. 1NS, 421 F.2d 1303 (9th Cir. 1970); Matter of Kane, supra. Moreover, the respondent has not shown prejudice because she has not shown that her awareness of that law would have influenced her actions. Finally, we lack jurisdiction to entertain the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge’s decision violates her equal-protection rights. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 339634 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO TIMOTEO REYES-REYES A.K.A. ARTURO T. REYES A.K.A. ARTURO TIMOTEO A.K.A. AUTHORO R. REYES A.K.A. ARTURO LINARES A.K.A. RUBEN MONTICELLO
FILE: AXX XX6 020 - CHICAGO, IL
January 16, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Isuf Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brendan Curran
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 10, 2007. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2007). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2007).
 
The respondent was convicted of Retail Theft on July 24, 2006. He was sentenced to probation. Because he violated probation, he was resentenced to two years in jail. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable for having an aggravated felony relating to theft.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that removability was not proved. The Immigration and Nationality Act provides that “Any alien … in and admitted to the United States shall, upon the order of the Attorney General, be removed if the alien is within one or more of the following classes of deportable aliens …. (iii) Aggravated Felony.--Any alien who is convicted of an aggravated felony at any time after admission is deportable.” See 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (1996). The term “aggravated felony” includes a theft offense (including receipt of stolen property) or burglary offense for which the term of imprisonment ¿isº at least one year.” See 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G) (1997).
 
Within the record is an information charging the respondent with Retail Theft and a certified statement of conviction and disposition. That document indicates that the respondent pled guilty to the charge and was sentenced to probation on July 24, 2006. It also indicates that the respondent was found guilty of violating probation and sentenced to two years on April 24, 2007. We find clear and convincing evidence of removability. See Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276, 286 (1966); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3) (1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a) (2007). Cf. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15, 1240.10 (2007).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also disputes that he received a two-year jail sentence for the theft offense. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the imposition of a sentence of imprisonment following revocation of probation is a modification of the original sentence. See U.S. v. Hidalgo-Marcias, 300 F.3d 281, 284-85 (2nd Cir. 2002) (conviction for attempted burglary became an aggravated felony after probation was revoked and sentenced to an additional one-year sentence); U.S. v. Jimenez, 258 F.3d 1120, 1125-26 (9th Cir. 2001) (conviction for infliction of corporal injury on a spouse became an aggravated felony after probation was revoked and sentenced to two years).
 
Also on appeal, the respondent claims he has received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has failed to fulfil the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501 (7th Cir. 2001).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board Of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM INNOCENTE
FILE: AXX XX3 884 - OAKDALE, LA
January 16, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario DeMarco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Craig A. Harlow
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s July 26, 2007, decision concluding that the respondent, a native and citizen of Italy, is removable and ineligible for cancellation of removal as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. Sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 240A(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), 1229b(a)(3)(2000).
 
During his removal hearing, the respondent admitted through counsel that he was removable as charged. (Tr. 2). Having conceded that he is removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, it necessarily follows that he cannot demonstrate the absence of an aggravated felony conviction in connection with his cancellation of removal application. Applicable regulations, which have the force and effect of law as to the Immigration Judges and this Board, provide in pertinent part that “the immigration judge may determine that removability as charged has been established by the admissions of the respondent.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (2007). Accordingly, the distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity and in the respondent’s presence during the removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission, and he will not now be heard to challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from that concession. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”). Furthermore, the binding effect of an attorney’s concession of removability applies with equal force in a proceeding predicated on criminal convictions. Matter of Pichardo, 21 I&N Dec. 330, 333 n.2 (BIA 1996). Finally, the respondent has not argued or proven that his prior attorney’s concession of removability constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N.Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 In conclusion, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States on the basis of his attorney’s concession of removability. Because the respondent is removable and ineligible for cancellation of removal on the basis of his concession, we do not address his appellate arguments regarding the validity of the aggravated felony charge or the Immigration Judge decision pretermitting his cancellation of removal application.
 
The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 339641 (BIA)

2008 WL 243738 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PETULA CELIA MILLER A.K.A. SANDY MILLER
FILE: AXX XX7 519 - HARTFORD, CT
January 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea R. Reid, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; reopening; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Jamaica. The respondent appeals an Immigration Judge’s March 9, 2007, decision, denying her request to reopen proceedings for the purpose of pursuing a new application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge with the following additions. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent alleges that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to reopen, because she presented prima facie evidence that she is currently eligible for adjustment of status based on her marriage to a lawful permanent resident. However, because the respondent is an arriving alien, the Immigration Judge has no jurisdiction to consider her application for adjustment of status. This is because amendments to the regulations provide that United States Citizenship and Immigration Service (USCIS) has exclusive jurisdiction over all adjustment of status applications for arriving aliens with one narrow exception. This exception permits an Immigration Judge to adjudicate an adjustment of status application for any alien who:
1. properly filed an application for adjustment of status with USCIS while the alien was in the United States;
 
2. departed from and returned to the United States pursuant to the terms of a grant of advance parole to pursue the previously filed application for adjustment of status;
 
3. the application for adjustment of status was denied by USCIS; and
 
4. DHS placed the arriving alien in removal proceedings either upon the arriving alien’s return to the United States pursuant to the grant of advance parole or after USCIS denied the application.
 
See 71 Fed. Reg. 27591-27592 (May 12, 2006) (revising 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2). Here, there is no indication that the respondent falls within this exception. For example, there is no indication that she departed from and returned to the United States pursuant to the terms of a grant of advance parole to pursue a previously filed application. Hence, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. We find that reopening is not warranted here, as the Immigration Judge does not have jurisdiction to adjudicate the respondent’s application for adjustment of status in this case. Any application for adjustment must be pursued with USCIS independent of these proceedings.
 
*2 The respondent additionally requested reopening based on alleged ineffective assistance provided by her former counsel(s). However, we can not find sufficient evidence of any ineffective assistance. Rather, it is clear in the correspondence (attached to the respondent’s motion), which constituted a response to the respondent’s filed grievance, that she was told that she would have to file an appeal by January 11, 2007. There is no indication that the respondent was prevented from doing so by her former counsel(s). The former counsel(s) warned the respondent of the due date for filing the appeal and clarified that their firm had not been retained to represent the respondent on appeal. The respondent’s former counsel(s) are not required to agree to represent the respondent on appeal, and the Representation Agreement (also attached to the respondent’s motion) between the respondent and her former counsel(s) specifically indicates that the agreement does not cover appeals. One of the respondent’s former attorneys indicates, in a letter, that the respondent was told that their law firm would not handle her appeal because they did not see good prospects for success with the appeal. That does not mean that the respondent was not free to pursue the appeal without counsel or to seek other legal representation. Rather, the letter sent by that firm indicates that the respondent could pursue the appeal, and it specifically indicates that the respondent would have to discuss whether that particular firm would be willing to handle such appeal with them. Thus, it is clear from the letter that the respondent could pursue the appeal whether or not that particular firm was retained to represent the respondent in filing such appeal. In sum, the record does not sufficiently establish that the respondent’s failure to file an appeal was due to ineffective assistance provided by her former counsel(s), nor is there sufficient evidence that the assistance provided by former counsel(s) resulted in any due process violation or the deprivation of a fair hearing. See Romero v. U.S. INS, 399 F.3d 109 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal of the denial of her motion to reopen, based on a new application for adjustment of status and based on ineffective assistance of counsel, is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 243738 (BIA)

2008 WL 243746 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARGARITA ARCEGA
FILE: AXX XX5 809 - BALTIMORE, MD
January 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rachel S. Ullman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dimple R. Shah
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On May 21, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under sections 240A(b)(l)(B) and (2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§§ 1229b(b)(l)(B) and (2)(A)(iii), based on a determination that the respondent was not a person of good moral character during the requisite periods. On July 20, 2005, the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a brief.¿FN1º
 
The respondent did not comply with the final order of removal. Rather, she continued to work without authorization as a nanny. The respondent filed the instant motion to reopen on July 5, 2007, after the Department of Homeland Security sought to enforce the order of removal. The motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent contends that the time limit for filing her motion should be equitably tolled because she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also contends that the Board should reopen proceedings sua sponte, or under section 240 (c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229 (c)(7)(C)(iv). In support of her motion, the respondent has submitted a large number of documents, as well as two supplemental motions with evidence. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes reopening.
 
The respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (lst Cir. 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 745-46 (4th Cir. 2006). The respondent contends that former counsel provided ineffective assistance at the hearing, on appeal, and failed to provide her notice of the Board’s decision. In support of her claims, the respondent’s current employer asserts that the respondent’s file did not include letters from former counsel regarding the pending appeal or the Board’s decision. The respondent also contends that she made payments after the Board dismissed the appeal, which supports her assertion that she did not have notice of the Board’s decision.
 
a review of the record does not indicate that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance at either the hearing or on appeal. The fact that the respondent did not obtain relief, the denial of which was based on her own misrepresentations, does not mean that her former counsel was ineffective. Further, we agree with the Disciplinary Board of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania that the respondent’s former counsel did not provide ineffective assistance during her appeal because her former counsel contacted her during the pendency of her appeal, but she did not respond or pay her fees in accordance with his letter dated April 6, 2005. Moreover, after the Board issued its decision, the respondent’s former counsel mailed a copy of the decision to the respondent at her last known address. See Motion to Reopen, Exh. C, Letter dated July 24, 2005. The respondent’s assertions regarding lack of notice are given minimal weight, given that the Immigration Judge previously found that she misrepresented facts to obtain an immigration benefit, that she concealed information, and was not forthcoming (I.J. Dec. dated 5/21/04 at 3-4). The fact that the respondent did not provide copies of her former attorney’s letters to her current employer does not establish lack of notice. Similarly, making several payments of $100 to former counsel after the Board’s decision was issued does not necessarily establish that the respondent did not have notice of our decision. Further, the respondent failed to demonstrate that she exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002)(equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf). The Immigration Judge issued his decision in 2004, we summarily dismissed the appeal in 2005, and the respondent was provided with notice of our decision soon thereafter, but did not hire new counsel until after the DHS contacted her in 2007. Even if the respondent did not have notice of our decision, her assertions of due diligence are not plausible given the passage of time from the Immigration Judge’s decision to the filing of the instant motion, the Immigration Judge’s prior findings of misrepresentation, former counsel’s assertions that he attempted to contact the respondent during the pendency of the appeal, but the respondent did not respond to his telephone calls, and the follow-up letter from the respondent’s former counsel mailed during the pendency of the appeal. The respondent’s motion to reopen under section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) is also denied because it is based on the same allegations and facts previously presented to, and rejected by, the Immigration Judge. We do not view section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) as an opportunity for an illegal alien to relitigate the same issues previously presented and rejected. Further, the respondent’s current motion under section 240(C)(7)(C)(iv) is untimely, and a review of the record, including the motion to reopen, and supplements, do not provide sufficient evidence of extraordinary circumstances or extreme hardship to her children so as to justify an exception to the time-bar. We decline, moreover, to reopen sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999) (fundamental change in law may warrant sua sponte reopening notwithstanding otherwise applicable time and number limitations on motions); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board retains limited discretionary power under the regulations to reopen or reconsider only in exceptional circumstances).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIAN GUO CHEN
FILE: AXX XX6 741 - NEW YORK, NY
January 10, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
June Zhou, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James D. Paoli
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on December 29, 2005, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s March 30, 2005, denial of a motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order issued on February 25, 2005.
 
On November 30, 2007, the respondent, a native and citizen of China, filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order. Under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. The respondent acknowledges that his motion is untimely, but seeks equitable tolling of the 180-day deadline because his motion is premised on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney, Mr. Xu.
 
The respondent’s motion will be denied for two reasons. First, it is untimely filed. The respondent seeks equitable tolling of the 180-day motions deadline based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has held that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Aliens relying on equitable tolling must establish that (1) their constitutional right to due process was violated by counsel’s conduct; and (2) they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled. Id.
 
We find that equitable tolling is not warranted in this case where the respondent has failed to exercise due diligence in pursing his case. The respondent’s prior motion to reopen, which was also premised on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, was denied two years ago because the respondent failed to comply with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Nor is this motion supported by evidence that he has complied with Lozada. He has simply failed to show that he exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2nd Cir. 2006) (finding that aliens had demonstrated that their counsel was ineffective in informing them that they did not need to appear at a hearing, but they had not met their burden of showing why they had not been diligent over a two-year period).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to reopen these removal proceedings will also be denied as number barred. Federal regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(D) provide an exception to the one-motion rule when a motion seeking to rescind an in absentia order is filed. However, that exception is limited to motions that are filed in deportation and exclusion proceedings. There is no equivalent exception in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) (providing an exception only to the time, but not to the numerical, restrictions on motions in removal proceedings); 1003.23(b)(4)(h), (iii)(D). Thus, unless another exception is shown to apply, the respondent’s second motion is number barred.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion is denied as time and number barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 243758 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 243727 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OJUOLAPE OLIVIA OVERSTREET A.K.A. OLIVIA OJUOLAPE OVERSTREET A.K.A. OJUOLAPE OLIVIA OGUNFOWOKAN A.K.A. OJUOLAPE OLIVIA ORENIYI
FILE: AXX XX1 826 - BALTIMORE, MD
January 9, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven Kreiss, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer E. Piateski
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on January 27, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s denial of a prior motion to reopen. The instant motion, which was filed on November 23, 2007, is both time and number barred. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), only one motion to reopen (whether before the Board or the Immigration Judge) is permitted in deportation proceedings and the motion must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. Assuming that the Fourth Circuit would recognize ineffective assistance as a basis for equitably tolling the restrictions on motions, the respondent’s motion makes no attempt to explain why she waited another three years after her last motion was denied to pursue her ineffective assistance of counsel claim against her former counsel.¿FN1 º Accordingly, we do not find an adequate basis to equitably toll the time and number restrictions on motions. Accordingly, the motion is denied as time and number barred.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent’s arguments related to eligibility for section 212(c) relief are not persuasive in any event because the charge based on commission of a crime involving moral turpitude was withdrawn, leaving as the sole charge of deportability the section 241 (a)(l)(D)(I) (termination of conditional permanent resident status) charge which does not have a statutory counterpart in section 212 of the Act. See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(f)(5); Matter of Brieva, 23 I&N Dec. 766 (BIA 2005).

2008 WL 243728 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ERIC JAMES BLAKE
FILE: AXX XX0 860 - NAPANOCH, NY
January 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º -Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a “Motion to Reconsider” on November 23, 2007. The Board previously dismissed the respondent’s appeal on October 24, 2007, for lack of jurisdiction as his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) was untimely filed on September 14, 2007, the Immigration Judge having rendered his decision orally on April 11, 2007. The respondent continues to argue in the pending motion, as he did on appeal, that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in proceedings before the Immigration Court. Moreover, he continues to take issue with the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2007, decision on a number of other bases. We noted in our October 24, 2007, decision that, if the respondent wished to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, the motion was to be filed with the Board. However, in challenging any other finding or seeking to reopen, the motion must be filed with the Immigration Court. In the current motion, no error with respect to the Board’s finding that his Notice of Appeal was untimely filed or our conclusion that we lacked jurisdiction in this matter, has been alleged by the respondent. There is no adequate basis to reconsider our decision dismissing the respondent’s September 14, 2007, appeal, filed more than 5 months after the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2007, decision. The motion is, therefore, denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley

2008 WL 243723 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANGELO VARELA
FILE: AXX XX4 839 - BOSTON, MA
January 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL/MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Rubin, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º -Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 27, 2007, finding the respondent removable, as charged, based on his record of conviction (Exh. 3), and determining that he was ineligible for relief from removal. While the respondent’s appeal was pending before the Board, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and remand this matter to the Immigration Court for further proceedings based on new, material, and previously unavailable evidence. The respondent’s request is premised on the claim that the conviction which forms the basis for the grounds of removal, has been vacated. Specifically, the respondent has presented evidence that on November 9, 2007, the New Bedford District Court, sitting in New Bedford, Massachusetts, granted the respondent’s Motion to Withdraw Guilty Plea and Vacate His Conviction¿FN1º on the basis of ineffective assistance of former counsel at his criminal trial, and continued the case for a new hearing on the respondent’s motion to suppress. We observe the respondent’s motion was properly served on the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), and the DHS has filed no opposition or other response to this motion, and the motion is deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3).
 
The Board in Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003), reversed on other grounds, Pickering v. Gonzales, 465 F.3d 263 (6th Cir.2006), held that if a court vacates a conviction based upon a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying proceedings, there is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes. However, an alien remains convicted of a removable offense when the predicate conviction is vacated simply for rehabilitative purposes or to aid the alien in avoiding adverse immigration consequences and not because of any procedural or substantive defect in the original conviction. Id. at 624; see also Saleh v. Gonzales, 495 F.3d 17, 25 (2d Cir. 2007) (joining and citing to the decisions of the various circuits in support of the Board’s decision in Pickering that if a court vacates an alien’s criminal conviction solely on the basis of immigration hardships or rehabilitation, rather than on the basis of a substantive or procedural defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, the conviction is not eliminated for immigration purposes and will continue to serve as a valid factual predicate for a charge of removability despite its vacatur); see also Matter of Chavez-Martinez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007) (alien’s conviction cannot be vacated solely for immigration purposes).
 
*2 In the matter before us, however, it is clear the criminal court judge’s ruling granting the respondent’s Motion to Withdraw Guilty Plea and Vacate His Conviction, was based on his finding the respondent’s former criminal counsel’s failure to move to suppress evidence at the respondent’s criminal trial constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. Therefore, the court’s action was premised upon what it perceived to be a constitutional infirmity in the underlying proceedings, rather than some form of post-conviction relief. See Rumierz v. Gonzales, 456 F.3d 31, 34-35 (1st Cir. 2006) (a vacated conviction is no longer a “conviction” within the meaning of the immigration laws only “if a court with jurisdiction vacates ¿theº conviction based on a defect in the underlying criminal proceedings.”); Herrera-lnirio v. INS, 203 F.3d 299 (1st Cir. 2000).
 
The question here is whether there is now an aggravated felony conviction, or any conviction, rendering the respondent not only removable but also statutorily ineligible for relief, or whether the proceedings should be terminated. Consequently, in view of the foregoing and the lack of opposition by the DHS, the respondent’s motion will be granted, the record will be remanded to enable the Immigration Judge to consider the new evidence offered by the respondent relating to his removability under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and(B)(i) of the Act, and to determine whether the respondent can now establish his eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the proceedings should be terminated.
 
Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

(Exh. 3).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board Of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO DE PARAMO
FILE: AXX XX0 262 - MIAMI, FL
January 3, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
  
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda Osberg-Braun, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. By decision mailed August 7, 2007, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia, and thereafter on October 1, 2007, denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a timely Notice of Appeal from the latter decision. The matter will be remanded.
 
The respondent contends that he filed a change of address with the Department of Homeland Security prior to the initiation of these proceedings, and that the DHS thus erred in not sending the Notice to Appear to his new, correct address, an error repeated by the Immigration Court when it sent the notice for the August 7, 2007, hearing date. Alternatively, the respondent contends that his order of removal in absentia should be rescinded because his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel in failing to correct the address error, and in failing to enter an appearance with the Immigration Court, which would have resulted in counsel receiving the hearing notice.
 
We find the respondent’s first argument unavailing. He admits to having received the Notice to Appear (NTA), which was sent to his former address and forwarded to him by the Postal Service. At that point, he was on notice that the address on the NTA was defective, and he was under obligation, as clearly stated in the NTA, to notify the Immigration Court of his current address. His failure to do so, which resulted in the sending of the Notice of Hearing for August 7, 2007, cannot be excused under the law of the applicable Circuit nor of this Board. Dominguez v. United States Att’y Gen’l, 284 F.3d 1258 (11th Cir. 2002); Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001). The respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are first presented on appeal, and their resolution will require findings of fact that are best resolved in the first instance by the Immigration Judge. The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), with respect to Mr. Soto, his former counsel. While there is evidence that the respondent hired Mr. Soto to represent him in “deportation” proceedings, and while the respondent contends on appeal that he informed Mr. Soto of the initiation of these removal proceedings, there is no response as yet from Mr. Soto. The Immigration Judge on remand shall re-consider the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal in light of the new claims presented regarding alleged ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed with respect to the issues presented in the respondent’s initial motion to reopen, and the proceedings are remanded for further consideration of that motion in light of the new allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4818611 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD SARFRAZ KHAN
FILE: AXX XX8 104 - HOUSTON, TX
December 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amen Omo Obas, Esquire¿FN1º
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated August 17, 2005. On August 17, 2005, the Board affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s request for a continuance based on a pending application for labor certification, denied a motion to remand for adjustment of status based on the approval of the labor certification of the respondent’s wife on April 11, 2005, and reinstated a grant of voluntary departure. On October 28, 2005, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our decision on the merits. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on August 2, 2007, seeks reopening for consideration of adjustment of status and/or cancellation of removal and is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The motion is denied as untimely filed.
 
The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We find that under the facts alleged ineffective assistance of counsel has not been shown. In the complaint filed with the Texas Bar, the respondent asserts, inter alia, that his former attorney did not timely file an appeal of his case with this Board. However, the record reveals that the appeal was, in fact, timely filed. In the motion to reopen, the respondent contends that his former attorney should have filed a timely motion to reopen for consideration of cancellation of removal and/or adjustment of status. The only potential qualifying relative for cancellation of removal mentioned in the current motion is the respondent’s wife, who became a lawful permanent resident on March 9, 2006, more than 90 days after our August 17, 2005, decision. Consequently, the respondent could not have shown that he had prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal or adjustment of status so as to timely file a motion to reopen. Contrary to the respondent’s contentions, the Board itself and Immigration Judges are without authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the DHS so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation. See Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 338 (BIA 1991). Moreover, we also decline to reopen sua sponte. Simply acquiring additional equities after a final order of removal does not establish an exceptional situation that would support circumventing the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. The f£.ct that the respondent’s wife is now a lawful permanent resident and he is the beneficiary of a pending visa petition for derivative adjustment does not provide a basis for sua sponte reopening.See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied as untimely, and the request for a stay of removal is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Amen Omo Obas, Esquire, has not submitted a motion to withdraw as counsel. Consequently, we consider Amen Omo Obas as the attorney of record.

2007 WL 4818600 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO CUEVAS-ROSAS
FILE: AXX XX9 468 - SAN DIEGO, CA
December 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Howard Johnson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J. Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 3, 2007, denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), in the exercise of discretion. The Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of cancellation of removal is affirmed pursuant to this Board’s authority under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(5). This Board agrees with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is undeserving of a favorable exercise of discretion on his application for cancellation of removal as it does not appear in the best interests of the United States that the respondent be allowed to remain in this nation. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978).
 
The respondent’s contention on appeal that his former counsel rendered ineffective assistance at the hearing by failing to present the testimony of the respondent’s daughters as witnesses is without legal merit. Ineffective assistance of counsel in a removal proceedings is a denial of due process if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. See Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). In any case presenting an ineffectiveness claim, the performance inquiry must be whether counsel’s assistance was reasonable considering all the circumstances. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 688 (1984). Methods of case presentation are within counsel’s professional judgment and are afforded great deference. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 688-89. Strategic decisions, such as whether to call a particular witness at the hearing, rest upon the sound professional judgment of the lawyer, Gustave v. United States, 627 F.2d 901, 904 (9th Cir. 1980). Such appropriately informed strategic decisions are virtually unchallengeable. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 690.
 
The assertion by respondent’s present counsel that the testimony of the respondent’s step-daughters should have been presented is not fact but opinion. On the contrary, the presentation of witness testimony also carries with it the right of cross-examination which may result in the identification of additional adverse information or reason to discount otherwise favorable information. Furthermore, the service of the respondent’s adult step-daughter in the armed services of the United States is of de minimis weight in assessing whether the respondent should be allowed to remain in this nation as her character is not at issue and is not a reflection upon the respondent’s character.
 
*2 The judgments of former counsel criticized by present counsel are the kind of strategic decisions that are virtually unchallengeable. This is not an instance where former counsel failed to meet a deadline or submit an application, nor is this an instance where former counsel did not reasonably prepare for the hearing or present any evidence on the respondent’s behalf. Rather, this is an instance where present counsel is second-guessing former counsel’s method of case presentation and strategic decisions, out of a belief, which this Board has little reason to believe is correct, that his own methods would have resulted in success. As such, in the absence of “egregious circumstances,” which have not been established in this matter, an alien is bound by the “reasonable tactical decisions of his counsel.” See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995), citing Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 383 (BIA 1986); see also Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 933-34 (9th Cir. 1986). The respondent was not prevented from reasonably presenting his case that he was deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4818600 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2007 WL 4711452 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZIAD HIRBAWI
FILE: AXX XX7 984 - CLEVELAND, OH
December 27, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Margaret Wong, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Victoria Christian
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on December 29, 2003, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on July 25, 2006, but the Immigration Judge denied it on October 2, 2006. This appeal followed. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel in these proceedings because his former counsel did not inform the Immigration Court of his change of address as promised, failed to notify the respondent of his hearing, and told him that his case remained pending with the Immigration Court while knowing that the respondent had been ordered removed from the United States in absentia. The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for establishing attorney negligence and prior counsel has not responded to the allegation. See Matter ofLozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465 (6th Cir. 2003).
 
The Sixth Circuit has not yet ruled on the issue of whether ineffective assistance of counsel warrants the equitable tolling of the time and number restrictions on motions. See Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407 (6th Cir. 2003). However, the court has held that equitable tolling does not apply when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. See Dunlap v. United States, 250 F.3d 1001, 1008 (6th Cir. 2001)(statute o flimitations may only be tolled where the circumstances are both beyond the petitioner’s control and unavoidable even with due diligence). In the instant case, the respondent was apprehended by immigration authorities in July 2006 and filed his request for reopening within that month (See respondent’s affidavit dated September 18, 2006, at paragraph #16). We therefore conclude that the respondent has pursued reopening with due diligence and therefore find that his motion was submitted timely.
 
The respondent has established based on the allegations set forth in his affidavit that prior counsel was negligent in his representation. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998) (requiring that counsel’s representation be egregious and it rendered the heating unfair). Apparently, prior counsel received the Notice of Hearing sent to him by the Immigration Court notwithstanding that he did not file a Notice of Appearance. He also was given an opportunity to respond to the serious allegation of negligence alleged by the respondent but has chosen not to. We find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings based on the respondent’s uncontested charges that set forth a prima case of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 In reaching our decision, we have considered that the DHS has opposed the reopening of these proceedings. According to the DHS, the respondent was responsible for his failure to appear for his heating because he did not stay in contact with his attorney or provide counsel and the Immigration Court with his current address. However, the respondent indicates in his affidavit that he moved to 4819 Prestwick Crossing, Westlake, Ohio 44145 in September 2002. He further indicates that he contacted his former attorney within a week to notify counsel of the change of address. The respondent states that counsel assured him that he would notify both the DHS and the Immigration Court of the change. The respondent further states in his affidavit that counsel continually informed him that everything was taken care of and the respondent would receive all required notices. In addition, the respondent indicates that he spoke with his former attorney on a few occasions in 2004 and in the beginning of 2005. Former counsel supposedly told the respondent in 2005 that he need not keep calling because he would tell the respondent when his heating would occur. We therefore cannot conclude that the respondent did not maintain contact with his former attorney. He also does not appear to be at fault for not submitting the required notice of his address change because his attorney had assured him that such was completed on his behalf. The respondent also appears to have been the victim of egregious attorney misconduct. Failing to notify the respondent of his hearing and informing the Immigration Court of the respondent’s address change constitutes negligence. The matter is compounded by former counsel’s supposed representation to the respondent that his case was pending for a hearing while knowing that the respondent had been ordered removed from the United States in absentia.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is sustained, these proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4711452 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4711459 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FERNANDO JOSE RIVERA-RIVERA
FILE: AXX XX8 686 - NEWARK, NJ
December 27, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joseph C. Sekula, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on October 3, 2006, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. In a prior decision dated December 22, 2004, we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal because he failed to file a brief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E) (2007). In denying his subsequent motion to reopen, we found that the respondent failed to establish that he was the subject of ineffective assistance of counsel such that his untimely request for reopening should be granted. The respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements for such a claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see Zheng v. Gonzales, 422 F.3d 98, 106 (3d Cir. 2005), Furthermore, we found that the respondent had failed to establish that his former attorney’s actions caused him prejudice because the relief for which he sought reopening - a visa petition with which to obtain adjustment of status - was not available to him for three reasons: (1) the respondent is inadmissible for having failed to reveal that his mother, who had filed a visa petition on the respondent’s behalf, had passed away before the visa was issued; (2) the respondent failed to apply for a waiver of the ground of inadmissibility under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i); and (3) the respondent failed to establish prima facie eligibility for relief by providing a sponsor if granted reinstatement of his visa petition. We also noted that both a waiver of inadmissibility and adjustment of status under section 212(i) and 245 of the Act, 8 U.s.c. §§ 1182(i), 1255, are discretionary and, therefore, in light of his fraud, not relief he would merit.
 
On June 1, 2007, the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit granted the government’s unopposed motion to remand. That motion correctly noted that a lawful permanent resident is not viewed as seeking admission with. 6 exceptions.¿FN1º See section 101(a)(13)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (a)(13)(C). The government’s motion asserts that the Notice to Appear lacks specificity in that it does not indicate which exception applies to the respondent. Although the respondent admits that he did not specifically raise this issue in his motion to reopen (Respondent’s Motion before the Third Circuit at 6-7), he argued that he was clearly prejudiced by his former attorney’s failure to argue that the factual allegations in the Notice to Appear do not support a finding of removability under section 212 of the Act.
 
*2 At the outset, we note that the respondent has apparently been removed from the United States (Respondent’s Br. Before the Third Circuit). We generally decline to render an opinion in such cases because our regulations specify that departure during the pendency of an appeal constitutes withdrawal of said appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.4; but see Chong v. District Direction, INS, 264 F.3d 378 (3d Cir. 2001) (holding that sufficient collateral consequences flowed from the Board’s order of removal that the case remained worthy of review despite the alien’s deportation). However, since the Circuit Court has mandated an answer to the questions raised in the government’s motion, we understand that mandate to govern this remand.
 
We find that the Notice to Appear alleges adequate facts with which to find the respondent removable as charged.¿FN2º In essence, the Notice alleges that the respondent never lawfully obtained permanent resident status because he obtained his status through fraud: he failed to reveal that his mother, who had filed a visa petition on his behalf, was deceased. Once fraud of this character is established, the respondent would not qualify as a lawful permanent resident relieved of the “admission” construct upon return from a trip abroad. See Matter of Koloamatangi, 23 I&N Dec. 548 (BIA 2003) (finding an alien who acquired permanent resident status through fraud or misrepresentation has never been “lawfully admitted for permanent residence” and was, therefore, ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)).
 
As before, we also decline to remand based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. In Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000), we stated that where an alien has filed an untimely motion to reopen alleging that the DHS failed to prove the alien’s removability, the burden of proof no longer lies with the DHS to establish removability, but shifts to the alien to demonstrate that an exceptional situation exists that warrants reopening by the Board on its own motion. It is not sufficient for the respondent to argue, as he did in his brief to the Third Circuit, that he could have provided explanations or defenses for the alleged fraud which might exculpate him. As we stated in Matter of Beckford, supra, “¿nºotwithstanding any possible problems with the Immigration Judge’s handling of the hearing or with the actions (or inaction) of previous counsel, the respondent, at a minimum, needed to demonstrate a substantial likelihood that the result in his case would be changed if reopening is granted.” ld. at 1219.
 
The respondent has not adequately shown that he was unaware that his mother was deceased at the time his visa was granted and that he had no role in obtaining the visa by fraud. And, even assuming that the respondent’s sister completed the paperwork for the adjustment of status application or that the visa application was not signed by the respondent, he was still not entitled to the visa or to the resident alien status it provided. Therefore, he was not entitled to this immigration benefit.
 
*3 Furthermore, the fact that he may have been qualified to seek other remedies is now irrelevant in that he was never entitled to the entry document he received. The possible relief for which he might have applied, including a waiver of inadmissibility, is discretionary and we have no way of knowing the outcome if the respondent had disclosed the truth and timely applied for such relief without pursuing his fraud. At this stage, the respondent’s attempts to remedy his fraud, or to excuse the improper receipt of permanent resident status, do not constitute exceptional circumstances which would warrant reopening on the basis of an untimely motion.¿FN3º
 
ORDER: The Board’s October 3, 2006, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen is affirmed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that it is clear that the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) became aware of the alleged fraud in the respondent’s and his siblings’ visa petition applications in 1999 and the agency intended not to grant their applications for lawful permanent residency. However, other evidence of record establishes that the respondent did adjust his status during that same year (Exh. 2). There is no evidence to establish that this status was ever rescinded.

2

We note the respondent’s argument before the Third Circuit that the DHS officer who issued the Notice to Appear failed to describe his title. According to the respondent, this, along with the fact that the Notice was served on the respondent a month after he attempted to enter, entitle the respondent to termination. However, the fact that the officer’s title was abbreviated as “II” or left off certain lines in the Notice to Appear is not a defect which warrants termination. As we stated in our previous order, at footnote 1, the regulations do not specify the format with which a title must be listed. See 8 C.F.R. § 239.1; see also Diaz-Soto v. INS, 797 F.2d 262 (5th Cir. 1986) (finding that the charging document need not require the actual signature of the Immigration and Naturalization Service official authorized to issue such documents). We decline to find, as suggested by the respondent, that the lack of title means the respondent would have been admitted as a lawful permanent resident because the DHS cannot meet its burden under the regulations.

3

In light of our decision, we decline to address the respondent’s arguments that our previous decision took on a formulaic view of the respondent’s ineffective assistance claims or that it ignored or confused the other issues raised (Respondent’s Br. Before the Third Circuit at 36-47). See Zheng v. Gonzales, supra, at 106 (noting that strict, formulaic view of motions alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, or those in which the alien fails to meet the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, are disfavored).


2007 WL 4711459 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4711446 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOPES, RICARDO
FILE: AXX-XX1-616 - BOSTON, MA
December 26, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Borges, Judith A., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 09, 2007. An alien in removal proceedings is accorded a statutory tight to counsel, but at his or her own expense, under sections 240(b)(4)(A) and 292 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. Removal proceedings may be conducted, even though an alien does not have counsel, as long as 10 days have elapsed since service of the Notice to Appear. Section 239(b)(3) of the Act. On appeal, the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in holding the heating when the respondent’s attorney failed to appear on his behalf. However, the record reveals that the respondent was provided with a list of organizations and attorneys which provide free legal services when he was served with the Notice to Appear on January 19, 2007. He was again provided with a list of attorneys who might represent him at little or no cost at the hearing on May 7, 2007 (Tr. at 3), and subsequently reminded that he could consult with tan attorney (Tr. at 46). The respondent indicated on more than one occasion that he had not obtained counsel, that he did not intend to obtain counsel (Tr. at 21), and that he intended to speak for or represent himself (Tr. at 6, 21). The record contains no entry of appearance before the Immigration Judge by any attorney. Moreover, at the heating on the merits on July 9, 2007, the respondent made no request for a continuance nor did he otherwise refer to having an attorney who failed to appear. Consequently, the respondent has not shown that there was any ineffective assistance of counsel. Although the respondent asserts that his constitutional tights have been violated, the Immigration Judge and the Board are not empowered to role on the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations under which we adjudicate. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992); Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343 (BIA 1982).
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R § 1003.1 (d)(3)(1). Under this standard, the Board may not reverse an Immigration Judge’s factfinchng unless we determine that the Immigration Judge committed clear error. See Matter of SH-, 23 I&N Dec. 462, 464-65 (BIA 2002) (stating that the Board must defer to the factual determinations of an Immigration Judge in the absence of clear error). In this case, we find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s factual findings. In addition, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s negative equities, including his criminal history, gang membership, and lack of stable employment, outweigh his positive equities including his lengthy residence in the United States and his extensive family ties. See Matter of OC-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); Matter of Matin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978). On appeal, the respondent argues, through counsel, that the Immigration Judge gave insufficient weight to his positive equities. We disagree. The Immigration Judge considered these factors, but concluded that the recency and seriousness of the respondent’s criminal activities indicated a lack of rehabilitation and outweighed his positive equities. The Immigration Judge’s conclusions were reasonable and we find no reason to overturn them. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4711420 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE CESAR FERNANDEZ-ZARATE
FILE: AXX XX1 067 - LOS ANGELES, CA
December 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nhu H. Muon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on March 22, 2006, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on December 4, 2006, but the Immigration Judge denied it on January 30, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that he was the victim of negligent advice provided to him by an individual purporting to be an attorney and such prevented him from attending his hearing. He argues that he established that his failure to appear was caused by an exceptional circumstance. The respondent indicates in his declaration that he received the Notice to Appear reflecting that his hearing would be held on May 2, 2006. He further indicates that he subsequently received the heating notice, and it indicated that his hearing would be held on March 22, 2006. According to the respondent, the non-attorney instructed him that the hearing date on the Notice to Appear controlled. He argues that he should not be penalized for relying on the incorrect advice given by an individual who he believed was an attorney. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision and his reliance on Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943 (9th Cir. 1999) (alien’s acceptance of advice of immigration consultant not to appear at deportation hearing does not constitute exceptional circumstance that would excuse his absence from hearing).
 
A claim alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy certain procedural requirements. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Among them is the requirement that the individual accused of negligence be informed of the allegation raised before the Immigration Court or the Board and allowed an opportunity to respond. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003) (noting that the purpose of the notice requirement is to allow the prior counsel an opportunity to contest the allegations of ineffective assistance so that the adjudicator can more accurately assess the claim). Herein, the respondent filed a complaint against the non-attorney, but he has not provided the individual with an opportunity to reply to the allegation raised in these instant proceedings. Consequently, the Board and the Immigration Judge cannot assess the respondent’s accounts against the accounts of the non-attorney. We will not find that the respondent was the victim of attorney negligence under these circumstances. It follows that the respondent has not shown that exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance for his hearing. We therefore need not address whether the motion to reopen was timely filed.
 
*2 The respondent further argues on appeal that he was not adequately notified of the hearing because neither the charging document nor the hearing notice were written in Spanish. However, the respondent admits that he received both documents, and understood his obligation to appear for a hearing. He also states in his declaration that his failure to attend his hearing was caused by a non-attorney advising not to appear in Immigration Court on the date reflected on the Notice of Hearing. Clearly, the respondent’s failure to appear was not caused by the hearing notice not being written in Spanish. We therefore cannot find that the respondent had no notice of his hearing. There is also no requirement that notices be translated into the respondent’s native language. See generally Lopes v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 81 (2nd Cir. 2006). This aspect of the appeal is dismissed.
 
Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed, and the appeal is dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
