2007 WL 4711436 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA SACARIAS DE PINEDO
FILE: AXX XX6 892 - SAN DIEGO, CA
December 20, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Salazar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the December 4, 2006, decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent’s removability under section 212(a)(6)(E)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”) is not at issue on appeal. 8 U.S.C.§ 1182(a)6)(E)(i). The respondent does contends that she was entitled to the relief of cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act as a lawful permanent resident and that the Immigration Judge failed to properly balance the adverse factors of record against those favorable to her. The respondent also alleges that there were due process violations that tainted these proceedings. We find her claims to be without support in the record or legal merit. The denial of relief will be affirmed and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge was thorough in his consideration of the record. We agree that the respondent’s equities include her long residence here; family ties and the presence of three United States citizen children;¿FN1º hardship; and her past employment history. However, these equities are outweighed by the unfavorable factors of record which include a criminal record for smuggling and prostitution, an earlier, undisclosed incident of smuggling as well as an adverse credibility finding which is amply supported by the record and undermines any claim she can show rehabilitation (I.J. at 17-19). We agree therefore in the denial of relief. To the extent she contends that the Immigration Judge employed a heightened standard of proof, this claim is without merit. Brief on Appeal at 32-33. Her application for relief was filed on June 13, 2006, or after May 11, 2005, so, her credibility as it related to meeting her burden of proof was properly judged under the REAL ID. Act of 2005.
 
The respondent separately challenges the result in her case on due process grounds. First, she asserts that the Immigration Judge was biased. Brief on Appeal at 21. She fails to point out when he was biased in these proceedings and nowhere does the record bear out her claim. Accordingly, we dismiss it. Secondly, she contends that the government failed to produce Exhibit 8, Form G-166 (Report of Investigation) and Exhibit 9, Form I-213 (Record of Deportable/ Inadmissible Alien), in a timely fashion and that its late submission prejudiced her ability to prepare her case and correctly recollect her prior statements at the time of her arrest for smuggling. The record indicates that these documents were offered by the government as rebuttal evidence, however, to impeach the respondent’s testimony. The Immigration Judge properly overruled the respondent’s objection to the introduction of these documents below (Tr. at 75). Moreover, while claiming prejudice, the respondent’s position on appeal is that notwithstanding the problems she faced preparing her case, the record and her equities “compel” a grant of this appeal. Brief on Appeal at 24. We therefore find she cannot establish prejudice since the late introduction of these documents did not preclude her from presenting her case, even if the government was required to present these documents at an earlier stage in the proceedings. We observe that the respondent is not contesting any of the information contained in exhibits 8 and 9; she simply wishes that had she remembered what she told the officer at the time, she would have testified consistent with those statements at her hearing. Brief on Appeal at 12, 18. Because she is not contesting the truth of the information contained in these exhibits, we find no support for her assertions now that the documents should have been authenticated or certified, too.
 
*2 Lastly, the respondent indirectly claims that she was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of her prior counsel. See Brief on Appeal at 18, fn. 2 and 24. She believes her in-court testimony in particular would have been better prepared, the Immigration Judge would not have concluded she lacked credibility, and her equities generally would have been better presented. Yet, none of the requirements for properly asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), have been met. Additionally, the respondent has not shown, as with her other due process claims, that she was actually prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

However, the respondent, who has the burden of proof in this application, chose not to present supporting evidence from her family members because she did not want them to know she was in removal proceedings (I.J. at 10). See also Tr. at 42-43.

2007 WL 4707388 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISOLDA CASTILLO-MONTENEGRO A.K.A. ISOLDA CASTILLO A.K.A. FABRIOLA MARTINEZ
FILE: AXX XX3 309 - LOS ANGELES, CA
December 17, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Keili M. Reynolds, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
In a decision dated February 13, 2006, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent has appealed. During the pendency of her appeal the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which we will treat as a motion to remand. The appeal will be dismissed, and the motion granted.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, was convicted on January 5, 1995, in the Municipal Court of Los Angeles, Van Nuys Judicial District, State of California, for the offense of petty theft in violation of section 490.1 of the California Penal Code. (Exhs. 2, 4). The respondent was also convicted on April 20, 1999, in the Municipal Court of Los Angeles, Van Nuys Judicial District, State of California, for the offense of theft of property in violation of 484(a) of the California Penal Code. The Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent, who was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident in 1994, could not demonstrate 7 years continuous residence necessary for cancellation of removal because her theft conviction in 1999 triggered the “stop time” rule and ended the respondent’s period of continuous physical presence. See section 240A(d)(l) of the Act.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that she is eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Act under the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit’s, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, decisions in Lepe-Guitron v. INS, 16 F.3d 1021 (9th Cir. 1994), and Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2005). Specifically, the respondent argues that because her father became a lawful permanent resident in 1989 that she should be able to impute his 7 years of lawful permanent residence status (Respondent’s Brief at 3).
 
In Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, 430 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2005), the Ninth Circuit decided to extend its decision in Lepe-Guitron v. INS, supra, finding that “for purposes of satisfying the seven-years of continuous residence ‘after having been admitted in any status’ required for cancellation of removal…., a parent’s admission for permanent residence status is imputed to the parent’s unemancipated minor children residing with the parent.” Id. at 1029. In that case, the alien had entered the United States with his parents in 1985 and his mother attained lawful permanent residence in 1990, when he was 7 years old. Id. at 1016. The alien in that case obtained his lawful residency on December 4, 1997, when he was 14 years old. Id. Subsequently, on October 16, 2002, the alien was convicted of a burglary offense and was placed into removal proceedings. Id. The Ninth Circuit concluded that the alien in Cuevas- Gaspar had established the 7 years continuous residence for purposes of section 240A of the Act because he could “tack on” the approximately 7 years of his mother’s permanent residency prior to his own, namely 1990 through December 4, 1997. Thus, despite the fact that the alien ceased accumulating continuous residence sometime in 2002 at which time he had only acquired approximately 4 years in his own right, he had accumulated 7 years continuous residence when “tacking on” that of his mother when he was an “unemancipated minor.” Id.
 
*2 Cuevas-Gaspar and Lepe-Guitron allows for the imputation of the parents’ permanent residency to the child based upon the child’s status as an “unemancipated minor,” (emphasis added). Here, the respondent was admitted to the United States in 1994 as a lawful permanent resident on May 23, 1994, when she was approximately 17 1/2 years old. At most, the respondent would be able to impute less than 6 months of her father’s lawful permanent residency before she turned 18 and no longer qualified as an “unemancipated minor child residing with the parent.” Cuevas-Gaspar v. Gonzales, supra. We do not view the rationale in these cases to allow the respondent to accumulate her period of permanent residency for a time in which she was not residing with a lawful permanent parent as an “unemancipated minor.”As a result, we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent is ineligible for cancellation of removal.
 
We next turn to the respondent’s motion to remand seeking an opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. The respondent contends that because of the ineffective assistance of her prior counsel she did not apply for this form of relief, although she was eligible for such a waiver. At the outset, we find that the respondent has complied with the technical requirements for ineffective assistance of counsel claims. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, “substantial prejudice” must be shown; such prejudice means that the alleged ineffectiveness affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000). Based upon the record before us, it appears that the respondent may be eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, and that competent counsel should have applied for such a waiver. Accordingly, we will grant the motion and remand for consideration of the respondent’s eligibility for a waiver under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is granted and the record remanded for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
Chris Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4707388 (BIA)
End of Document

    1  Review Granted by Freckleton v. Holder, 9th Cir., August 23, 2010
2007 WL 4707375 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AKOSUA AMPONSAAH FRECKLETON
FILE: AXX XX0 758 - PHOENIX, AZ
December 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
CERTIFICATION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Charles H. Kuck, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Arthur S. Raznick
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On November 21,2005, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States in absentia because she failed to appear for her hearing. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on December 12, 2005, but that request was denied on January 27, 2006. The respondent filed a timely appeal from this decision. On August 8, 2006, the Board remanded the record to the Immigration Court to address with specificity the respondent’s argument that her good faith confusion regarding the time of her hearing combined with the alleged negligence of her former counsel constitutes an “exceptional circumstance” to excuse her failure to appear. After considering this argument, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen on September 25, 2006, and certified the record back to the Board. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he or she did not receive proper notice of the time and place of the hearing, or because he or she was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his or her own. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act. In this case, the only issue is whether the respondent failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent explained that she did not appear for her hearing because she mistakenly believed that it was scheduled for November 21, 2005, at 4:30 p.m., rather than at 8:30 a.m. She reported discussing her case with her former attorney the month before the hearing, and advised him that she was relocating to Georgia but would return for the 4:30 p.m. hearing. Although the respondent mentioned the incorrect time for the hearing, her prior counsel did not correct her misunderstanding. The respondent received a telephone call from her former attorney when she arrived at the airport in Phoenix, Arizona at approximately 10:00 a.m. on the day of the hearing, and he asked why she failed to appear. After explaining that an order of removal had been issued in absentia, the respondent’s prior counsel instructed her to drive directly to the courthouse because he planned to advise the Immigration Judge that there was a misunderstanding about the time of the hearing and that she was on her way. According to the respondent, he called her approximately a half an hour later to instruct her to drive to his office. When the respondent’s husband asked whether they could go to the courthouse to explain that they were late for the hearing due to “an honest mistake,” her former attorney explained that all of the hearings for the rest of the day had been canceled and the court was closed because a court employee was having a baby. The respondent maintains that her prior counsel exercised poor judgment when he advised her to drive to his office rather than the courthouse because she could have arrived less than 2 hours late and explained her tardiness. Further, she argues that her former attorney was negligent in failing to file her application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
*2 The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent failed to establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the hearing on November 21, 2005, based on her confusion regarding the scheduled time. See Valencia-Fragoso v. INS, 321 F.3d 1204 (9th Cir. 2003) (alien’s alleged misunderstanding of time of hearing did not constitute exceptional circumstances justifying reopening). Although the respondent submitted evidence showing that she complied with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), she failed to establish that she suffered any prejudice due to her former counsel’s actions. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9thCir. 2004); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241,1246 (9th Cir. 2000). Compliance with the procedural aspects of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel alone is not sufficient to warrant reopening. To prove a Fifth Amendment due process violation, the respondent must show both error from the ineffective assistance of counsel, and that she suffered prejudice as a result of that error. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
As noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent was notified of the date and time of her hearing, and she was responsible for ensuring her attendance. The fact that her prior counsel failed to correct her when she misstated the time of her hearing does not amount to ineffective assistance of counsel because the respondent has not shown that she was incapable of recording the proper time and making the necessary arrangements to appear at her hearing, or that her former attorney was obligated to verify that she recalled the information correctly. While the respondent suggests that her prior counsel erred in directing her to come to his office instead of the courthouse, she acknowledged that these instructions were given approximately 30 minutes after he advised her to drive directly to the courthouse and that he subsequently explained that the court was closed before she could arrive. Although the respondent argues that her prior attorney committed error when he failed to seek a section 212(c) waiver of inadmissibility, the Immigration Judge explained that the respondent’s former counsel was prepared to present arguments in support of this form of relief at the November 21, 2005, hearing. Consequently, we conclude that the respondent has not shown prejudice flowing from the alleged errors. We find that based upon the evidence of record, the respondent has not met the burden of demonstrating exceptional circumstances under the statute. Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4707375 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4707376 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEON ANDREW BAPTISTE
FILE: AXX XX0 366 - CHICAGO, IL
December 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Swaray Conteh, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Kostich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101 (a)(43)(A))
  
APPLICATION: Waiver under section 212(c)
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Trinidad and Tobago and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s September 1, 2006, decision, denying the respondent’s application for a waiver of removal under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). For the reasons set out below, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge with the following additions. Because the aggravated felony ground of removal applicable in this case, i.e., “sexual abuse of a minor” under section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A),¿FN1º has no statutory counterpart in the grounds of inadmissibility under section 212(a) of the Act, the respondent is not statutorily eligible to apply for a section 212(c) waiver. Matter of Blake, 23 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 2005); see also Matter of Valere, 473 F.3d 757, 761-62 (7th Cir. 2007) (finding that alien’s crime of indecent assault of a minor, “like Blake’s crime of sexual abuse of a minor-had no statutory counterpart in § 212(a) at the time ¿the alienº pleaded guilty …. and application of Blake … was not impermissibly retroactive).
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that his case is distinguishable from Blake, supra, and Valere, supra, because his guilty plea was entered prior to enactment of the Immigration Act of 1990, Pub. L. No. 101-649, § 511 (a), 104 Stat. 4978, 5052 (“IMMACT”), which rendered aliens ineligible for section 212(c) relief who had been convicted of an aggravated felony and who served a prison term of at least 5 years. In support of this distinction, the respondent cites Toia v. Fasano, 334 F.3d 917 (9th Cir. 2003), wherein the Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit held that the IMMACT bar does not apply to aliens who pleaded guilty prior to enactment of IMMACT. However, we find Toia, supra, inapposite, for the following reasons: (a) unlike Toia, supra, here, the Immigration Judge did not bar the respondent from seeking a section 212(e) waiver based on IMMACT; (b) the record does not indicate that the respondent served a prison term of at least 5 years; and (c) Toia, supra, does not provide any relief for the respondent, as he was otherwise ineligible for a section 212(c) waiver at the time he pleaded guilty for the reasons set forth above. See Toia, supra, at 921 (stating that aliens who pleaded guilty prior to enactment of IMMACT may still apply for a section 212(c) waiver if they are otherwise eligible for such relief).
 
*2 The respondent also raises issues relating to the validity of his underlying criminal conviction, including allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. Even if we were to accept the respondent’s allegations, neither the Immigration Judge nor the Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction, unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to examine an alien’s claims regarding his guilt or innocence. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). In other words, we are bound by the conviction records and have no authority to retry the respondent’s criminal case. Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On July 9, 1986, the respondent was convicted of child molestation in violation of IND. CODE § 35-42-4-3(b) (1985). Further, the respondent concedes that this offense constitutes “sexual abuse of a minor” under section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act (Resp’t’s Br. at 6).

2007 WL 4707380 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WEI LY BOKEL A.K.A. WEILY L. BOKEL A.K.A. WEI-LY LIU A.K.A. WEILY LIU
FILE: AXX XX5 301 - ARLINGTON, VA
December 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(l) º - Crime involving moral turpitude (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination; cancellation of removal
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2007, decision ordering her removed from the United States to Brazil as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. The appeal will be sustained and the removal proceedings will be terminated.
 
The respondent is a native of Taiwan, a citizen of Taiwan and Brazil, and a lawful permanent resident of the United States. On December 7, 2004, she was convicted in the United States District Court for the Western District of Virginia of unlawfully attempting to remove a child from the United States with the intent to obstruct the lawful exercise of the parental rights of another in violation of 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a), the international parental kidnapping statute. As a result of this conviction, the respondent was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of 33 months.¿FN1º
 
On the basis of the aforementioned conviction, the Immigration Judge determined that the respondent is deportable from the United States pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (2000), as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony; to wit, an offense relating to “obstruction of justice” for which the term of imprisonment is at least 1 year. Section 101(a)(43)(S) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(S). In support of that determination, the Immigration Judge observed that because “parental rights” are defined by 18 U.S.C. § 1204(b)(2) as arising “by operation of law, court order, or legally binding agreement of the parties,” it necessarily follows that “¿oºbstructing parental rights ¿in violation of 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a)º obstructs justice of the Court.”
 
Whether the respondent’s offense of conviction qualifies as an “aggravated felony” under section 101(a)(43)(S) of the Act is a legal issue that this Board reviews de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2007).¿FN2º In determining whether an offense constitutes an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(S) of the Act, this Board considers that the obstruction of justice offenses listed in 18 U.S.C. §§ 1501-1518 provide “substantial guidance.., as to the contours of the ‘obstruction of justice’ category of offenses.” Matter of Espinoza, 22 I&N Dec. 889, 892, 894 n.4 (BIA 1999) (holding that misprision of a felony in violation of 18 U.S.C. § 4 does not qualify as an offense “relating to obstruction ofjustice” within the meaning of section 101(a)(43)(S)). In general, those offenses “have as an element interference with the proceedings of a tribunal or require an intent to harm or retaliate against others who cooperate in the process of justice or might otherwise so cooperate.” Id. at 892. As we have explained, moreover, “we do not believe that every offense that, by its nature, would tend to ‘obstruct justice’ is an offense that should properly be classified as ‘obstruction of justice”’ under section 101(a)(43)(S). Id. at 893-94. On the contrary, “¿tºo include all offenses that have a tendency to, or by their nature do, obstruct justice would cast the net too widely.” Id. at 894. With these principles in mind, we turn to the facts of the present case.
 
*2 When the respondent committed her offense the statute of conviction, 18 U.S.C. § 1204, provided as follows, in pertinent part:
  
§ 1204. International parental kidnapping
 
(a) Whoever removes a child from the United States, or attempts to do so, or retains a child (who has been in the United States) outside the United States with intent to obstruct the lawful exercise of parental rights shall be fined under this title or imprisoned not more than 3 years, or both.
 
(b) As used in this section—
(1) the term “child” means a person who has not attained the age of 16 years; and
 
(2) the term “parental rights”, with respect to a child, means the right to physical custody of the child—
 
(A) whether joint or sole (and includes visiting rights); and
 
(B) whether arising by operation of law, court order, or legally binding agreement of the parties.
 
 
We conclude that this offense does not qualify as one “relating to obstruction of justice” within the meaning of Matter of Espinoza, supra. Specifically, although the offense is obviously a very serious one, it is not defined by reference to elements requiring interference with the proceedings of a tribunal or the official duties of a law enforcement officer, nor does conviction for the offense require proof that the accused harmed or threatened a person who would otherwise have cooperated in the process ofjustice. And although the definition of “parental rights” at 18 U.S.C. § 1204(b)(2)(B) does make reference to rights of physical custody arising “by operation of law, court order, or legally binding agreement,” this is insufficient in our view to establish that the act of kidnapping necessarily “obstructs justice” in any meaningful sense. Specifically, a parent’s right to physical custody of his or her child need not be determined by a court or administrative tribunal in order to exist “by operation of law.” In New York, for example, a biological parent enjoys a presumptive right to physical custody of his or her children “by operation of law,” unless and until that right is legally terminated. See, e.g., In re Michael B., 80 N.Y.2d 299, 309 (N.Y. 1992). Consequently, an individual who intends to obstruct the “parental rights” of another within the meaning of 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a) does not necessarily intend to thereby disregard or frustrate the execution of any legal order or process.
 
The Immigration Judge’s characterization of the respondent’s offense as one “relating to obstruction of justice” is not without some legal support. We note, for example, that persons convicted under 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a) are sentenced pursuant to the federal “obstruction of justice” sentencing guideline. U.S.S.G. § 2J1.2. Furthermore, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has held that a violation of 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a) warranted imposition of a three-level enhancement under U.S.S.G. § 2J1.2(b)(2) for “substantial interference with the administration of justice” because “¿aºlthough the abduction did not interfere with an ongoing proceeding, ¿itº prevented proper legal proceedings from occurring by taking matters completely outside the purview of the administration of justice.” United States v. Amer, 110 F.3d 873, 885 (2d Cir.), cert. denied, 522 U.S. 904 (1997). Yet in our view such considerations merely reflect that particular violations of 18 U.S.C. § 1204(a) may “have a tendency to … obstruct justice”; they do not serve to establish that attempted international parental kidnapping can reasonably be considered an “offense relating to obstruction of justice” within the meaning of section 101(a)(43)(S).
 
*3 In light of the foregoing, we will reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision sustaining the aggravated felony charge against the respondent. In view of the fact that the Department of Homeland Security has not charged the respondent with deportability under any alternative ground, moreover, we will also terminate the removal proceedings.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the removal proceedings are terminated.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

United States v. Bokel, 185 Fed. Appx. 234 (4th Cir. 2006). On August 14, 2006, the District Court re-sentenced the respondent, and once again imposed a 33-month term of imprisonment. See Bokel v. United States, 2007 WL 1052482, at *2, *8 n.9 (W.D. Va. April 4, 2007) (slip opinion). Furthermore, the respondent’s habeas petition was denied by the District Court for insufficient evidence, id., and the respondent’s appeal from that denial has now been dismissed by the Fourth Circuit. United States v. Bokel, 2007 WL 2666794 (4th Cir., September 7, 2007).

2

We reach the merits despite the fact that the respondent’s handwritten notice of appeal contains a much less explicit challenge to the aggravated felony charge than she advanced before the Immigration Judge. The respondent is pro se on appeal and has attempted to articulate her full legal history to us as she understands it. Under the circumstances, we do not hold her to the same standards of clarity that we would require from a seasoned legal practitioner.

2007 WL 4699937 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO SALAZAR-CARMONA
FILE: AXX XX3 158 - SAN DIEGO, CA
December 13, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher J. Slender, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Donald W. Pearcy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on November 15, 2002, when we returned the record to the Immigration Court without further action because the respondent withdrew his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s May 21, 2002, decision finding the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), and ordering him removed from the United States as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s January 16, 2007, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen proceedings based upon ineffective assistance of counsel and an intervening change in the law. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
As an initial matter, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen was untimely.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23.
 
In 2001, the respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, and a lawful permanent resident, was convicted of unlawfully taking and driving a vehicle in violation of section 10851(a) of the California Vehicle Code and was sentenced to a 365 day term of imprisonment. Even assuming for the sake of discussion that the respondent’s motion had been timely filed, we conclude the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider because in 2002 she correctly relied upon Matter of V-Z-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 1338 (BIA 2000) (holding that the offense defined by section 10851(a) of the California Vehicle Code qualifies categorically as a “theft offense” under section 101 (a)(43)(G) of the Act). In other words, the respondent’s untimely motion before the Immigration Judge presented no legal argument, change of law, or overlooked argument or aspect of the case that would establish that, based on the law as it existed in 2002, the Immigration Judge erred in her prior conclusion. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006).
 
The respondent contends that he is eligible for equitable tolling because his previous counsel withdrew his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s May 21, 2002, decision and the respondent alleges he did not learn of the former counsel’s actions and ineffective representation until 2006. Respondent’s Brief at 2. We conclude that the respondent did not act with due diligence because he failed to demonstrate a steadfast pursuit of his case throughout proceedings before the agency. Cf. Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993 (9th Cir. 2007) (finding due diligence where alien was steadfast in pursuit of relief, including continual investigation and hiring two other attorneys, in unbroken efforts to retain competent counsel and file motion to reopen). Rather, the respondent delayed contacting current counsel to inquire about the status of his case until 2005, nearly 3 years after his previous counsel filed his appeal. In addition, the respondent has failed to adequately explain why he failed to investigate and pursue relief from removal during this time period.
 
*2 In the alternative, even if the respondent established due diligence, we cannot say that the previous counsel’s act of withdrawing the respondent’s appeal constituted ineffective assistance, given the state of the law at the time the appeal was withdrawn in 2002. See Matter of V-Z-S-, supra. We also note that the respondent has not complied with all of the procedural requirements for raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and the record does not show a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 5587-88 (9th Cir. 2006), and the cases cited therein.
 
The rationale in the Penuliar case cited by the respondent has been repudiated by the United States Supreme Court in Gonzales v. Duenas-Alvarez. See Penuliar v. Ashcroft, 395 F.3d 1037 (9th Cir. 2005), petition for reh’g denied, 435 F.3d 961 (9th Cir. 2006); Gonzales v. Duenas-Alvarez, 127 S. Ct. 815 (2007). Furthermore, the Ninth Circuit’s decision in United States v. Vidal, 504 F.3d 1072 (9th Cir. 2007) does not provide the respondent with plausible grounds for relief, since the respondent was not convicted under section 10851 (a) as an accessory after the fact. Rather, the respondent plead guilty to driving a motor vehicle and knowing it was stolen, which reflects that the respondent was convicted as a principal. Exhibit 2. The respondent, therefore, stands convicted of an aggravated felony, and his argument on appeal is foreclosed by Duenas-Alvarez, supra. Accordingly, the following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER; The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent concedes that his motion was untimely. Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 6, 14.

2007 WL 4699960 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTUR0 LOPEZ GUITIERREZ
FILE: AXX XX9 082 - LOS ANGELES, CA
December 12, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Acosta, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
 
CHARGE:
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 13, 2007, denying the respondent!s motion to reopen. An Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent deported in absentia for his failure to appear for the hearing on November 14, 1996. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge found that there was no good cause to toll the 180-day deadline for the entirety of the 10 year period that elapsed since the respondent received the Immigration Court’s in absentia order of deportation. He also determined that even if timely, the respondent failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear. Specifically, the Immigration Judge found that pursuant to the holding of the Ninth Circuit in Singh-Bhathal v. INS, 170 F.3d 943 (9th Cir, 1999), the respondcnt’s voluntary choice to follow the advice of an immigration consultant instead of the notice of bearing issued by the Immigration Court is not an exceptional circumstance. The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s request for sua sponte reopening.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that he has established exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear for the hearing on November 14, 1996, due to ineffective assistance of counsel. He also asserts that he was prejudiced by the actions of the immigration consultant that assisted him, and that he exercised due diligence in seeking to reopen his deportation proceedings.
 
We find that the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel, as he relied on the advice of an immigration consultant who falsely represented herself to be a lawyer, that it was not necessary for him to appear for his hearing. See Monjaraz-Munoz, 327 F.3d 892 (9th Cir. 2003) (if an alien fails to appear because of his actual and reasonable reliance on counsel’s erroneous advice, it can constitute a circumstance beyond the alien’s control); see also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999)(the statute of limitations to reopen an order of deportation is equitably tolled where the alien’s late petition is the result of deceptive actions by a representative); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
However, we find that the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel was filed untimely, even if equitable tolling is taken into consideration. See I.J. Dec. dated April 12, 2007. In Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2002), the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals determined that the limitation period on motions to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be tolled until the alien was aware of the harm resulting from his two former attorney’s misconduct. While equitable tolling is available for untimely motions to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent failed to exercise due diligence in pursing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See e.g. RodriguezLariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir.); Socop-Gonzalez, 272 F.3d 1176; Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). As noted by the Immigration Judge, according to the respondent’s own declaration, he received the in absentia deportation order in November 1996, and by January 1997, the respondent was fully aware that Ms. Dorantes had defrauded him because he learned that she was being investigated by authorities, and that criminal charges had been brought against her. See Respondcnt’s Declaration, dated March 1, 2007. The respondent claims that since March 1997, he has consulted with three different attorneys, all of whom informed him that he did not have a case and he would not be able to reopen his proceedings. However, the respondent states that prior to meeting with his current counsel in August 2006, he last met with an attorney in late 2003. In his declaration, the respondent avers that if he had known there was something he could do, such as filing a motion, he would have done it in a beartbeat because he wanted to have a meaningful day in court.
 
*2 The respondent has failed to establish that he acted with due diligence required for equitable tolling. The respondent concedes that in November 1996, he received the in absentia order of deportation from the Los Angeles Immigration Court. The respondent was informed that the Immigration Judge’s decision was final unless a motion to reopen is filed with the Immigration Court in accordance with Section 242B(c)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252B(c)(3), and the respondent was provided with the address of the Los Angeles Immigration Court. Yet the respondent never inquired with the Immigration Court after receiving the in absentia order of deportation in November 1996, to pursue reopening through filing a motion to reopen with the court. Moreover, the respondent did not seek out any counsel regarding his in absentia order of deportation from late 2003 until August 2006, at which time he met with current counsel. Even if we accept that prior counsel was ineffective and failed to advise the respondent that he was required to attand the hearing on November 14, 1996, the respondcnt’s assertions of ineffective assistance of counsel cannot be overcome by the lack of respondcnt’s due diligence in pursuing his case. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: IRAKLI MIKIASHVILI TAMAR KAPANADZE LIA MIKIASHVILI NINO MIKIASHVILI
FILES: AXX XX1 315 - NEWARK, NJ
AXX XX1 311
AXX XX1 312
AXX XX1 313
December 11, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vladislav Sirota, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º -Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact (AXX XXX XXX & AXX XXX XXX)
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents, natives and citizens of Georgia, have timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s September 24, 2007, decision. The respondents contend that the Immigration Judge erred in denying their untimely motion to reopen. The respondents request that we stay their removal from the United States. The stay request will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
We adopt the procedural history set forth in the Immigration Judge’s September 24, 2007, decision. As set forth in the Immigration Judge’s order, in addition to the respondents’ most recent motion to reopen, the respondents previously filed two other motions to reopen. Inasmuch as the respondents are limited, with certain exceptions, to filing one motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondents’ motion is number-barred and should be denied on that basis. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
The respondents also contend that the proceedings should be reopened because of their prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. The respondents contend that they have complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998). In Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. However, as set forth in the Immigration Judge’s order, the respondents failed to submit an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with their prior counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondents in this regard. Inasmuch as the respondents failed to include an affidavit as required, we agree with the Immigration Judge that they failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 Lastly, the respondents argue that proceedings should be reopened so that the lead respondent may apply for asylum due to his lengthy absence from Georgia and involuntary renunciation of his Georgian citizenship. However, the regulations concerning motions to reopen before an Immigration Judge only waive the time and numerical limitations for motions to reopen based on “changed country conditions arising in the country of nationality” not personal circumstances as described in the respondents’ motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(i). Based on the foregoing, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the respondents’ motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondents’ request for a stay of removal is denied.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4707541 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAE SUN KO
FILE: AXX XX7 130 - SAN ANTONIO, TX
December 10, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se¿FN1º
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On September 19, 2007, the respondent filed this motion to reopen our February 12, 1988, decision dismissing his appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(e)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days atter the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. In the instant case, the respondent’s motion to reopen would have been due on or before September 30, 1996. Hence, the respondent filed this motion to reopen out of time. We will deny the untimely motion to reopen.
 
This case was last before the Board on June 3, 1993, when we denied a previous motion to reopen in which the respondent sought of voluntary departure, or, in the alternative, reopening to apply for suspension of deportation. The previous motion to reopen was premised on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel during the deportation proceedings. The instant motion is, in substance, virtually identical to the previous motion, except that the respondent now requests reopening to enable him to apply for adjustment of status based on an approved visa petition (Form I-360).
 
Initially, we note that while the respondent reiterates the very same ineffective assistance of counsel claim put froth in the previous motion, he has put forth neither arguments nor evidence to account for the untimeliness of the instant motion. The Board, however, retains limited discretionary power to reopen proceedings sua sponte under 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(a). See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (discussing the Board’s limited authority to reopen proceedings sua sponte). Nevertheless, for the following reasons, we refrain from exercising that power in this case.
 
First, the Board has already considered and rejected the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. On November 1, 1983, an Immigration Judge found the respondent deportable as charged, but granted him the privilege of voluntary departure with an alternative order of deportation to Korea. The respondent did not appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision. However, the respondent failed to depart the United States on or before March 1, 1984, pursuant to the grant of voluntary departure, and he failed to present himself for deportation on October 10, 1985, pursuant to a “bag and baggage” letter from the former Immigration and Naturalization Service. In the instant motion to reopen, as in the previous motion to reopen, the respondent claims that he unknowingly failed to depart the United States as required due to ineffective assistance of counsel. In our previous decision, we rejected that claim as frivolous and dilatory. Now, more than 14 years after our June 3, 1993, decision, the respondent reiterates the same ineffective assistance of counsel claim in the instant motion. As a motion to reopen is not an opportunity to repeat previously considered and rejected arguments, we will not revisit the ineffective assistance of counsel claim at this time. See INS v. Jong Ha Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N 399, 402 (BIA 1991).
 
*2 Second, this case does not warrant reopening sua sponte to enable the respondent to apply for adjustment of status under section 245(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). See Matter of J-J-, supra. The respondent failed to comply with the terms of a grant of voluntary departure, and he has been subject to a final deportation order entered on March 1, 1984. He failed to present himself for deportation on October 10, 1985, pursuant to a “bag and baggage letter,” and he has remained in the United States in violation of immigration laws for more than 23 years, during which time he abused the immigration process by filing frivolous motions before the Immigration Judge and the Board. Regardless of statutory eligibility for adjustment of status, accrued equities, or potential hardship, the respondent, who has shown utter disregard for the immigration laws of the United States, does not merit a favorable exercise of discretion. See Giri v. Keisler, 2007 WL 3276110 (5th Cir. 2007) (extending the fugitive disentitlement doctrine, which limits a criminal defendant’s access to the judicial system whose authority he evades, to the immigration context where the petitioners are fugitive aliens who have evaded custody and failed to comply with a removal order); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 257 (BIA 1985) (finding that an alien in immigration proceedings who fails to report for deportation does not merit the favorable exercise of discretion required for reopening). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied. The respondent’s request for a stay of deportation is denied.
 
The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent is therefore considered to be proceeding pro se. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(1).

2007 WL 4699891 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOHORDY CANTO-PEREZ
FILE: AXX XX5 221 - FISHKILL, NY
December 10, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, is a lawful permanent resident of the United States. As a result of his October 24, 1991, conviction for manslaughter in the first degree, for which he was sentenced to 7 to 21 years’ incarceration, he was placed in deportation proceedings. By a decision dated July 20, 1995, an Immigration Judge found the respondent to be deportable as charged, found he had abandoned his application for a waiver pursuant to section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), and ordered him to be deported to the Dominican Republic. The respondent did not file a timely appeal. Subsequently, on April 25, 2005, the respondent filed a special motion with the Immigration Judge, again seeking a waiver pursuant to section 212(c) of the Act in light of INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). On June 8, 2005, the Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding the respondent to be statutorily ineligible for that relief. By a decision dated September 29, 2005, this Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision of June 8, 2005. Thereafter the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board on November 28, 2005, contending that prior counsel had provided ineffective assistance. The Board denied the motion on February 13, 2006, finding that the respondent had failed to comply with the requirements for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
On March 8, 2007, the record was remanded from the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit. While finding that the Board did not abuse its discretion in denying the motion to reopen with regard to the ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the court noted that the Board had not addressed the respondent’s contention in the addendum to his motion dated January 14, 2006, that the Immigration Judge improperly considered statements of a corrections officer and consequently ordered the respondent deported for failure to attend his deportation heating.¿FN1º
 
It is the respondent’s contention that the Immigration Judge improperly considered statements of a corrections officer at the facility at which the respondent was incarcerated, to the effect that the respondent refused to attend his deportation hearing because the respondent had not been permitted to shower before the hearing. The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in determining that the respondent had abandoned his claim for failure to attend his hearing. It is the respondent’s contention that his case should be remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of his application for section 212(c) relief.
 
*2 The respondent’s hearing on his application for section 212(c) relief was scheduled to be held on July 20, 1995, at the facility at which he was incarcerated. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent failed to appear and that the Immigration Judge had been advised by a corrections officer at the facility that the respondent refused to appear unless he were permitted to take a shower first, which privilege had apparently been denied to him by the prison authorities. The Immigration Judge then proceeded to deem the respondent’s application for relief abandoned (Tr. 61-71). The respondent was represented by counsel at the July 20, 1995, hearing, and on July 27, 1995, counsel was provided with a July 20, 1995, letter from the corrections officer describing the respondent’s refusal to attend the hearing.
 
Motions to reopen proceedings are disfavored and this Board has “broad discretion” to grant or deny such motions. INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314, 322-23 (1992); INS v. Rios-Pineda, 471 U.S. 444, 449-50 (1985). Thus, a motion to reopen under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) will not be granted unless the movant establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). Even if a prima facie case for relief is made out, the Board may deny the motion in discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
In view of the respondent’s refusal to appear at the hearing, at which he was represented by counsel, we find no error on the part of the Immigration Judge in proceeding in his absence. The respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge acted improperly or that he was prejudiced by the Immigration Judge’s conduct of the hearing. We also note that the respondent, who was represented by counsel, did not timely appeal this action, and has not addressed either his failure to submit a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s determination or provided an explanation as to why he did not submit a timely motion regarding this claim. Rather, the respondent waited for more than 10 years after his refusal to attend the hearing to raise this issue. Accordingly, as we find no error by the Immigration Judge, and in view of the respondent’s unexplained and excessive delay in raising this issue, reopening is not warranted.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

The addendum to the motion was not included in the record before the Board at the time of the February 13, 2006, decision.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IVANIA ZOMORA SOTELO
FILE: AXX XX7 971 - NEW YORK, NY
December 7, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Wishnie, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1252(a)(2)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
The respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s August 23, 2007, decision denying her motion to reconsider the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2007, denial of her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order entered against her on June 5, 1997, will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nicaragua, was originally placed into these proceedings with the issuance of an Order to Show Cause on July 24, 1990 (Exh. 1). According to the record, a Notice of Appearance was filed on the respondent’s behalf by an accredited representative on June 25, 1992. On July 28, 1993, the respondent, through her accredited representative, conceded deportability as charged and accepted voluntary departure for a year or until July 27, 1994. Subsequently, after failing to depart, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge seeking suspension of deportation under NACARA. On October 15, 1996, over the objection of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
The record reflects that a Notice of Appearance was filed on behalf of the respondent by an attorney on September 6, 1996. On October 22, 1996, the respondent’s counsel was sent a Notice of Hearing by certified mailing notifying the respondent of a hearing scheduled on her application for suspension of deportation on February 13, 1997. After the respondent failed to appear at this scheduled heating, her case was postponed until June 5, 1997. On April 3, 1997, the respondent’s counsel was sent by certified mail a Notice of Hearing for June 5, 1997, and a signed return receipt was received by the Immigration Court. However, the respondent again failed to appear and an in absentia order, of removal was entered against her on that date. Subsequently, 10 years later, the respondent, through new counsel, filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order on June 20, 2007, arguing that she did not receive proper notice of her prior June 5, 1997, hearing and also arguing changed country conditions in Nicaragua affecting her claim. According to the respondent, no notice of hearing was sent to her address.
 
On June 27, 2007, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind her in absentia order of removal (I.J. at 1-2). The Immigration Judge noted the respondent’s appearance at her July 28, 1993, hearing and the grant of voluntary departure (I.J. at 1-2). In regard to the respondent’s scheduled June 5, 1997, hearing in relation to the grant of her motion to reopen, the Immigration Judge noted the inclusion in the record of the Notice of Hearing for the June 5, 1997, heating sent to respondent’s counsel and returned with a signed return receipt (I.J. at 2). The Immigration Judge also noted the respondent’s admission as to having departed the United States on February 15, 1997, after the filing of her motion to reopen but prior to the hearing (I.J. at 2). The respondent remained outside of the United States until 2001 (I.J. at 2). As a result, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent’s motion was untimely and that she did not have jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (2007), which bars the filing of a motion to reopen on behalf of a person subject to proceedings subsequent to departure from the United States (I.J. at 3-4). The Immigration Judge also noted that she would have denied the motion in discretion given the respondent’s previous immigration history (I.J. at 3-4).
 
*2 On July 23, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge contending for the first time that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel alleging that her attorney failed to notify her of the scheduled hearings and the possibility of an in absentia order. See Respondent’s Motion at 2. The respondent further alleged that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that her motion was untimely filed because of the ineffective assistance of counsel and that she was without jurisdiction because, the respondent argued, she did not depart the United States while she was subject to a final order of removal inasmuch as the motion to reopen had already been granted. Id. at 3 (emphasis added). The respondent alleged that she was facially eligible, for suspension of deportation. The DHS opposed the respondent’s motion arguing that the Immigration Judge had no jurisdiction under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1), and that the respondent failed to show due diligence in regard to her ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The DHS conceded that the Immigration Judge erred in finding that she did not have jurisdiction as found in her decision because the motion to reopen had already been granted.
 
On August 23, 2007, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider noting the DHS’s concession but finding that she had no jurisdiction under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) because the respondent departed the United States prior to the hearing on her motion which bad already been granted. The Immigration Judge also found that, while the respondent substantially complied with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), she did not show due diligence in pursuing her claim. On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in applying 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) to her case because she was not under a final order of removal at the time she departed. In opposition, the DHS contends that her motion is untimely and also alleges that the respondent has another outstanding order of removal entered against her in absentia and was required to surrender for deportation on February 14, 2002, but did not. According to the DHS, the respondent is in separate proceedings under a different alien number because she reentered the United States in 2001 under a false name.
 
Initially, we find that the Immigration Judge misapplied 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) to the respondent’s case to find that she did not have jurisdiction. The record reflects that the respondent was inside the United States when her attorney filed the motion to reopen on her behalf on September 26, 1996; she did not depart until February 11, 1997, just 2 days prior to her scheduled February 13, 1997, initial hearing date. Thus, the filing bar in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) is not applicable to the respondent’s case.
 
*3 However, we agree that the respondent has failed to establish her burden for reopening and rescission and further find that she has failed to sustain her burden of proof in establishing her ineffective assistance of counsel claim. As previously discussed, the respondent did not file her motion until 10 years after the in absentia order was entered against her. In this regard, she now claims that her motion should be granted because her former attorney, who represented her during her motion, failed to notify her of the hearing date and, on this basis, asserts that she has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As we understand the respondent’s contentions, she argues both a lack of notice and that any applicable filing deadline for her motion should be excused because she has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent predicates her lack of notice argument upon her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and, thus, these arguments in this case are inextricably intertwined.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has determined that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). In order to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in this way, an alien must demonstrate not only that her constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that she has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case. Id. Here, we find that the long delay in filing the motion to reopen is not reasonably explained by the circumstances of this case. The evidence of record indicates that the respondent hired counsel to file her motion to reopen. Contra, respondent’s July 19, 2007 declaration in support of motion to reconsider, (¶ 13), claiming she was never told her case had been reopened, only that her attorney did not thereafter notify her of the bearing date. In this regard, the respondent has failed to provide any reasonable basis as to why, during a 10-year span, she did not contact her counsel to find out when a hearing had been scheduled or why she waited 10 years to learn of the outcome of those proceedings. In this regard, the respondent’s claim that she was not notified of the hearing date is not reasonable inasmuch as she was aware that her motion was granted and failed to take any action for 10 years.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699895 (BIA)

IN RE: MARIA LOURDES BARBARA SOLOMON FLORES A.K.A MARIA LOURDES BARBARA FLORES A.K.A. MARIA LOURDES BARBARA FURY
FILE: AXX XX6 529 - BALTIMORE, MD
December 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jon Eric Garde, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leslie E. Smith Turner
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 22, 2006, when we affirmed an Immigration Judge’s April 8, 2005, decision denying the respondent’s motion to continue the proceedings pending the adjudication of a visa petition filed on her behalf. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as a matter of discretion based, in part, on her finding that the respondent attempted to conceal the fact that she had participated in fraud to procure a visa to enter the United States. See I.J. at 12-13. See also Onyeme v. U.S. I.N.S., 146 F.3d 227, 231 (4th Cir. 1998) (whether to grant a motion to continue deportation proceedings is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. On July 2, 2007, the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit declared that the Immigration Judge’s refusal to grant the continuance was not an abuse of discretion, and denied the respondent’s petition for review of our decision. Flores v. Gonzales, 231 Fed.Appx. 283 (4th Cir. 2007) (unpublished). The respondent’s now moves to reopen our August 22, 2006, decision based on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(c). See also Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Although the Board has no record of receiving the motion prior to October 9, 2007, the respondent’s attorney asserts that the motion was timely filed on August 20, 2006, and he has submitted documentation in support thereof. We will deem the motion to have been timely filed. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent attempted to conceal pertinent facts was based on an adverse credibility determination. The respondent now reiterates her appellate arguments that she was confused during the removal hearings, and that she did not knowingly participate in fraud in procuring a visa. The Board rejected these arguments when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision, and we will not revisit them at this time. See INS v. Jong Ha Wang, 450 U.S. 139, 141 (1981) (discussing motions to reopen). The respondent further attempts to blame her former attorney for allegedly failing to prepare her adequately to testify at the removal hearings. It is not alleged by the respondent, and there is no basis to determine, however, that her former attorney advised her to conceal facts from the Immigration Judge. We emphasize that the respondent was placed under oath and that she swore to tell the truth during the removal hearings. See Tr. at 12. The respondent’s current explanations to account for the attempted concealment of facts fail to overcome her obligation to testify truthfully during the removal hearings. We reject the respondent’s efforts to shift the blame for her own actions to her former attorney. Indeed, we find no prejudicial error on the part of the respondent’s former attorney. See Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76 (4th Cir. 1989) (To prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation proceeding, an alien must show not only ineffective representation, but also prejudice to him which occurred as a result of that ineffectiveness). The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. The request for a stay of removal is also denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    1  On Reconsideration IN RE: ETZHAK NIMNI, BIA, February 29, 2008
2007 WL 4699742 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ETZHAK NIMNI
FILE: AXX XX3 973 - EL PASO, TX
December 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victoria Calle, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark J. Wurtz
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on June 29, 1999, because he failed to appear for his hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on August 15, 2007, but the Immigration Judge denied it on September 13, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues On appeal that he was not adequately notified of the hearing. The record reflects that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to the respondent’s former counsel, Bernard Rosenbloom. A copy was also mailed to the respondent at his last known address, 527 Madison Avenue, No. 2, Elizabeth, NJ 07201. The respondent denies having received the hearing notice from either the Immigration Court or his counsel. A Notice of Hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) and Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (1985). We further consider that the hearing notice was sent to an address provided on the Form I-830 and stated on counsel’s motion to withdraw filed on May 18, 1999. There is also no indication that the post office returned the notice as undeliverable. The respondent has further not denied the correctness of the address and has not alleged that he was not residing at that location when the notice was mailed. He also does not allege any problems with mail delivery at his home. In addition, the respondent was aware that he was in removal proceedings and apparently made no attempt to verify his immigration status until 2003. His reliance on the instruction of another alien that it often takes years to obtain a hearing date is not a valid excuse for failing to do so.
 
We have considered the totality of the evidence presented, and we cannot find based on the respondent’s denial of receipt that he has rebuted the presumption of delivery of the hearing notice. Maknojiya v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 588, 589 (5th Cir.2005) (noting the lower burden required for rebutting the presumption of delivery for correspondence sent by regular mail was met where both alien and his counsel submitted affidavits indicating that they did not receive the notice that reset the hearing from an earlier hearing date than originally scheduled). The notice provided in these proceedings was therefore adequate because it was received by former counsel and mailed to the respondent at his correct address. This aspect of the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
The respondent further argues that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and therefore exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance for his hearing. He indicates in his affidavit that former counsel Bernard Rosenbloom did not notify him of his hearing, and told him that venue in these proceedings would be changed to New Jersey. He further indicates that Mr. Rosenbloom instructed him to wait for his next hearing date, and presumably did not inform the respondent that he had withdrawn as his representative. The respondent further indicates that he did not know until September 2003 that he was ordered removed from the United States in absentia. He also indicates that a second attorney negligently delayed his submission of his motion to reopen.
 
*2 A request for rescission of an order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Even if we equitably tolled the 180-day period for seeking reopening, we cannot find that the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for establishing attorney negligence. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383 (5th Cir. 2001); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The record reflects that a complaint has been filed against Mr. Rosenbloom with a Texas disciplinary authority. However, there was no showing that Mr. Rosenbloom was notified of the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel raised herein and allowed an opportunity to respond. We cannot find based solely on the respondent’s accounts that he was the victim of attorney negligence without a showing that his attorney was given an opportunity to provide his rendition of the facts. The respondent has therefore failed to establish that he was the victim of attorney negligence, and we cannot find that exceptional circumstances prevented his appearance for the hearing. We therefore need not address whether the respondent’s second attorney negligently delayed the submission of the motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699742 (BIA)
End of Document
