Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GONZALO EMILIO TAVERA-URIBE
FILE: AXX XX1 337 - MIAMI, FL
December 6, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mayra Joli, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Theresa M. Scala
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.8.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(1)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, was ordered removed from the United States on October 10, 2006, because he failed to appear for a hearing. On March 30, 2007, he filed a motion to reopen alleging that he did not receive the Notice of Hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on May 16, 2007. On May 18, 2007, the respondent requested reconsideration before the Immigration Judge, indicating that he had filed a complaint against his prior counsel and that his prior counsel’s negligence caused his failure to appear for the hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on June 21, 2007. The respondent appealed from the Immigration Judge’s June 21, 2007, decision and requested reopening of proceedings based on the criminal court’s August 6, 2007, order granting his motion to vacate his plea on the charge of possession of cannabis. On August 29, 2007, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his request for reopening because it was unclear whether his conviction had been vacated for substantive reasons and, even if it was vacated for substantive reasons, he remained convicted of attempted burglary of a structure, which rendered him removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I). The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider our August 29, 2007, decision. The respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
In his motion to reconsider, the respondent argues that we erred in dismissing his appeal. Specifically, the respondent contends that he established that he did not receive notice of his original hearing and that his prior counsel was negligent by failing to submit a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative (Form G-28) before the Immigration Court. The respondent further contends that proceedings should be reopened because his conviction for attempted burglary of a structure has been recently reopened.
 
We find that the respondent’s motion to reconsider fails to identify any material error of fact or law in our prior decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (stating that a motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Instead, the motion to reconsider reiterates many of the arguments presented by the respondent on appeal. Specifically, the respondent’s arguments regarding notice of his hearing and his prior counsel’s negligence were raised on appeal. As we considered these arguments in rendering our prior decision, we decline to revisit them. The remaining arguments presented by the respondent do not establish that we overlooked any material issue in rendering our prior decision or that we otherwise erred in dismissing his appeal and denying his request for reopening. Specifically, we reject the respondent’s argument that we erred in our prior decision by finding that it was unclear if he had notified his prior counsel that he had accused him of ineffective assistance of counsel. In this regard, we note that, in our prior decision, we found that, even if the procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel were established, reopening was not warranted because the respondent failed to establish that his prior counsel was negligent.
 
*2 To the extent that the respondent argues that proceedings should be reopened because his conviction for attempted burglary of a structure has been reopened by the criminal court, we find that reopening is not warranted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent is not asking us to give effect to an actual court order vacating his criminal conviction. Instead, the respondent seeks to have his removal proceedings reopened and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge only to have the proceedings held in abeyance pending what he hopes is a favorable disposition of the collateral attack on his conviction in the criminal court. Collateral attacks on convictions or requests for post-conviction relief do not prevent a conviction from being final for immigration purposes unless relief is granted. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996).
 
Finally, to the extent that the respondent seeks to have us reopen proceedings sua sponte, we do not find that reopening on our own motion is warranted in the present matter. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen eases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Chris Grant
FOR THE BOARD

*1 On May 4, 2007, the Board affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The respondent, who is from China, filed a motion to reopen on July 13, 2007. The motion, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be granted, and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The motion to reopen presented to the Immigration Court claimed that the respondent did not appear at his July 5, 2006, removal hearing because a former attorney had told him that he had filed a motion seeking to change venue and the respondent did not have to appear at the scheduled hearing in Texas. In affirming the decision of the Immigration denying reopening, the Board considered the respondent’s contention that the attorney told him that he did not need to attend the scheduled hearing. However, the Board determined, the respondent failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. That is, although the respondent had submitted an affidavit with his motion, he failed to submit evidence that he had filed a disciplinary complaint against prior counsel, or informed prior counsel of his allegations.
 
With the motion to reopen, the respondent presents evidence that he has now filed a disciplinary complaint against prior counsel, and has notified prior counsel of his claims. Former counsel responded, and disputes that he told the respondent that he did not need to attend the hearing.
 
Under the circumstances, we find it appropriate to allow the respondent an opportunity for a hearing.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s order of May 4, 2007, is rescinded.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Frederick D. Hess

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Order Vacated on Reconsideration by IN RE: EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORLAS A.K.A. EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORALES, BIA, January 18, 2008
2007 WL 4699736 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDUARDO ALFREDO VERA-MORLAS
FILE: AXX XX8 057 - LANCASTER, CA
November 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Acosta, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela M. Fontova
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was previously before us on July 12, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 28, 2007, which denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, filed this motion to reopen and to terminate removal proceedings on October 9, 2007. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen and terminate proceedings because the DHS never established his removability by clear and convincing evidence, and that the failure of his attorney, Edgardo Quintanilla, to raise the issue of removability on appeal constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 5-11, Exh. C. With respect to attorney Quintanilla, the motion appears to substantially comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 824-25 (9th Cir. 2003). However, we do not find that the respondent has established the requisite prejudice necessary to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel from attorney Quintanilla to warrant reopening. See Serrano v. Gonzales, 469 F.3d 1317, 1319 (9th Cir. 2007).
 
We agree with the respondent that it is the DHS’s burden to establish that an alien is removable. See section 240(c)(3)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a). However, removability may be established by admission of the respondent. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(a). In this case, during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through his counsel at that time, attorney Daniel Hanlon, admitted all of the factual allegations and conceded removability as charged in the Notice to Appear (Exh. 1; Tr. at 13, 18). Such an admission by the respondent’s attorney, acting in his professional capacity during the respondent’s removal hearing, is binding on the respondent, and he is not permitted to challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from that admission. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986). See also U.S. v. Davis, 332 F.3d 1163, 1168 (9th Cir. 2003) (factual stipulations may be treated as judicial admissions, which have the effect of withdrawing a fact from issue and dispensing wholly with the need for proof of the fact, and are binding on both the parties and the court). In this motion, the respondent has not demonstrated that, if attorney Quintanilla had raised the issue of removability before us on appeal, the outcome would have been different, i.e., we would have found that the respondent was not bound by his attorney Hanlon’s admissions on the record and that he was not removable. See Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950, 955 (9th Cir. 2003). Although the respondent states that he did not feel that attorney Hanlon adequately represented him because he did not challenge removability, see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Exh. C, we emphasize that the current motion does not comply at all with Matter of Lozada, supra, with respect to attorney Hanlon.¿FN1º Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In the brief on appeal to this Board, the respondent, through attorney Quintanilla, argued that attorney Hanlon provided ineffective assistance when he failed to present to the Immigration Judge the argument that the respondent’s alcohol and drug use was a disease. See Respondent’s Brief at 6-9. We rejected this argument in our July 12, 2007, decision on appeal, noting that tactical decisions in the preparation and presentation of an alien’s case did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel, and citing Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 934 (9th Cir. 1986).

2007 WL 4707408 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RONALD ERIC DELHAYE
FILE: AXX XX7 240 - FLORENCE, AZ
November 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Run A. Kamran, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maria N. Bjornerud
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before this Board on July 27, 2007, when we sustained the appeal of the DHS from the Immigration Judge’s grant of a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1182(c), in the exercise of discretion, denied the application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act in the exercise of discretion, and vacated the grant of section 212(c) relief by the Immigration Judge. The respondent, through new counsel, has now filed a motion to reopen alleging that his former counsel, who represented him before the Immigration Judge, was ineffective in his representation because he failed to adequately prepare and present the respondent’s case. The contention of present counsel that former counsel was ineffective is wholly without factual or legal merit.
 
The instant motion does comply with the requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth by this Board in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). However, the instant motion does not establish that his former counsel was ineffective in representing him at his hearing before the Immigration Judge. Respondent’s present counsel asserts that respondent’s former counsel was ineffective because he did not present the testimony of available witnesses, because he did not present the testimony of an expert witness, and because he did not object to questioning by the Immigration Judge which present counsel argues concerned immaterial matters.
 
The Department of Homeland Security, Immigration and Customs Enforcement (“DHS”), correctly notes in opposition to the respondent’s motion, that former counsel presented affidavits executed by the available witnesses and informed the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s spouse would testify to facts consistent with her affidavit. The DHS also notes that respondent’s former counsel did submit a recommendation letter from a clinical psychologist in support of the respondent’s claims of rehabilitation. Finally, the DHS also correctly asserts that the questioning of the respondent by the Immigration Judge concerning the respondent’s sexual practices is relevant to a determination of whether a favorable exercise of discretion is warranted where concerns in the case arise due to the respondent’s sexual abuse of a child.
 
Ineffective assistance of counsel in a removal proceedings is a denial of due process if the proceeding was so fundamentally tmfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case. See Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). In any case presenting an ineffectiveness claim, the performance inquiry must be whether counsel’s assistance was reasonable considering all the circumstances. See Strickland v. Washington, 466 U.S. 668, 688 (1984). Methods of case presentation are within counsel’s professional judgment and are afforded great deference. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 688-89. Strategic decisions, such as whether to call a particular witness at the hearing, rest upon the sound professional judgment of the lawyer.  Gustave v. United States, 627 F.2d 901, 904 (9th Cir. 1980). Such appropriately informed strategic decisions are virtually unchallengeable. See Strickland v. Washington, supra at 690.
 
*2 The assertion by respondent’s present counsel that presenting witnesses likely would have altered the outcome of the instant case is not fact but opinion. On the contrary, the presentation of witness testimony also carries with it the right of cross-examination which may result in the identification of additional adverse information or reason to discount the favorable information in a statement or affidavit that would otherwise go unchallenged. The decision in this matter to rely upon certain written statements and affidavits rather than presenting testimony subject to cross-examination is precisely the kind of informed strategic decision that rests upon the sound professional judgment of an attorney. Similarly, respondent’s former counsel had nothing to gain on behalf of the respondent by objecting to questioning by the Immigration Judge that the Immigration Judge found relevant to whether he was going to grant section 212(c) relief in the exercise of his discretion. Indeed, while information was elicited by the Immigration Judge’s questioning which concerned this Board, the Immigration Judge determined by his questioning that, in his opinion, the respondent was deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion.
 
The judgments of former counsel criticized by present counsel are the kind of strategic decisions that are virtually unchallengeable. In this matter, former counsel’s strategic decisions in the presentation of the respondent’s case initially resulted in a favorable determination. We do not find that those strategic decisions represent an attorney performing below the minimum standards of competence which the respondent had a right to expect. This is not an instance where former counsel failed to meet a deadline or submit an application, nor is this an instance where former counsel did not reasonably prepare for the hearing or present any evidence on the respondent’s behalf. Rather, this is an instance where present counsel is second-guessing former counsel’s method of case presentation and strategic decisions, out of a belief, which may not be accurate, that his own methods would not only have resulted in success before the Immigration Judge but also before this Board. As such, in the absence of “egregious circumstances,” an alien is bound by the “reasonable tactical decisions of his counsel.” See Matter of Gawaran, 20 I&N Dec. 938, 942 (BIA 1995), citing Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 383 (BIA 1986); see also Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931, 933-34 (9th Cir. 1986). The respondent was not prevented from reasonably presenting his case that he was deserving of a favorable exercise of discretion. Accordingly, the instant motion to reopen is denied because the motion does not establish that respondent’s former counsel was ineffective.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4707408 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICIA LARA
FILE: AXX XX4 815 - SAN DIEGO, CA
November 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chris A. Chouteau, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Olga El Bagdadi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Remand
 
The respondent appeals the October 5, 2006, decision of the Immigration Judge ordering her removed to Mexico and has filed a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion to remand. The motion to remand will be granted.
 
The respondent moves to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel. To warrant such a remand, the respondent must comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and demonstrate that she was prejudiced by her former counsel’s deficient performance. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent has sufficiently complied with the Lozada requirements, including having given her former counsel an opportunity to respond to the allegations and having filed a complaint with the State Bar of California. The respondent argues that her former counsel admitted the factual allegations and conceded removability without the respondent’s knowledge or consent, despite being told by the respondent before the hearing that she had not known that the passengers in her car were not in possession of legal documents to enter or reside in the United States.
 
The respondent submits that her former counsel did not meet in advance with her to prepare for the hearings, did not adequately review and discuss with her the facts of the case, and failed to take into account the respondent’s statement to him minutes before her first hearing that she had not known that the passengers in her car were not in possession of legal documents to enter the United States. The documents in the record support the respondent’s claim that she lacked knowledge that her passengers did not have valid documents (Exhs. 4, 6). By admitting on behalf of the respondent the charge of alien smuggling, the respondent’s former counsel waived the defense of lack of knowledge. This amounts to deficient representation.
 
The Immigration Judge based his finding of removability on the admissions and concessions that the respondent’s former counsel made on her behalf. We therefore conclude that the respondent was prejudiced by her former counsel’s deficient performance. Under these circumstances, we conclude that a new hearing is warranted. We will therefore grant the respondent’s motion to remand.
 
ORDER: The motion to remand is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4699735 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON ARCADIO GUTIERREZ-CASTILLO
FILE: AXX XX5 617 - BOSTON, MA
November 30, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert J. Caron, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 20, 2001. The respondent, who is from the Dominican Republic, filed the pending motion to reopen on October 3, 2007. The motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent was convicted in 1980 in New York of assault in the first degree, and was sentenced to incarceration for 6-18 years. The respondent spent 11 years in prison as a result of this conviction (Tr. at 53). The Immigration Judge concluded that, after passage of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-546 (“IIRIRA”), the respondent was ineligible for relief under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and the Board affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from Randy Olen, who represented him before the Immigration Judge and on appeal to the Board. The respondent claims that Olen erred in not filing a brief with the Board contending that he should be able to apply for 2120(h) relief. Equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence. Chen v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 151, 154 (1st Cir. 2005); Jobe v. 1NS, 238 F.3d 96, 98-99 (1st Cir. 2001). The Board notes that the appeal was filed on December 12, 1996, and according to their affidavits neither the respondent nor Olen made any effort to check the status of the appeal until August, 2007, almost 11 years later. However, the Board also acknowledges that its August 20, 2001, decision was sent to an incorrect address for Olen.
 
Even tolling the motions deadline in this case, the motion is denied for failure to show prejudice resulting from the alleged failures of prior counsel. Zeru v. Gonzales, 503 F.3d 59, 72 (1st Cir. 2007); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The respondent contends that he should have been able to seek relief under section 212(h) of the Act, notwithstanding the passage of IIRIRA, as he was eligible for such relief at the time deportation proceedings were instituted in 1994, and proceedings were delayed while the now-DHS considered an I-130 visa petition filed on his behalf. However, the respondent is statutorily precluded from a section 212(h) waiver of inadmissibility as an alien who has been admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident who has been convicted of an aggravated felony. See Matter of Yeung, 21 I&N Dec. 610 (BIA 1996) (finding that IIRIRA’s amendments to section 212(h) relief, which make a lawful permanent resident alien convicted of an aggravated felony ineligible for a waiver, are retroactive); see also Alvarez-Barajas v. Gonzales, 418 F.3d 1050, 1054-55 (9th Cir. 2005); Guaylupo-Moya v. Gonzales, 423 F.3d 121 (2d Cir. 2005).
 
*2 The respondent urges the Board to reopen proceedings under its sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). This action is taken only in “exceptional situations.” Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002); Zhang v. Gonzales, 469 F.3d 51, 53 (1st Cir. 2006)(acknowledging Board’s authority to reopen sua sponte is beyond court’s jurisdiction to review). As the respondent is ineligible for the relief requested, the Board declines to reopen proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
The pending motion is, therefore, denied, and the request for a stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699735 (BIA)

2007 WL 4699896 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALBERTO FERNANDEZ ALVAREZ
FILE: AXX XX7 101 - MIAMI, FL
November 30, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan C. Freire, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s June 6, 2007, decision affirming the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed an opposition. The motion will be granted, the appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
A motion to reconsider must specify the errors in the prior Board decision and be supported by pertinent authority. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In his motion to reconsider, the respondent maintains that the Board erred in failing to address his contention on appeal that the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We agree that the Board should have addressed that issue. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
Immigration Judges have regulatory authority to set filing deadlines, and if those deadlines are not met the “opportunity to file that application or document shall be deemed waived.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). While Immigration Judges have broad authority under the regulations, 8 C.F.R. § 1240.41, that authority is not without limitation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (the Board may review de novo the exercise of discretion by an Immigration Judge). The respondent contends that clerical error on the part of his former counsel caused him not to timely file his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996).¿FN1º
 
In this case, we conclude that the Immigration Judge was unnecessarily rigid in his application of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) and abused his discretion by denying the respondent’s timely motion to reopen. The Immigration Judge appears to have failed to take into account all factors relevant to reopening. First, the motion to reopen was filed, including the completed application for relief, well over a month before the scheduled Individual Hearing and indicated a readiness to go forward on that scheduled date. Cf. Matter of Shanu, 23 I&N Dec. 754, 764-65 (BIA 2005) (affirming the Immigration Judge’s pretermission of the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal when it was filed after the deadline set by the Immigration Judge and on the scheduled day of the Individual Hearing, thereby precluding advance review of it by the government and the Immigration Judge), vacated on other grounds, 450 F.3d 578 (4th Cir. 2006). Second, the DHS did not oppose the motion to reopen.¿FN2º There is no asserted prejudice to the DHS or negative effect on the Immigration Court that may have resulted from the late filing. Finally, the respondent’s application for a section 212(c) waiver on its face reveals a substantial likelihood of success. It shows the respondent has very strong positive equities, including the fact that he has been a lawful permanent resident for almost 40 years and that the convictions serving as a basis for removability occurred in 1977. Accordingly, the prejudice to the respondent by denying the late filing was significant. Under these circumstances, we conclude that it was an abuse of discretion for the Immigration Judge to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. See Ungar v. Sarafite, 376 U.S. 575, 589 (1964) (“a myopic insistence upon expeditiousness in the face of a justifiable request for delay” can amount to an abuse of discretion). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be granted. Upon reconsideration, the appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for proceedings consistent with this order.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is granted, and the Board’s June 6, 2007, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Upon reconsideration, the appeal is sustained and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

Although it is clear that the respondent was prejudiced by his former counsel’s error, he does not raise ineffective assistance of counsel and has not met the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).

2

Nor has it filed an opposition to the respondent’s appeal or to this motion to reconsider.

2007 WL 4707485 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABBAL JAWANDOR SEASAY, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY BABAR JAWANDOR, PETITIONER
FILE: AXX XX7 317 - HOUSTON, TX
November 26, 2007
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Elizabeth M. Mendoza Macias, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Pauline A. Appelbaum
Associate Regional Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Petition to classify status of alien relative for issuance of immigrant visa
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The petitioner filed an appeal from a decision dated September 19, 2005, by the district director of the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) denying the petition to classify the respondent as the child of a United States citizen. The district director, in denying the visa petition, referenced the Notice of Intent to Deny, dated August 9, 2005, which stated that section 204(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(e), applied because the beneficiary’s prior marriage was fraudulent. Matter of Tawfik, 20 I&N Dec. 166 (BIA 1990), states that the evidence of a fraudulent marriage “must be documented in the alien’s file and must be substantial and probative.” Id. at 167.
 
In the instant case, the petitioner did not respond to the NOID. Further, the record contains substantial and probative evidence of marriage fraud, both in statements made by the beneficiary’s former spouse indicating that their marriage was not bona fide, and in investigative reports presented by DHS.
 
On appeal, the petitioner submits several sworn statements where the beneficiary’s spouse has recanted her statements that her marriage to the beneficiary was not bona fide. However, the petitioner has not explained why this evidence was not submitted previously. He states that his prior attorney should have submitted this evidence, but the petitioner has not raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Where, as here, a petitioner has been put on notice of a deficiency in the evidence and has been given an opportunity to respond to that deficiency, this Board will not accept evidence offered for the first time on appeal. Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Obaigbena, 19 I&N Dec. 533 (BIA 1988). Under the circumstances, we need not and do not comment on the sufficiency of the evidence forwarded on appeal. For the reasons stated above, the appeal is dismissed.
 
The petitioner may, of course, file a new visa petition that is supported by competent evidence that the beneficiary is entitled to the status sought under the immigration laws.
 
Ana L. Mann
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4707485 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4699734 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISIDRO HIDALGO ARRIAGA
FILE: AXX XX6 029 - MIAMI, FL
November 26, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTIONS
  
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&Y Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43XF)
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals an Immigration Judge’s August 20, 2007, decision finding him removable as charged. The respondent also moves to remand the proceedings and to stay his removal pending the disposition of this appeal. The appeal will be dismissed; the motion to remand will be denied and the motion to stay will be denied as moot.
 
The respondent argues that his state felony conviction resulting in a sentence of more than one year stems from ineffective assistance of counsel. He asserts that he was originally offered a plea bargain requiring him to serve less than 1 year’s confinement in the county jail, and he was aware that a sentence of less than 1 year would have no immigration consequences. However, the respondent contends, his trial counsel advised him that he could accept a term of probation in lieu of incarceration, without informing him that if he violated the terms of the probation, he could face deportation. The respondent further asserts that his probation officer illegally imposed a “special condition” by requiring him to report to a probation office in another county. Since the “special condition” was not announced at sentencing, the respondent posits, according to Florida law it was invalid and could not form the basis for the probation revocation and the imposition of the sentence that underpins his aggravated felony removal charge.
 
To the extent that the respondent attempts to assert a claim of ineffective assistance by his state criminal trial counsel, this is not the forum with jurisdiction to address such a grievance. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031 (BIA 1999) (the Board lacks jurisdiction to consider a collateral attack on a facially valid judgment of conviction). Similarly, the respondent’s challenge to the conditions of his probation constitute a collateral attack that properly should be resolved before the body that imposed those conditions. See id. According to the record before us, the removal charges against the respondent have been sustained by clear and convincing evidence and no forms of relief from removal are available to him. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s findings.
 
The respondent alleges in his remand motion that he was never presented with or served personally or by certified mail with a Notice to Appear.¿FN1º This statement is belied by his testimony at his June 22, 2007, telephonic removal hearing, when, in response to the Immigration Judge’s query as to whether he had received the charging document setting forth the reasons why the government alleged that he was reportable, the respondent replied “Yes.” See Tr. at 1 line 19. He acknowledges that he was aware of the charges, but claims that the 2-day telephonic notice of his hearing provided him insufficient time to retain counsel or prepare for the hearing. In contrast, not only does the record reflect that he conceded service of the charging document, but the Immigration Judge continued his hearing for 4 weeks to allow him to obtain counsel. See id. at 4, 5. The respondent also propounds his arguments that he received unsatisfactory advice from trial counsel and that he was ill-served by the conditions of his probation, issues which, as discussed supra, are beyond the purview of the Board. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carillo, supra. He also identifies factors that would mitigate in his favor if he qualified for a form of relief from removal, which, as noted, he does not. Under these circumstances, there is no purpose to be served by remanding the proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to stay removal during the pendency of this appeal is denied as moot.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent also argues that he was never served with an Order to Show Cause. However, an Order to Show Cause would have been inapplicable to these removal proceedings, which were commenced on March 13, 2007. The “Order to Show Cause,” which initiated deportation proceedings, was replaced with the “Notice to Appear,” which initiated removal proceedings, upon the enactment of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act (IIRIRA), effective April 1, 1997.

2007 WL 4699877 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDDY JOHNNY ROMAN
FILE: AXX XX0 891 - NEW YORK, NY
November 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Stanley H. Wallenstein, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 11 $2(a)(2)(A)(i)(II)º - Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s May 17, 2006, decision ordering the respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic, removed from the United States as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation. Section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S,C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) (2000)
 
In a Notice to Appear (“NTA”) filed with the Immigration Court on February 3, 2005, the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) charged the respondent with inadmissibility under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act on the basis of a 1997 conviction for attempted criminal sale of cocaine in violation of sections 110 and 220.39 of the New York Penal Law. (Exh. 1). At a removal hearing conducted on March 30, 2005, the respondent’a attorney admitted all factual allegations contained in the NTA, admitted “the basis for charge of removal,” and requested a continuance to explore whether the respondent was eligible for any form of relief from removal. (Tr. at 8). At a subsequent hearing conducted on May 16, 2006, moreover, the same attorney conceded that the respondent was not in fact eligible for any form of relief. (Tr. at 30-31). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed on the basis of his admissions and concessions. On appeal, the respondent now argues for the first time that his removability was not established by clear and convincing evidence, despite the admissions and concessions of his counsel, because the DHS offered no documentary evidence to prove the existence of his 1997 drug conviction. The argument is meritless.
 
Applicable regulations, which have the force and effect of law as to the Immigration Judges and this Board, provide in pertinent part that “the immigration judge may determine that removability as charged has been established by the admissions of the respondent.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (2005). Accordingly, the distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity during the respondent’s removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission, and he will not now be heard to challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from that concession. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); see also Ali v. Reno, 22 F.3d 442, 446 (2d Cir. 1994) (citing Matter of Velasquez, supra, and holding that an alien in deportation proceedings is bound by the admissions of counsel); Oscanyan v. Arms Co., 103 U.S. 261, 263 (1880) (holding that “¿tºhe power of the court to act in the disposition of a trial upon facts conceded by counsel is as plain as its power to act upon the evidence produced”); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”).¿FN1º Furthermore, an attorney’s concession of removability is binding even if the removal proceeding is predicated on criminal convictions. See Matter of Pichardo, 21 I&N Dec. 330, 333 n.2 (BIA 1996) (“had the respondent admired to the Service’s factual allegation or conceded his deportability … it would have been unnecessary for the Service to present a record of conviction or other documents admissible as evidence in proving a criminal conviction”); LeTourneur v. INS, 538 F.2d 1368, 1371 (9th Cir. 1976) (holding alien bound by counsel’s concession of deportability in the crime involving moral turpitude context).
 
*2 In conclusion, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States on the basis of his attorney’s concession of removability. Because the respondent is removable on the basis of his concession, moreover, we do not address his appellate arguments regarding application of the categorical and modified categorical approaches.
 
The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent has not argued or proven that his prior attorney’s concession of removability constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).

2007 WL 4707402 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NSHO JACKSON AKAI A.K.A. NSOH JACKSON AKAI
FILE: AXX XX3 560 - BALTIMORE, MD
November 19, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pro se
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel J. Menes
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s September 10, 2007, decision denying as number-barred his motion to rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against him on April 15, 1998, and reopen proceedings. The Immigration Judge noted that a prior motion to reopen had been denied by the court on July 31, 2007. The respondent argues on appeal that his prior attorney rendered ineffective assistance, which resulted in the denial of his initial motion to reopen and the failure of the Immigration Judge to consider the merits of his later-filed motion. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) urges affirmance. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, we note that the respondent has failed to comply with any of the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º Even assuming that the respondent had complied with Matter of Lozada, supra, and established that his former counsel’s performance was deficient, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that he was prejudiced by counsel’s representation. See Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76, 78 (48th Cir. 1989) (finding that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim there must be a showing of prejudice by counsel’s performance). The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s initial motion to reopen because he failed to submit an affidavit to support his contention that he did not receive the Notice to Appear, and did not consider the merits of the respondent’s subsequently-filed motion to reopen because the respondent was number-barred from presenting a successive motion. The record reflects that while the respondent attached an affidavit to his later-filed motion stating that he did not receive the Notice to Appear, he did not address the evidence attached to the response by the DHS reflecting that the Notice to Appear was sent by certified mail to the address that the respondent provided and that the return receipt contains a signature reflecting delivery. In his appeal brief, the respondent argues that the opposition of the DHS to his motion to reopen “was only about court notice slip and P.O. Box address,” and that the Immigration Judge only considered this evidence but did not consider the other evidence in the record regarding the bona fides of his marriage and his request for adjustment of status. However, the respondent was required to establish that he was entitled to rescind the in absentia order of removal and reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge could consider his request to adjust status.¿FN2º Because the respondent does not address the relevant issues regarding notice of the heating, he has failed to establish that he was prejudiced by his former attorney’s alleged deficient performance.
 
*2 As for the merits of the appeal, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent is entitled to file only one motion to reopen an in absentia order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). Because the respondent previously filed a motion to reopen that was considered and denied by the Immigration Judge on July 31, 2007, the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s subsequently-filed motion as number barred.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

2

While an alien in certain circumstances may seek to reopen to apply for new relief without rescinding a motion to reopen, the respondent was not seeking new relief, and any request to reopen to apply for adjustment of status would be untimely under 8C.F.R. § 1003.23 Co)(3). See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).

2007 WL 4707477 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SADRUDDIN SHABUDDIN SOOMAR
FILE: AXX XX5 252 - DALLAS, TX
November 16, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joshua Turin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Saundra Haynes Arrington
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
  
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
On June 29, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for a continuance and ordered him removed from the United States. The respondent has appealed from this decision. The appeal will be dismissed. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
  
I. BACKGROUND
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Pakistan. He originally entered the United States in 1980 but left in 1983 to return to Pakistan. He then returned to the United States in 1987 and appears to have entered without inspection. The respondent filed an application for labor certification through his former attorney, Terry Gish, in 2001. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) placed him in removal proceedings in January 2005. Mr. Gish appears to have been the respondent’s attorney at this time, but he never appeared at the respondent’s hearings. The Immigration Judge thus gave the respondent the opportunity to obtain new counsel.
 
On December 22, 2005, the respondent appeared at his hearing with his present attorney. The Immigration Judge then granted the respondent three continuances to allow his attorney to look into the availability of various forms of relief and to obtain the respondent’s file from his former counsel. The respondent’s current counsel was never able to obtain the respondent’s file. On June 29, 2006, however, the respondent’s attorney asked the Immigration Judge to continue the respondent’s case once again to preserve the respondent’s eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1551 (i). The Immigration Judge denied the request on the ground that the case had already been postponed on numerous occasions and many efforts had been made to assist the respondent in pursing relief but the respondent’s prospects for relief remained highly speculative. The respondent has appealed from this decision.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his request for a continuance was a denial of due process because it pretermitted both his potential application for adjustment under section 245(i) of the Act and his claim of prejudicial ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent contends that he retained his former attorney to file a labor certification before April 30, 2001 to preserve his eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act, but it appears that his former attorney may not have filed the labor certification in a timely manner. The respondent argues that this potential failure constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel because it effectively precludes him from pursuing adjustment under section 245(i) of the Act. The respondent also maintains that his former attorney’s failure to turn over his file constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and makes the Immigration Judge’s removal order reversible as the order denies the respondent due process. The respondent claims that it is his former attorney’s failure to turn over his file that makes him unable to show the Immigration Judge that he is eligible for adjustment under section 245(i) of the Act and entitled to a continuance of his case.
  
II. ANALYSIS
 
*2 The key issue before us on appeal is whether the Immigration Judge erred in denying the respondent’s request for a continuance given all of the circumstances in the respondent’s case, including a potential claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. According to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29, an Immigration Judge “may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown.” The decision to grant or deny a continuance is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); see also Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984) (finding that an alien must show that he was prejudiced by a procedural defect before his proceedings will be invalidated).
 
In this case, we find that the Immigration Judge had good cause to deny the respondent’s request for a continuance. And the respondent has not offered any evidence to indicate that the Immigration Judge’s ruling deprived him of a full and fair hearing. The evidence of record instead indicates that the respondent is not eligible for any relief from removal.
 
The only relief for which the respondent claims eligibility is adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act. To be eligible for this form of relief, the respondent had to file an application for labor certification or an application for an immigrant visa on or before April 30, 2001. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(1). The information in the respondent’s brief on appeal suggests that the respondent did not meet this requirement. Specifically, the respondent’s attorney states in footnote 1 on pate 7 of the brief that he has obtained the respondent’s file from the Department of Labor and that the cover letter to the respondent’s application for labor certification bears a stamp stating “ALC Rcvd May 04, 2001”. See Brief for Appellant at 7, fn 1. The respondent contends that it is the date the application was postmarked rather that the date of the stamp that establishes the filing date, but this contention is incorrect. The pertinent regulation, 8C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(2), states that, for an application for labor certification, “properly filed” means that the application was filed and accepted pursuant to the regulations of the Secretary of Labor.”¿FN1º The regulations of the Secretary of Labor, in turn, indicate that the filing date for a non-electronically filed application is the date the application is stamped. See 20 C.F.R. § 656.17(c)(titled “filing date” and stating that “¿nºon-electronically filed applications accepted for processing shall be date stamped” while ¿eºlectronically filed applications will be considered filed when submitted”). Given this information, it does not appear that the respondent’s failure to obtain his former attorney’s file and the postmarked date of his application for labor certification impacts his case. The information in the respondent’s appellate brief establishes that the respondent’s application for labor certification was properly filed on May 4, 2001. The respondent therefore does not appear to qualify as a “grandfathered alien” for the purposes of section 245(i) of the Act and does not appear eligible for adjustment of status under this provision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(1). In light of this fact, we cannot conclude that the Immigration Judge erred in refusing to continue the respondent’s proceeding to allow him to pursue adjustment of status. See Ahmed v. Gonzales, 447 F.3d 433, 439 (5th Cir. 2006) (finding that alien lacked good cause for a continuance because he was ineligible for removal relief under the relevant statutes); Ramchandani v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 337, 339-40 (5th Cir. 2005).
 
*3 In addition, even if the respondent’s application for labor certification had been filed on or before April 30, 2001 so that the respondent could qualify for adjustment under section 245(i) of the Act, the respondent, at the time of his hearing before the Immigration Judge, still would have had many requirements to satisfy before he truly would have been entitled to adjustment of status. First, his application for labor certification would have to have been approved, and, as of the date of his appellant brief, this had not happened. Next, the respondent would need to have an employer file a visa petition on his behalf, have that petition approved, and have a current priority date. This process could take years and, given the number of opportunities for denials and the ultimate discretionary nature of adjustment itself, we cannot conclude that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the respondent’s request for a continuance to pursue the process. See Ahmed v. Gonzales, supra, (finding that Immigration Judge had good cause to deny continuance when alien had only filed labor certification and did not yet meet requirements for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act).¿FN2 º In fact, once the DHS initiates deportation or removal proceedings, neither an Immigration Judge nor this Board can terminate or indefinitely adjourn the proceedings in order to delay an alien’s deportation or removal. See Matter of Quintero, 18 I&N Dec. 348 (BIA 1982). Given these facts, we find no basis for reversing the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a continuance.
 
In reaching this conclusion, we note that the respondent’s possible claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not alter our findings. While the respondent’s former counsel may have been responsible for the late filing of the respondent’s application for labor certification, our ruling on the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance would have been the same whether the labor certification was timely filed or not. And the late filing will not prejudice the respondent with respect to his ability to apply for adjustment until his labor certification is approved and he becomes the beneficiary of an approved and current visa petition. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003)(indicating that alien must show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel). Accordingly, we cannot find that the respondent is entitled to further proceedings on account of the actions of his former counsel.¿FN3º
 
Finally, with respect to the respondent’s due process arguments, we note that the Fifth Circuit repeatedly has held that discretionary relief from removal, including an application for adjustment of status, is not a liberty or property right that requires due process protection. See Ahmed v. Gonzales, supra, at 440, citing Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 475 (5th Cir. 2004)(stating, in a removal context, that due process claims revolving around an alleged failure to receive discretionary relief are not based upon a constitutionally protected liberty interest) and Mireles-Valdez v. Ashcroft, 349 F.3d 213, 219 (5th Cir. 2003) (holding that eligibility for discretionary relief from a removal order is not a liberty or property interest warranting due process protection). We therefore cannot conclude that the respondent has raised valid due process arguments.
 
*4 Based on the foregoing, we find that the Immigration Judge acted properly in denying the respondent’s request for a continuance, and we dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The regulation (8 C.F.R. § 1245.10(a)(2)) cross-references 20 C.F.R. § 656.21 for labor certifications, but 20 C.F.R. § 656.17 (basic labor certification process) appears to be the relevant regulation.

2

The respondent has cited Subhan v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 591 (7th Cir. 2004), in support of his arguments, but the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the circuit in which the respondent’s case arises, has declined to follow the Seventh Circuit’s reasoning in Subhan. We therefore cannot conclude that the ruling is controlling in this case.

3

In reaching this conclusion, we note that it is not clear that the Immigration Judge or this Board has the power to remedy the late filing of the respondent’s labor certification even if the respondent is able to establish potential eligibility for adjustment of status and prejudice.

IN RE: JUAN PABLO DUARTE

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael P. Rummel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler
  
APPLICATION: Termination; Remand
 
On February 28, 2005, the Immigration Judge found the respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, removable as charged. This matter was last before us on July 13, 2006, when we granted the respondent’s motion to remand pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in finding that the evidence submitted revealed a factual question as to whether the respondent smuggled aliens into the United States. The respondent now appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 17, 2006, decision again finding him removable as charge d. The appeal is dismissed.
 
In pertinent part, the regulations provide that the Board may summarily dismiss any appeal in which the only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(2)(B). In this case, the respondent indicated through counsel at the November 17, 2006, hearing that he was not objecting to the Form I-213, the essential document through which the Department of Homel and Security (“DHS”) aimed to prove the charge (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 8-12). The Form I-213 reflects that the respondent admitted to borrowing the California birth certificates of his sister- in-law and his cousin’s son in an attempt to smuggle two aliens into the United States from Mexico (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 4-5). The respondent further indicated at the hearing that he would not be presenting any rebuttal evidence, motion to suppress the DHS documentation, or allegations of coercion (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 11-13). Moreover, the respondent does not contest the Form I-213 in his brief, stating “I know what I did was wrong and have learn ¿sicº my lesson.” The respondent also conceded that he is not eligible for any form of relief from removal (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 2-3, 13- 14). Therefore, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering him removed to Mexico.
 
We further note that the respondent alleges in his brief that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to contest the Form I-213. As an initial matter, remand is not warranted on this basis because the respondent failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, supra. Additionally, the record does not support the respondent’s contention that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance. The espondent’s attorney stated that he reviewed the Form 1-213 with him through a Spanish interpreter and he offered no objections (Tr. at 8-9). The respondent also did not object to the Form I-213 or the representations of his attorney at the hearing, despite the fact that a Spanish interpreter was present. Accordingly, we reject the respondent’s claim that his concession of removability resulted from ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 For these reasons, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: DAISSY BARRERA DE CANTU
FILE: AXX XX1 011 - HARLINGEN, TX
November 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ricardo Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mike Ochoa
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Removability
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s November 22, 2006, decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States as an alien who has been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude committed within 5 years after admission for which a sentence of 1 year or longer may be imposed. See section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that the Immigration Judge deprived her of a full and fair hearing when he denied her request for a continuance so that she could pursue post-conviction relief with respect to her January 6, 2006, conviction for the offense of theft, a Class A misdemeanor under Texas law (Exh. 2; Tr. at 4-5). The decision whether to grant a continuance is committed to the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. Thus, an Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance request will not be overturned on appeal unless the respondent establishes that the denial deprived her of her due process right to a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987). As a rule, an Immigration Judge need not hold removal proceedings in abeyance based on the mere possibility that a collateral criminal proceeding could have a future impact on the alien’s immigration status. The respondent had (and has) a valid “conviction” for immigration purposes, and her collateral attack upon that conviction does not operate to negate its validity until it is in fact overturned or materially amended. Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). We have received no indication that the respondent’s conviction has been vacated or otherwise affected by her collateral attack. Therefore, the respondent has not established that the Immigration Judge’s denial of her continuance request has compromised the fairness of her removal proceedings or resulted in prejudice. See Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). Consequently, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s request for a continuance.
 
During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent’s counsel filed a motion to re-calendar the briefing schedule. While he noted that the respondent’s prior attorney indicated in her Notice of Appeal that he would not be filing an appellate brief, the respondent’s current counsel requests a copy of the transcript and an opportunity to file a brief in support of her appeal. We note that a copy of the transcript was provided to the respondent’s prior attorney, and the respondent was given until April 12, 2007, to submit a brief to the Board. The respondent had an opportunity to request an extension of the briefing schedule prior to April 12, 2007. However, her request to file an appellate brief was received by the Board more than 2 months after this deadline. We note that there are no allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel in this case. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Under the circumstances, we find it inappropriate to re-calendar the briefing schedule. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and her motion is denied.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699889 (BIA)

REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 The Board dismissed an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying reopening on July 14, 2006. On June 1, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen that is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003. 2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “D HS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion. The Board will reopen proceedings under its sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003. 2(a), and reissue the July 14, 2006, decision.
 
The respondent alleges that her former attorney, Jose L. DelCastillo, failed to properly advise her so that she could file a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. The respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). More over, in a submission concerning the respondent’s complaint against him, former counsel reveals his lack of knowledge regarding the respondent’s appeal rights, by stating that the respondent could have filed a petition for review with the Second Circuit, as she lived in Connecticut. In fact, this case arises within the jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit: “The petition for review shall be filed with the court of appeals for the judicial circuit in which the Immigration Judge completed the proceedings.” See section 242(b) (2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b)(2). In this case, the proceedings took place in Phoenix, Arizona, as an Immigration Judge there issued an order of removal in absentia, and then denied motions to reopen this decision.
 
The Board’s July 14, 2006, decision will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. See Firmansjah v. Ashcroft, 347 F.3d 625 (7th Cir. 2003) (Board has authority to reissue decision).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated July 14, 2006, attached hereto, is here by reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2007 WL 4699969 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD HANIF MIRZA A.K.A. MUHAMMAD H. MIRZA
FILE: AXX XX5 229 - HARTFORD, CT
November 9, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin Conlon, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The Board issued its final administrative decision in this proceeding on August 31, 2006, when we dismissed the rcspondent’s appeal. On June 14, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion is untimely and the respondent has not demonstrated that a regulatory exception applies. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and (3). The respondent contends that the time limit on reopening should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of former counsel.
 
The motion will be denied because the respondent has not demonstrated that his former counsel, Mr. DelCastillo, provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Mr. DelCastillo was an attorney that the respondent hired to file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit. Apparently, a labor certification was approved on behalf of the respondent in September 2006, and Mr. DelCastillo agreed to arrange for the filing of an I-140 employment based visa petition, which he did in October 2006, It is unclear, however, whether the respondent agreed to have Mr. DelCastillo file a motion to reopen with the Board, or entered into an attorney-client relationship for this purpose. Further, it is unlikely that the Board would have granted such a motion because the respondent’s I-140 was not approved until March 2007, long after the expiration of the 90-day time limit within which to file a timely motion to reopen.
 
In addition, in order to qualify for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i), the respondent would have to demonstrate that he was “grandfathered” by the previous religious worker visa submitted on his behalf, which was approved in 2000. However, despite this approval, subsequent events, including this Board’s affirmance on August 31, 2006, of the decision to deny adjustment of status on that petition, call into serious question whether that petition was genuinely “approvable when filed,” or was fraudulent. The mosque which filed the petition closed amidst allegations that it generated numerous fraudulent visa petitions of “religious workers.” The respondent has earned his primary income from work as a taxi driver, supplemented by teaching private students. Accordingly, at the time a timely motion to reopen could have been filed, it was speculative, and remains speculative, whether the respondent is eligible for the relief he seeks, adjustment of status. Therefore, the respondent would not have been entitled to reopening of his proceedings based on approval of the labor certificate. See Elbahjah v. Keisler, --F.3d--, 2007 WL 2935884 (2d Cir. Oct. 10, 2007)(IJ not required to continue proceedings based on speculative eligibility for adjustment of status). As such, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699969 (BIA)

2007 WL 4699882 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUANA REYES-DIAZ
FILE: AXX XX9 170 - CHICAGO, ILLINOIS
November 8, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Conlan, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 20, 2007, to deny her motion to reopen an order of deportation entered in absentia on February 27, 1996. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she was not adequately notified of the hearing. Former section 242B(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(a)(2), provides in pertinent part that in deportation proceedings written notice shall be given in person to the alien (or, if personal service is not practicable, written notice shall be given by certified mail to the alien) of the time and place of proceedings. Former section 242B(c)(1) of the Act states that the written notice shall be considered sufficient if provided at the most recent address provided by the alien. However, no written notice is required if an alien has failed to provide an address pursuant to the requirement set forth in section 242B(a)(1)(F) of the Act. See former section 242B(c)(2) of the Act; Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
On November 15, 1995, the Immigration Court sent the respondent the hearing notice via certified mail at 15 Hawthorne CT, #3, Aurora, IL 60506. That address is contained on a form EOIR-33 filed by the respondent on June 16, 1995. The respondent states in her declaration that she moved to Minneapolis in September 1995 and did not file a change of address form with the Immigration Court. We therefore find that the notice was proper in these proceedings because it was sent to the respondent’s last known address. The respondent was also not entitled to written notice of her hearing by failing to comply with her address reporting requirements.
 
The respondent further argues that exceptional circumstances prevented her from appearing for her hearing. She indicates that she hired an attorney prior to her hearing who had assured her that he would receive all notices on her behalf and notify her of all required appearances in the Immigration Court. According to the respondent, former counsel did not file a Notice of Appearance in these proceedings resulting in her inability to receive the hearing notice. The respondent indicates that she received a letter from former counsel dated May 16, 2007, that informs her of the order of deportation and asks whether she wanted to seek reopening. The respondent states that she authorized former counsel to do so. She further states that she was later informed by counsel’s office that her case was reopened and closed administratively with the consent of the government. The respondent indicates that she believed that her case had remained closed until she was later arrested and learned that she was subject to an order of deportation. She argues that these proceedings should be reopened based on ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 A motion to reopen based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days of the order of deportation. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that the doctrine of equitable tolling applies to the time limitations for motions to reopen during periods when a respondent is prevented from filing his or her motion because of deception, fraud, or error, provided that the respondent acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error and was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance. See Patel v. Gonzales, 442 F.3d 1011 (7th Cir. 2006). Herein, the respondent charges former counsel with providing her with incorrect information regarding her immigration status and has filed a complaint against former counsel with a state bar association. The respondent has not, however, provided any evidence that she advised former counsel that he was accused of ineffective assistance of counsel before the Immigration Court and given an opportunity to respond to the motion to reopen. The declaration from current counsel states that former counsel was notified of the bar complaint to satisfy the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He does not state that he also informed former counsel of the ineffective assistance of counsel argument raised in these proceedings.
 
In Patel v. Gonzales, 496 F.3d 829 (7th Cir. 2007), the Seventh Circuit stressed the importance of notifying an attorney that his competence and integrity has been impugned to allow counsel to present his rendition of the facts. We cannot find that the respondent established that she was the victim of attorney negligence based solely on her personal accounts. It follows that the respondent has not shown that she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel and equitable tolling of the period for seeking reopening is inappropriate. The motion to reopen was filed untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is also affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4699882 (BIA)
End of Document
