2007 WL 4699883 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HANA’A AL-MOHTADI
FILE: AXX XX3 336 - NEW ORLEANS, LA
November 8, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rekha Sharma-Crawford, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Joseph B. La Rocca
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s September 19, 2006, decision denying her motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia deportation order that was entered on March 13, 1997. The respondent argues that she did not receive notice of her hearing.
 
We find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that on February 27, 1997, the hearing notice was successfully delivered, by certified mail, to the office of the respondent’s former attorney.¿FN1º See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(b)(5)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); see also Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
The respondent does not dispute that Richard A. Bimbaum was her counsel at the time of the scheduled hearing. She was aware that she was in proceedings, yet the affidavit originally provided with the motion to reopen makes no mention of any communications with Mr. Birnbaum or the immigration court regarding the status of her case after he filed the motion to change venue to New Orleans in January of 1997. The respondent provided a second affidavit on appeal. The Board ordinarily will not consider any previously available evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995).
 
While there are some instances where we will consider new evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent was hardly diligent in pursuing such a claim.¿FN2º Her motion was not filed until more than nine years after she last communicated with her prior counsel. The rcspondent’s October 10, 2006, bar complaint states that the “reason for not following up with Mr. Richard all those years is that I was under the assumption that my case was ¿pºending.” It was not until a supplemental affidavit dated January 16, 2007, that the respondent claimed to have made any efforts to contact Mr. Birnbaum about her case. Her affidavit states that those attempts ended approximately three months after the January 1997 motion to change venue. She did not exercise due diligence by failing to make any inquiries about her deportation case for nearly a decade. Thus, even if the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit were to adopt the doctrine of equitable tolling in the Context of untimely motions to reopen in absentia orders, a favorable exercise of our discretion is not warranted in this case because the respondent did not act with due diligence.¿FN3º We affirm the result reached by the Immigration Judge because the motion is untimely.
 
*2 The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4707494 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LESLIE E. WOGHIREN
FILE: AXX XX0 601 - NEWARK, NJ
October 30, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew Guadagno, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(D), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(D)º- Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Nigeria, has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s August 13, 2007, decision. The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We adopt the procedural history set forth in the Immigration Judge’s August 13, 2007, decision. We briefly note, as set forth in the Immigration Judge’s order, that the respondent was granted voluntary departure on December 23, 2005. More than a year later, on August 7, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. He claimed that his prior counsel failed to advise him to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(b), and a Form I-751 hardship waiver. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent met the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), but failed to establish that the filing deadline for his motion should be equitably tolled. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to submit supporting evidence to show that his children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship, as required to establish eligibility for cancellation of removal. The respondent now appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen.
 
We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Inasmuch as the respondent is in deportation proceedings, he is not eligible for cancellation of removal.¿FN1º Furthermore, the record indicates that the respondent filed a Form I-751 waiver, which was denied (Exh. 3). Therefore, the respondent has not shown that his prior attorney provided him ineffective assistance or that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge failed to inform him of his apparent eligibility for cancellation of removal. We recognize that an Immigration Judge is under an affirmative obligation to inform the respondent of any apparent forms of relief from deportation which may be available to him. See Matter of Cordova, 22 I&N Dec. 966, 970-71 (BIA 1999); Matter of Ulloa, 22 I&N Dec. 725, 726 (BIA 1999). Nonetheless, we note that the respondent did not make this claim in his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. Furthermore, we find no due process violation, as alleged by the respondent, because the respondent, who is in deportation proceedings, is not eligible for cancellation of removal.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the record does not contain a Notice to Appear or any evidence that the respondent was placed in removal proceedings.

2007 WL 4707360 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IMELDA CUEVAS-AGUILAR A.K.A. IMELDA AGUILAR-CASTILLO
FILE: AXX XX2 900 - SAN DIEGO, CA
October 29, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William J. Baker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before us on October 7, 2004, when we determined that the respondent’s appeal was untimely filed, dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, and returned the record to the Immigration Judge without further action. 8 C.F.R. §§1003.38(b), (c).
 
On August 9, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion to reconsider the dismissal of her appeal. See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(6). The respondent seeks reinstatement of her appeal. The respondent’s motion is premised on their argument that their appeal was untimely filed due to their counsel’s ineffectiveness. See generally Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider filed nearly 3 years after the issuance of our prior order is untimely. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act (a motion to reconsider shall be filed within 30 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal). However, because this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit and the respondent presents an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we will consider whether this case warrants equitable tolling of the time limits on motions to reopen. The Ninth Circuit has held that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
We find that equitable tolling is not warranted in this case where the respondent has failed to establish that she exercised due diligence in the 3-year period sought to be tolled. The respondent’s motion is not supported by her own statement or affidavit describing her efforts to determine the status of her appeal. The respondent attached a “verification” to the motion in which she states that she has read and believes the contents of the motion to be true. According to the motion, one week after the hearing, the respondent retained Attorney Rodriguez to file an appeal on her behalf. After three months, she did not hear from him and began calling him. For six months she tried unsuccessfully to contact him at which time she gave up and decided to “wait for the attorney.” See Respondent’s Motion at 2-3. Around April 2007, she spoke with the attorney and was informed by the attorney’s secretary that her case was “impossible.” Id. The respondent does not state when she hired new counsel who informed her that the appeal was dismissed as untimely filed. Id.
 
*2 Given the respondent’s “verification” and accepting the motion as the respondent’s own statement, we find that she was on notice that there was a problem with her appeal three months after she retained Mr. Rodriguez when despite her numerous efforts, she was unable to communicate with him. She said at times “the office would even be closed.” See Respondent’s Motion at 3. It was not until two years later that she actually spoke to Mr. Rodriguez. The respondent simply failed to show that she exercised due diligence during this time. She does not state when she retained new counsel and how long it took her to file the instant motion. Although we need not reach the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim because the motion is untimely filed, we add that the respondent has failed to comply with any of the procedural requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). This is not a case where the ineffective assistance of counsel is obvious and undisputed on the face of the record so that we would dispense with these requirements. Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion will be denied as time barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4182347 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SULMA GOMEZ A.K.A. ZULMA MENDOZA GUTIERREZ¿FN1º
FILE: AXX XX3 956 - LOS ANGELES, CA
October 19, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Arthur Evangelista, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated December 4, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen her deportation proceedings, which had been conducted in absentia under section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The respondent filed a timely appeal from that decision. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The respondent does not contest the fact that she was personally served with an Order to Show Cause on June 9, 1989 (Exh. 1). The respondent also concedes that on August 22, 1989, she and her counsel appeared before an Immigration Judge, and were informed she was scheduled for a hearing on November 2, 1989. This hearing was later rescheduled to March 23, 1990, and notice of the same was sent to respondent’s counsel. In her motion and on appeal the respondent contends that counsel did not inform her of either the rescheduled hearing, or that she was ordered deported in absentia on March 23, 1990. While she contended in her motion to reopen that her prior representative thus afforded her ineffective assistance of counsel, she submitted no evidence in this regard. On appeal the respondent has submitted an affidavit alleging former counsel failed to inform her of her rescheduled hearing and the Immigration Judge’s in absentia decision. She also provided a copy of a letter purportedly sent to counsel in this regard.
 
An Immigration Judge may conduct a hearing in absentia if an alien has been given a reasonable opportunity to be present at a proceeding, and without reasonable cause fails or refuses to attend or remain in attendance. Matter of Nafi, 19 I&N Dec. 430 (BIA 1987); see also Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988). Lack of notice may constitute reasonable cause for missing a hearing. See Matter of Lopez-Barrios, 20 I&N Dec. 203 (BIA 203). Moreover, the Board has held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute “reasonable cause” where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997).
 
Here, the respondent failed to submit any evidence with her motion substantiating her claim that she did not receive notice of her deportation hearing because of former counsel’s ineffective assistance. While she has submitted some evidence in this regard on appeal, the Board ordinarily will not consider evidence first proffered on appeal, as its review is limited to the record of proceedings before the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Moreover, we do not find that a remand in this regard is warranted. The respondent has submitted two of her own declarations and a copy of a letter purportedly sent from current counsel to her former representative in this regard. However, there is no evidence indicating that Mr. Vega was, in fact, ever served with the letter from present counsel. Moreover, the respondent states she has not complied with the requirement that she submit a complaint with the appropriate bar authorities because she “only recently discovered” she had been ordered deported in absentia and was unaware she could file a complaint, and did not know how to do so (Resp.’s Br. at 8).¿FN2º
 
*2 The respondent has not cited any regulation or precedent citing such lack of knowledge as an excuse for failing to comply with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor do we otherwise find that any exists. Nor has the respondent explained how the bar complaint requirement has otherwise been “fully served by other means.” See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The timing of her knowledge of the alleged ineffective assistance is irrelevant to this requirement. The respondent is represented by counsel who should be able to assist her in filing an appropriate complaint, and comply with this central requirement of Matter of Lozada, supra. Given this basic deficiency, we do not find that a remand for further review of this claim is warranted. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). Similarly, the aspect of the respondent’s motion requesting reopening to pursue an application for adjustment of status and/or NACARA relief, was untimely because it was not filed by September 30, 1996. See 8 C.F.R. § 3.23(b)(1). We note that the result of this case is not guided by our decision in Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998), which applies to motions to reopen under section 242B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b, not to motions to reopen under section 242(b) of the Act.
 
In sum, we agree that the respondent had proper notice of her March 23, 1990, hearing as the same had been given to her attorney of record. Consequently, the Immigration Judge did not err in declining to reopen the respondent’s deportation proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

This is the name provided by the respondent on her motion to reopen.

2

We observe that the respondent has not explained whether she appeared in court for the November 2, 1989, hearing (and if not, why) in light of her admission she received personal notice of the same, and her claim she did not know her hearing had been rescheduled (Motion at 1).

2007 WL 4182367 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIA TREJO-HINOJOSA
FILE: AXX XX3 899 - FLORENCE, AZ
October 18, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Maria Bjornerud
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(E)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(E)(i)º - Alien smuggler (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings; relief from removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s July 16, 2007, decision finding her removable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal due to her conviction for an aggravated felony as that term is defined in section 101(a)(43)(N) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(N), is dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on April 4, 1990 (Exh. 1). On June 5, 2005, the respondent was convicted in the United States District Court, Southern District of Texas, for the offense of transportation of an unlawful alien within the United States in violation of 8 U.S.C. §§ 1324(a)(1)(A)(ii) and (B)(ii), and was sentenced to 33 months’ imprisonment (Exh. 3). The respondent was placed into the instant proceedings with the issuance of the Notice to Appear on May 29, 2007 (Exh. 1).
 
In his July 16, 2007, decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ineligible for relief from removal due to her conviction for an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(N) of the Act (I.J. at 2).¿FN1º On appeal, the respondent contends that she has been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel arguing that her prior attorney failed to zealously object to the Department of Homeland Security’s evidence of her conviction and failed to prepare proper applications for relief, e.g., an application for withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3). See Respondent’s Brief at 4-5 and 8. On appeal, the respondent claims eligibility for withholding of removal as a member of a “particular social group” comprised of “Mexican women who have been raped and who fear reporting the crime to the Mexican police who will not protect her.” Id. at 12-13. The respondent claims that she was raped by her former fiance. Id. The respondent also asserts that her conviction is not one for an aggravated felony. Id. at 5-7. Finally, the respondent contends that the record should be remanded so that she may apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227b(a). See Respondent’s Brief at 11-13.
 
*2 Despite the respondent’s contentions on appeal, we concur in the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the record of conviction in this case establishes that the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(N) of the Act. See Matter of Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 486 (BIA 1999) (holding that an alien who is convicted of transporting an illegal alien within the United States in violation of 8 U.S.C. § 1324(a)(1)(A)(ii), was convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(N)of the Act). As a result of her conviction, the respondent is not eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.¿FN2º
 
Moreover, even if we concluded that the respondent had met the technical requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), we find that she has failed to show that any action by her former counsel in not filing a withholding of removal application prejudiced the outcome of her case. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In this regard, the respondent has failed to set forth a valid claim for withholding of removal under the Act based upon her claimed membership in a particular social group. See Castro-Perez v. Gonzales, 409 F.3d 1069 (9th Cir. 2005) (finding that a woman, basing her claim on membership in a particular social group, who claimed that she suffered past persecution when she was raped by a man she dated and that the Honduran government would not protect her, failed to show eligibility for asylum and withholding of removal because she could not show that an agent of the government committed the rape or that the government was unable or unwilling to protect her). The evidence the respondent has submitted with her appeal does not establish that the Mexican government would be unable or unwilling to protect her and, thus, does not affect the outcome of her case; therefore, she has failed to establish that a remand is warranted for any reason. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(2007).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent applied solely for voluntary departure during her underlying proceedings.

2

It appears that the respondent may have confused the original charge against her which was withdrawn and the aggravated felony charge. Insofar as the respondent disputes the original charge, we find no reason to address her arguments since the charge was withdrawn.

2007 WL 4182279 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS FELIPE MERCEDES-PICHARDO
FILE: AXX XX6 450 - NEW YORK, NY
October 15, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge Guttlein, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Lodged: Sec. 241(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act
  
APPLICATION: waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated June 20, 2006. On that date, we adopted and affirmed an Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2004, decision finding that equitable estoppel did not prevent the government from initiating deportation proceedings against the respondent after he was granted permanent resident status through the Special Agricultural Worker (“SAW”) program. The respondent was granted temporary resident status through that program (under section 210 of the Immigration and Nationality Act) on September 9, 1988 and was convicted on July 25, 1989, of attempted criminal sale of a controlled substance. His adjustment to permanent resident status was finalized after the conviction—on September 1, 1990. Because his previous conviction rendered him inadmissible to the United States, the Department of Homeland Security initiated deportation proceedings against the respondent.
 
In support of his July 30, 2007, motion to reopen, the respondent now states that he received ineffective assistance of counsel and that his former counsel should have cited to the controlling United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit precedent Lok v. INS, 548 F.2d 37 (2d Cir. 1977) (“Lok I”), in arguing that he was eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. Lok v. INS, supra, a case involving a waiver of inadmissibility submitted in conjunction with an application for adjustment of status, stands for the proposition that an alien’s 7 years of lawful unrelinquished domicile need not follow an admission for lawful permanent residence. That is, an alien could accrue “lawful domicile” without being admitted for lawful permanent residence, or prior to such an admission. See Matter of Lok, 16 I&N Dec. 441 (BIA 1978). In that case, both this Board and the Second Circuit later found that Lok’s period of residence prior to his admission as a lawful permanent residence could not count toward the 7 years required under former section 212(c) of the Act because prior to his admission, he was under an order of deportation, awaiting action on a private relief bill pending in the United States Senate. See Lok v. INS, 681 F.2d 107 (2d Cir. 1982) (“Lok II”). The respondent’s former counsel has filed a response to the allegation that she provided ineffective assistance below, and argues that Lok I does not apply to the respondent’s case because his drug-related conviction rendered him inadmissible to the United States, and, accordingly, he was ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act.
 
*2 The motion is denied as untimely, and because the respondent failed to demonstrate any ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Although the Second Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, recognizes equitable tolling of such deadlines in ineffective assistance of counsel cases, the court held that an alien must demonstrate not only that his constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). Here, the respondent does not explain why some 13 months passed after our last decision before he was able to discover and pursue a remedy for the allegedly ineffective assistance. The respondent appears to suggest that he learned of the basis for the ineffective assistance of counsel claim through the results of a Freedom of Information Act request. Yet, he provides no detail on this contention (except to claim, erroneously, that there is no copy of his 212(c) application in the record of proceedings), and in the end, he does not demonstrate that he pursued any available claims with due diligence. Moreover, the respondent failed to comply with the requirements for alleging ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in that he failed to lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between him and his former attorney. The affidavit accompanying the motion to reopen does not reveal any details of the representation agreement between the respondent and his former attorney, such as any fee structure, or descriptions or dates of meetings between the two parties.
 
Moreover, we cannot on this record find that any ineffective assistance occurred. The respondent’s former attorney was justified in not relying upon Lok v. INS, supra, because there was no issue below as to the respondent’s accrual of the required 7 years of lawful residence. Rather, at issue below was the respondent’s admissibility, and, therefore, eligibility for the waiver. Below, counsel diligently advanced the argument that the respondent was eligible to apply for such relief. Although we do not agree with his former counsel’s current argument-raised in defense of the ineffective assistance claim-that the respondent’s drug conviction rendered his prior admission through SAW void pursuant to Matter of Koloamatangi, 23 I&N Dec. 548 (BIA 2003), we also do not agree with the respondent that counsel’s failure to cite to Lok I rendered her representation of him ineffective. We therefore find no justification for the late motion to reopen, and we accordingly deny the motion.
 
*3 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 4182279 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4182277 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLADYS NNENNAYA JORDEN,BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY BOBBY HARRIS JORDEN, PETITIONER
FILE: AXX XX5 616 - HOUSTON, TEXAS
October 15, 2007
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Michael Eheman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Pauline Appelbaum
Associate Regional Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The petitioner has appealed the December 17, 2005, decision by the District Director, denying the visa petition which was submitted by him on behalf of the beneficiary, his spouse. In denying the petition the District Director referenced the Notice of Intent to Deny, which cited discrepancies in interviews between the petitioner and beneficiary. With his appeal, the petitioner submitted a motion to reopen, seeking to have new evidence considered. The motion is denied and the appeal is dismissed.
 
In visa petition proceedings, the petitioner has the burden of establishing eligibility for the benefits sought. See Matter of Brantigan, 11 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 1966). The petitioner must prove the required elements by a preponderance of the evidence. See Matter of Pazandeh, 19 I&N Dec. 884 (BIA 1989). Where the bona fides of a marriage are challenged, the petitioner must present documentary or testimonial evidence to show that it was not entered into for the primary purpose of evading the immigration laws. See Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975). However, we note that the failure to produce affirmative evidence of the bona fides of the marriage, by itself, is not sufficient to establish that the marriage is a sham marriage. Compare 8 C.F.R. §§ 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B), (D) (2007) with 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(ii) (2007).
 
The parties married on July 17, 2003, and the visa petition was filed on October 9, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security Citizenship and Immigration Services (CIS), held separate interviews of the petitioner and the beneficiary in November of 2004 to determine the bona fides of the marriage. See Lutwak v. United States, 344 U.S. 604 (1953); Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 1983); Matter of McKee, 17 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1980). Following the interviews, the District Director denied the visa petition, concluding that the petitioner failed to prove the existence of a bona fide marital relationship.
 
During the interviews, there were discrepancies between the testimony of the petitioner and the beneficiary. For example, the petitioner stated he met the beneficiary at his home in April 2004, while the beneficiary stated they met at her aunt’s home in April 2003. The petitioner also reported she had met both of his sisters, while she stated she had not met either. Moreover, the documentation provided by the parties regarding cohabitation was all created within a short time before the interviews although by that time they had been married for more than a year. None of this documentation related to the period encompassing their first year of married life. Although the parties were issued a request for further evidence detailing specific documentation needed to support the petition, they failed to adequately reply to the same. Rather, the petitioner provided only an unsworn and uncorroborated statement from his aunt, a 2001 tax return, and evidence of employment regarding the beneficiary.
 
*2 On appeal, the petitioner filed a motion to reopen, and submitted additional evidence. However, the Board has held that, where a petitioner has been put on notice of a deficiency in the evidence and has been given an opportunity to respond to that deficiency, it will not accept evidence offered for the first time on appeal. Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764 (BIA 1988). As noted, the petitioner failed to adequately respond to the request for further evidence. In the subsequently-issued notice of intent to deny the petitioner was informed of the perceived discrepancies between his answers and those of the beneficiary obtained at the interviews, and was again provided an opportunity to submit further evidence and respond to the same. However, the petitioner offered no response, and thus failed to rebut the noted derogatory evidence. On appeal, the petitioner submitted evidence with his motion in support of the bona fides of his marriage.¿FN1º As noted above, the Board does not accept new evidence on appeal where, as here, the petitioner previously was given an opportunity to submit such evidence. As such, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
The evidence before the District Director supports the denial of the visa petition. No satisfactory explanation for any of the noted discrepancies or evidentiary deficiencies has been provided. As such, we agree with the District Director the parties failed to demonstrate that a bona fide marriage exists. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied, and the appeal is dismissed.
 
The petitioner may, of course, file a new petition on the beneficiary’s behalf that is supported by competent evidence that the beneficiary is entitled to the status sought under the immigration laws.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We recognize that petitioner’s counsel avers the petitioner had, in fact, provided him with documentation responsive to the Notice of Intent to Deny, but that he erroneously failed to file the same. This assertion is in the nature of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, no effort has been made to comply with any of the requirements for such a claim, including the filing of a bar complaint. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10(1st Cir. 1988). In light of this failure, and as we do not accept evidence offered for the first time on appeal, denial of the motion is warranted. For the petitioner’s benefit, we note that the evidence submitted with the motion contains the same deficiencies as that previously offered as it fails to include documentation relating to the first year of their marriage. Nor is any explanation offered for the noted discrepancies in their interviews.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RODOLFO MARTINEZ-PEREZ
FILE: AXX XX9 634 - ANCHORAGE, AK
October 12, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rebecca L. Maxey, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Edward L. Dunlay
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on August 1, 2005, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s May 24, 2004, decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(4).
 
On May 11, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen based, inter alia, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion, which was filed nearly two years after the issuance of a final administrative order, is untimely filed. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7)(C)(i) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
However, because this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit and the respondent presents an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we will consider whether this case warrants equitable tolling of the time limits on motions to reopen. The Ninth Circuit has held that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
The respondent claims that his prior counsel, Mr. Ra Shipps, failed to timely inform him of our August 1, 2005, decision. He first learned of our decision on or about April 17, 2007, and filed the instant motion within one month. We find that the respondent exercised due diligence and that this case warrants equitable tolling. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) states that it does not oppose the respondent’s motion. It notes that if the respondent’s allegations against Mr. Shipps are true, that the respondent acted with due diligence and established that Mr. Shipps prejudiced his case. The respondent subsequently filed a statement from Mr. Shipps who accepted responsibility for failing to inform the respondent of our August 1, 2005, decision. In addition, the respondent has provided evidence that he is the beneficiary of an approved immediate relative visa petition and that he is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1255(a).
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has established that his case should be reopened to allow him the opportunity to apply for relief from removal. The appropriate orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 4182240 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROBERT RAMIREZ-OLORTIGUE
FILE: AXX XX7 292 - NEWARK, NJ
October 11, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Regis Fernandez, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On August 13, 1998, the respondent’s proceedings were “terminated” on Form I-471, by motion of the legacy Immigration and Naturalization Service (INS), to allow the respondent the opportunity to pursue an adjustment of status claim. Specifically, on the Form I-471, entitled Motion by Trial Attorney to Immigration Judge After Commencement of Deportation Hearing, the INS requested that “the deportation proceedings against the respondent be terminated to permit his case to be considered or reconsidered by the District Director.” The Form I-471 was signed by the Immigration Judge on August 13, 1998. At the bottom of the page, in fine print right above the Immigration Judge’s signature, the form explains that “if the action taken by the District Director is favorable to the respondent, the pending deportation proceedings shall be terminated; but that if the action taken by the District Director is not favorable to the respondent the pending deportation proceedings shall remain in full force and effect.” On January 10, 2003, the INS made a motion to “recalendar” the respondent’s proceedings. Therein, the motion indicated that the respondent lived at 12 Sanford Place, Apt. #106, and that his attorney, Lawrence LeRoy, Esquire, practiced at 228 Mt. Prospect Avenue. Notices of Hearing were subsequently sent out to those addresses, and on July 17, 2003, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable in absentia. Thereafter, on August 8, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, arguing that he did not appear for his hearing because neither he nor his former attorney still resided at the addresses provided in the INS’ January 10, 2003, motion. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on August 16, 2007, stating that the Notice to Appear, dated January 30, 1998, was personally served on the respondent, and Mr. Leroy was the respondent’s last counsel of record. The respondent appealed this decision. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending appeal, which will be sustained.
 
The respondent now argues that neither he nor his attorney understood that they were required to continue updating the Immigration Court about their address subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s grant of the 1998 motion. The respondent argues that he moved from his address of record before January 10, 2003, and that he never received a copy of the Immigration Judge’s 2003 decision; he also indicates that he did not maintain contact with Mr. Leroy throughout the process of his adjustment application process. Lastly, the respondent states that he only recently learned that he had been ordered removed when he was detained by immigration agents in 2007, and he filed the AXX XXX XXX instant motion within a short period after his detention. The respondent requests that we remand the record to allow him a full evidentiary hearing regarding his removability.
 
*2 As an initial matter, we note that the Form I-471 used in 1998 to “terminate” the respondent’s proceedings is a form appropriate for deportation, rather than removal, proceedings. As such, the process by which the Immigration Judge administratively closed the respondent’s hearing has been misplaced from its inception. Moreover, given that the Form I-471 never employs the words administrative closure, but rather appears to request termination of the respondent’s proceeding, we find the Immigration Judge’s grant of the motion, and the respondent’s subsequent confusion regarding his status, understandable. While the fine print at the bottom of the page explains the subsequent actions that may be taken by INS, the more prominent statements on the form do not indicate the conditional nature of the termination. Lastly, we note that the Form I-471 was filed by the legacy INS, and while the respondent’s attorney signed the form itself, it is also understandable that the respondent may not have understood the effect of the motion.¿FN1º
 
Given the circumstances presented by the respondent, we herein sustain his appeal, and remand the record to the Immigration Court for further proceedings regarding the respondent’s removability and relief therefrom.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

While the respondent does not argue an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the basis of his appeal is that he was under the impression that his proceedings had been terminated.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTIAGO MOLINA-JIMENEZ
FILE: AXX XX5 607 - SAN DIEGO, CA
October 5, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard Wynn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alan S. Rabinowitz
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has appealed a decision from an Immigration Judge rescinding and reopening the respondent’s in absentia deportation proceedings, and subsequently terminating the deportation proceedings. The respondent has filed an opposition to the appeal. The appeal filed by the DHS will be dismissed.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent had a legitimate claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent contends that he was not able to file complaints against the firm Immigration Legal Options because it does not seem to exist anymore. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding that the requirements for making claims of ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), are generally reasonable, but need not be rigidly enforced). In addition, we agree that, given the DHS’s continued acceptance of the respondent’s various immigration applications throughout the years, the respondent had no actual or constructive notice of the deportation order entered against him. Moreover, given that the DHS granted the respondent’s application for advance parole, the respondent departed the United States with the express approval of the DHS, and it is reasonable to conclude that the respondent was not aware of the consequences of his departure. Given the totality of the circumstances in this case, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is not barred by the regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) from applying for a motion to reopen, and was eligible for rescission of his in absentia deportation proceedings.
 
Further, we also find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent, having returned to the United States under advance parole, is an arriving alien and no longer subject to deportation proceedings. Therefore, the Immigration Judge properly terminated the respondent’s deportation proceedings, noting that the DHS could properly place the respondent in removal proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The DHS’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 3301447 (BIA)

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARLON ORLANDO SANCHEZ-CALDERON
FILE: AXX XX8 773 - LANCASTER, CA
October 4, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia Vargas, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on July 27, 2007, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). The respondent now moves for reconsideration and reopening pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 (2007). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion asks the Board to reconsider our conclusion that, on balance, his equities in the United States were insufficient to outweigh his criminal record. Specifically, the respondent argues that the Board erred in referring to his criminal record, which consists of two convictions, as “extensive.” He also suggests that we did not give sufficient weight to his equities in this country, particularly his family ties, which include his lawful permanent resident grandmother, two United States citizen parents, and two United States citizen children.
 
A motion to reconsider must include an allegation of material factual or legal error in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority. See Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). While the respondent’s criminal record may not qualify as “extensive,” it is indeed a serious adverse factor in this case, as it consists of a controlled substance violation as well as a firearms offense (I.J. at 8-9; Exhs. 2, 3). Additionally, we observed in our decision that the respondent failed to demonstrate rehabilitation, as the record reflects that he failed to appear for court-ordered rehabilitation after being convicted in December 2005 of possession of a controlled substance, to wit, cocaine, in violation of section 11350(a) of the California Health and Safety Code (I.J. at 8-9; Exh. 2). Thereafter, a bench warrant was issued for his arrest (I.J. at 14). We further note that during his removal hearing, the respondent failed to take responsibility or demonstrate remorse for his November 2006 conviction under section 12025(a)(1) of the California Penal Code for carrying a concealed firearm within a vehicle (I.J. at 15, 17). In light of these serious adverse factors, we are unable to conclude that we erred in finding that the respondent’s equities, while considerable, were insufficient to outweigh the adverse factors evidencing his undesirability as a permanent resident of the United States. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7, 11 (BIA 1998); Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581, 584-85 (BIA 1978). We find, therefore, that the respondent has failed to identify any error of fact or law in our previous decision.
 
*2 The respondent also seeks reopening on the basis of new evidence, including a declaration from the mother of his two children, two Western Union receipts, and a copy of his grandmother’s passport. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). He asserts that, while this evidence could have been obtained prior to his hearing, his former attorney failed to recognize the need to do so; he therefore argues that his case warrants reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has submitted an affidavit concerning the details of his agreement with his former attorney, along with a complaint filed with the State Bar of California, a copy of which was apparently served on the respondent’s former counsel. Thus, the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for motions to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 824-25 (9th Cir. 2003). Nevertheless, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that the outcome of his case was prejudiced by his attorney’s actions or inaction. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Although the Immigration Judge cited the respondent’s failure to document his financial support of his children in his overall discussion of potential hardship to his qualifying relatives, it appears that the more significant consideration was the fact that the respondent had not seen the children, ages 3 and 2, since they relocated to Arkansas with their mother in June 2005 (I.J. at 13-14). Thus, even assuming the respondent was contributing to their upbringing financially, there is still insufficient evidence to support the conclusion that his removal to Honduras would exercise a significant hardship on his young children, given that he has not been a regular presence in their lives for quite some time. Furthermore, while the Immigration Judge criticized the respondent for claiming his lawful permanent resident grandmother as a dependent on his 2002 and 2003 federal income tax returns, when she apparently did not relocate to the United States from Honduras until 2004, we did not consider this to be a significant factor in our prior decision; thus, even if the respondent were able to establish that his grandmother was living with him prior to 2004, it would not affect the outcome of his case. We conclude, therefore, that the respondent has failed to show that he was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel and that he has failed to meet the heavy burden required for reopening. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
*3 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen is denied.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

2007 WL 3301460 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARINA GUADALUPE RODRIGUEZ-CASTRO
FILE: AXX XX3 746 - HARLINGEN, TX
October 3, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eric P. Lin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lessa N. Whatmough,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER;
 
*1 PER CURIAM, The respondent, a citizen of Honduras, was ordered removed from the United States after she failed to appear for a hearing on June 26, 2001. Over five years later, on November 16, 2006, she filed a motion to reopen supported by an affidavit which explained that when she was first released on bond she provided the address of a friend in Seattle, Washington, as her contact address and that “her friend advised me that if I received any documents from immigration he would contact me so that I would be able to show up to court and comply with my responsibilities.” She indicated in her affidavit that after she went to Seattle she maintained contact with her friend and called him every week to inquire about her court date, She stated that “¿i ºt was not until this year that I was aware that I had been ordered removed because I had failed to appear for court.” On December 29, 2006, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, finding that she had not demonstrated that she had not been provided with proper notice of her July 26, 2001, removal hearing. That decision was not appealed to the Board.
 
On May 29, 2009, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. In this motion, the respondent, claimed that she was not provided with proper notice of the removal hearing and also claimed ineffective assistance of prior counsel in preparing the first motion to reopen. The second motion includes an affidavit executed on June 8, 2007, by the person who had provided his address as the contact address for the respondent which states: “I never received any mail for ¿the respondentº at my address until late 2001 when I received a document from Immigration saying that the bond had been revoked and that I was supposed to bring ¿respondentº in to Immigration.”¿FN1º
 
The Immigration Judge denied the second motion to reopen on July 6, 2007, finding that it was number barred. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent asserts in her Notice of Appeal that the bar on successive motions to reopen should be equitably tolled because the respondent’s prior attorney negligently prepared her initial motion to reopen. The respondent indicated that a brief would be filed, but no brief has been submitted. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s second motion to reopen was number barred from consideration. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) (providing that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia).
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has not yet published a decision on whether it is proper to equitably toll either the time or number restrictions on seeking reopening if attorney negligence prevented compliance with those requirements. Even were equitable tolling appropriate in some instances involving ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent has not demonstrated that prior counsel provided ineffective assistance or that the outcome of her first motion to reopen would have been altered by more effective representation. Under these circumstances, the respondent has not demonstrated that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the second motion to reopen.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Counsel for the respondent also provided an affidavit explaining that an initial affidavit purportedly executed by the respondent’s contact person on January 23, 2007, and submitted with the second motion to reopen, contained incorrect information, and had been discovered to have been prepared and executed without the affiant’s knowledge.

2007 WL 3301709 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUZIA FUSTAGUIO DO NASCIMENTO
FILE: AXX XX5 244 - BOSTON, MA
September 27, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
June Beack, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on February 5, 2004, at which time we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s December 20, 2002, decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen her March 7, 1997, in absentia hearing for ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded to the motion, which will be denied. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s final order was issued in absentia on March 7, 1997, and the motion to reopen is therefore both number-barred and filed years out of time. Id.
 
The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel that she received from the attorney who represented her before the Immigration Court. The respondent concedes that she has already filed one motion to reopen based on her allegations, and we note that both the Immigration Judge and this Board found that she has failed to meet the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent now requests, three years subsequent to our last consideration of her ineffective assistance of counsel claim, that we grant her motion to reopen because she has now set forth sufficient evidence in support of her claim. However, equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. Chen v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 151, 154 (1st Cir. 2005); Jobe v. INS, 238 F.3d 96, 98-99 (1st Cir. 2001). The respondent does not assert an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against her second attorney, who represented her in her prior motion, and she has otherwise presented an insufficient explanation for her 10 year delay in bringing the current motion. Accordingly, she has failed to demonstrate due diligence sufficient to equitably toll the motions deadline, and the motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 3301709 (BIA)

2007 WL 3318154 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JURAJ JANOCKO
FILE: AXX XX5 307 - HARTFORD, CT
September 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Glenn L. Formica, Esquire
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen, following our order entered on July 24, 2007.
 
We find the contentions regarding alleged ineffective assistance of counsel to be unavailing. Litigants are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys absent egregious circumstances. LeBlanc v. INS, 715 F.2d 685 (1st 1983); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Ineffective assistance of counsel is a denial of due process only if the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his or her case. Mejia Rodriguez v. INS, 178 F.3d 1139 (11th Cir. 1999); Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491 (9th Cir. 1986).
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by: 1) an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; 2) a showing that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and 3) a showing that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, or if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7th Cir. 2001).
 
On the one hand, the respondent argues that the assistance provided by former counsel Kevin Hoffkins was so egregious as to prejudice his case; on the other hand, it is argued that Mr. Hoffkins’ assistance was not such as to warrant filing a disciplinary complaint. In any event, it is not explained, and it is not evident to us, how the outcome would have been any different had Mr. Hoffkins provided any different assistance. The focus of our order was whether or not the Immigration Judge’s decision not to grant a continuance was valid, and we agreed that the respondent had failed to provide good cause for a continuance. The events related by the respondent in an effort to establish ineffective assistance of counsel do not provide for a different conclusion regarding the basis for the denial of a continuance by the Immigration Judge.
 
However, it is now reported that the respondent’s approved work visa has since become current. As the respondent appears eligible for adust his status, we will remand this case to permit him to pursue such relief.
 
*2 Given the lack of adverse factors in this case, and the non-opposition to the motion, we will grant it and remand the case to afford the respondent the opportunity to pursue his application for adjustment of status.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 3318154 (BIA)

IN RE: TERESA JEZIERSKI
FILE: AXX XX0 826 - CHICAGO, IL
September 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul Djurisic, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mirta J. Woodall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(l)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on June 7, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying her application for a waiver of the joint petition filing requirement under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4)(B). The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of her prior counsel. The motion will be denied.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent maintains that she was prejudiced by her former attorney’s failure to seek a waiver of the joint petition filing requirement under section 216(c)(4)(A) of the Act, or file an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act, 8U.S.C. § 1229b(b). In addition, she argues that her prior counsel erred in failing to file a brief with the Board in support of her appeal.
 
Although the respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec.637 (BIA 1988), she has failed demonstrate prejudice flowing from the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Mojsilovic v. INS, 156 F3d. 743, 749 (7th Cir. 1998); see also Pop v. INS, 279 F.3d 457, 460-461 (7th Cir. 2002). The respondent’s dissatisfaction with her former counsel’s strategy in pursuing relief is not grounds for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998). Further, the respondent has offered no evidence in her motion to establish that she would have prevailed if her former attorney had pursued these forms of relief. To the contrary, after questioning the respondent regarding potential forms of relief, the Immigration Judge concluded that she was ineligible for cancellation of removal based on her lack of a qualifying relative (Tr. at 12-17). See section 240A(b)(l)(D) of the Act. Finally, the respondent has failed to establish that the Board might have sustained her appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision had her prior counsel submitted a brief. The record reflects that the Board reviewed the Immigration Judge’s decision and affirmed the results of the decision, deeming it the final agency decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen fails to establish that the further development of the arguments contained in her Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) might have led to a different result in this matter. Based on the forgoing, we conclude that the respondent has not shown prejudice flowing from the alleged errors. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley

2007 WL 3318150 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARLOS ANTONIO SALDANA-URUCHIMA A.K.A. ANTONIO SALDANA, TONY SALANA, TONY MALLO AND ANTONIO YOLO
FILE: AXX XX1 464 - LOS FRESNOS, TX
September 25, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anita Delgado, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM The respondent is a native and citizen of Ecuador who pled guilty to two separate crimes of petit larceny in New York. The respondent hired an attorney who appeared telephonically on several occasions. On July 9, 2007, the Immigration Judge attempted to contact the respondent’s attorney, but was unable to do so (Tr. at 26-27; Letter dated July 20, 2007 from Jay C. Sherman). The Immigration Judge informed the respondent that his attorney was not available and asked the respondent what he wanted to do regarding his removal case. The respondent indicated that he would “rather just sign off for a deportation order.” (Tr. at 27). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent was removable as charged, and ordered the respondent removed (Tr. at 28-30). The respondent accepted the Immigration Judge’s order of removal as final and waived his right to an appeal (Tr. at 30-32). See also I.J. Dec. dated July 9, 2007.
 
On August 2, 2007, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal. He alleges that his due process rights were violated because his former attorney provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel. He contends that the Immigration Judge should have provided him with the opportunity to obtain new counsel, and that he may be eligible for a U visa. The respondent has not substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 385 a 2 (5th Cir.2001). Further, the respondent has not shown prejudice resulting from former counsel’s ineffectiveness. See Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993). The respondent did not ask for a continuance or request the opportunity to obtain new counsel. The respondent asked for an order of deportation and waived his right to an appeal. Further, the respondent does not argue that the order of removal was incorrect and has not demonstrated that he is prima facie eligible for relief from removal.
 
Since the record indicates that the respondent’s waiver was knowing and intelligent, and since the respondent has not demonstrated a basis for accepting the appeal despite the waiver, we lack jurisdiction over the instant appeal. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320, 1322 (BIA 2000); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(G) (summary dismissal of appeal appropriate where appeal is barred based on waiver of right to appeal). See also Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALEJANDRO NOVOA TORRES
FILE: AXX XX6 855 - NEW YORK, NY
September 21, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jon E. Jessen, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; section 212(c) waiver
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 18, 2005, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal based upon his failure to file an appeal brief after stating in his Notice of Appeal that such a brief would be filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E). On May 25, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance by the attorney who represented him on appeal to the Board. We have nothing in the record to indicate that the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the motion. The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), an individual may move to reopen his case but must generally do so within 90 days of the entry of the final order in the underlying proceeding. However, the 90-day filing deadline may be equitably tolled where the motion to reopen is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154 (2d Cir. 2006). In order to prevail on such a motion the movant must show that he exercised “due diligence in pursuing the case during the period ¿heº seeks to toll.” Ali v. Gonzales, 448 F.3d 515, 517 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must also meet the procedural requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Finally, the movant must establish that he suffered prejudice because of his attorney’s actions or failure to act. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638. The documents submitted with the motion to reopen establish that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and that he has shown he exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period he seeks to toll.
 
Upon review of the record before us we find it significant that the respondent was represented at his hearing by the same attorney who failed to file a timely brief during the appeal before the Board. We also find it significant that the respondent’s only criminal conviction of record occurred almost 15 years ago. Taking these factors into account, along with the lack of opposition by the DHS to the motion to reopen, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. While we conclude that the Immigration Judge properly set out the law concerning what factors should be considered and how the factors should be balanced in a section 212(c) waiver determination, we will provide the respondent with another opportunity to present evidence in support of his application. We note that one reason the Immigration Judge denied the section 212(c) waiver application previously was that the testimony and documents in the record showed that the respondent was a productive and contributing resident only during the few years prior to the 2004 hearing and the respondent did not account for other years of his residence in the United States (I.J. at 3). On remand, the respondent will be given an opportunity to account for his other years in the United States prior to the 2004 hearing, as well as for the years that have passed since the hearing. The DHS will also be given the opportunity to submit further evidence relevant to the respondent’s application for relief.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated October 18, 2005, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 28, 2004, is vacated insofar as it denied the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
