** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IDRISSA HAMIDOU
FILE: AXX XX0 325 - DETROIT, MI
September 19, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea J. Ferrara, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rosario S. Shoudy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s July 19, 2007, denial of his motion to reopen removal proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered on March 21, 2007. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) argues that the respondent’s appeal should be dismissed, and the Immigration Judge’s decision affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent appeared for an initial master calendar hearing on September 13, 2006. The hearing was rescheduled until March 21, 2007, to allow time for the adjudication of a pending I-130 visa petition (I.J. at 1). The respondent and his counsel failed to appear for the March 21, 2007, hearing. An in absentia order was entered ordering the respondent removed to Niger (I.J. at 2). On May 31, 2007, the respondent’s former counsel filed a motion to reopen, and on June 14, 2007, the Immigration Judge denied the motion (I.J. at 2). A second motion to reopen was filed on June 14, 2007, by respondent’s new attorney, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that the respondent had notice of the hearing, and that he failed to establish that his former attorney’s actions prejudiced his case (I.J. at 3). On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in not finding that the ineffective assistance of his former counsel amounted to exceptional circumstances sufficient to rescind the in absentia order. The respondent further argues that the Immigration Judge erred in requiring that the respondent demonstrate his former counsel’s ineffective assistance prejudiced his case. See Respondent’s brief at 4-5.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(B)(4)(iii); Matter of Guzman, 22 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1) of the Act.
 
*2 The respondent states on appeal that he did not appear for the March 21, 2007, hearing because his former counsel advised him he did not have to go back to court until his I-130 visa petition was adjudicated. The respondent argues that his former attorney’s misrepresentations amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel and should be considered “exceptional circumstances” for purposes of rescinding the in absentia order. Respondent’s brief at 4. In order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel the respondent must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
Along with the respondent’s motion to reopen which was filed with the Immigration Judge, he also submitted an affidavit detailing the interactions with his former attorney, a letter to his former attorney requesting a response to the respondent’s affidavit, and a written request for investigation of an attorney with the attorney grievance commission for the state of Michigan. Based on this documentation, it appears that the respondent has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in requiring that he further demonstrate his case was prejudiced by his former counsel’s actions in order to establish ineffective assistance of counsel. To support his argument the respondent cites to two Board decisions, over a decade old, that hold that in deportation proceedings under section 242B(c)(3) of the Act, prejudice need not be shown to obtain relief from an in absentia order. See Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603 (BIA 1996); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996). Section 242B(c)(3) of the Act was repealed by Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996 (IIRAIRA). The respondent has not cited to any current legal authority under the applicable section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act to support his argument. Furthermore, the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, where this case lies, has held that to establish ineffective assistance of counsel in order to rescind an in absentia order, a claimant must establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. See Denko v. I.N.S., 351 F.3d 717, 723-4 (6th Cir. 2003) citing Taniguchi v. Schultz, 303 F.3d 950, 958 (9th Cir. 2002) (to show ineffective assistance of counsel, a party must demonstrate that the actions of his/her attorney resulted in prejudice to the party). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*3 We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent has failed to demonstrate he was prejudiced by the actions of his previous counsel. See Matter of Santos, 19 I&N Dec. 105 (BIA 1984). First, the respondent received actual notice of the March 21, 2007, hearing date in open court on September 13, 2006 (Exh. 2). The Notice of Hearing contained warnings that he could be taken into custody and/or an order may be entered for removal in his absence if he failed to appear at the hearing. The notice was personally served on the respondent and his former attorney. See Denko, supra at 725 (failure to heed prior warnings regarding attendance at hearing and neglect to confirm understanding of attorney’s instruction does not result in fundamental unfairness). Additionally, the respondent has not demonstrated that but for his former attorney’s counsel that he need not attend another hearing until his I-130 petition was adjudicated, he would be entitled to continue residing in the United States. The respondent admitted the charges of removability at the September 13, 2006, hearing. The respondent has not demonstrated that he is the beneficiary of an approved I-130 visa petition so that he is eligible to apply for adjustment of status. Finally, the respondent has not demonstrated that he is eligible or has applied for any other form of relief from removal. See Denko, supra, at 723-25.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent has failed to establish that the in absentia order should be rescinded due to exceptional circumstances. In light of our denial of the respondent’s appeal, we decline to address his further request for stay of removal. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 3318632 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 3318683 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NORMA ILIANA RODRIGUEZ
FILE: AXX XX1 917 - SAN ANTONIO, TX
September 19, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa S. Brodyaga, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Christina Playton
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(ii)º - False claim to United States citizenship
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination; cancellation of removal; remand
 
The respondent has appealed from an Immigration Judge’s written decision dated May 4, 2007. The record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
A central issue in this case is whether the respondent derived United States citizenship from her mother, who was born in the United States. It is undisputed that the respondent was born in Mexico in 1981. Therefore, she is presumed to be an “alien” within the meaning of section 101(a)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(3), and must come forward with evidence to demonstrate that she is a United States citizen. Matter of Rodriguez-Tejedor, 23 I&N Dec. 153, 164 (BIA 2001) (citing Matter of Leyva, 16 I&N Dec. 118, 119 (BIA 1977)). The respondent contends that she derived United States citizenship through her mother pursuant to section 309(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1409(c). That provision provides that a child born outside the United States and out of wedlock acquires the “nationality status of the mother, if the mother had the nationality of the United States at the time of ¿the child’sº birth, and if the mother had previously been physically present in the United States or one of its outlying possessions for a continuous period of one year.”
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondent had provided insufficient evidence of citizenship to rebut the presumption of alienage arising by virtue of her foreign birth. In relevant part, he stated that the respondent’s mother, who was the only witness with direct knowledge of the dispositive facts relating to continuous presence, testified in a vague manner. The Immigration Judge stated that the testimony “lacks specificity in detail as to names, address, and most importantly dates. The Court cannot conclude that Respondent has come any where near the level of establishing by a preponderance of the credible evidence that she acquired U.S. citizenship.” I.J. at 7. The Immigration Judge later stated that he found that the respondent’s mother testified credibly “to the extent that she believed what she stated but she simply has insufficiently detailed, independent, corroborated knowledge to meet Respondent’s burden of proof as to the claim of U.S. citizenship.” I.J. at 8.
 
*2 We conclude that the Immigration Judge’s decision, as presently constituted, does not provide us with a meaningful basis for appellate review. See generally Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). We are unable to ascertain the extent to which the testimony of the respondent’s mother was reliable; the Immigration Judge’s statements on page 7 as to vagueness and lack of detail imply an adverse credibility finding, but a positive credibility finding was made on page 8. Further, the Immigration Judge’s decision does not contain findings as to the credibility of a witness who testified at length with regard to whether the respondent’s mother had made inconsistent statements about her continuous physical presence in the United States. Also, the Immigration Judge did not specify what type of corroborative evidence the respondent should have presented, and did not articulate why such evidence was necessary to the burden of proof. Absent these specific findings, we are unable to assess the arguments of the parties on appeal with respect to whether the preponderance of the evidence establishes that the respondent is a United States citizen. Therefore we will remand the record for more complete findings on any disputed issues of fact. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv) (Board will not engage in factfinding in the course of deciding appeals).
 
As an independent basis for remanding this record to the Immigration Judge, we agree with the respondent’s arguments regarding ineffective assistance of counsel. In support of a motion to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel,¿FN1º the respondent has submitted an affidavit setting forth in detail the nature of her problems with the representation by former counsel (see Declaration, dated August 21, 2007). A complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and former counsel was provided an opportunity to respond. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), qff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Former counsel’s response included an explanation that the respondent’s application for relief, which was deemed as abandoned by the Immigration Judge, was not timely filed due to poor communication and a mistake in the attorney’s office. See Respondent’s Exh. C. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not responded in any level of detail to the assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, because it appears that the respondent has complied with the threshold requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra, and since the respondent, as the record now stands, appears prima facie eligible for relief, the motion will be granted.
 
Finally, we find no basis to remand to a different Immigration Judge, as requested by the respondent. We have reviewed the several reasons asserted by the respondent in support of such a course of action. These reasons, taken singly or in conjunction, are insufficient to justify remand to another Immigration Judge. Over the course of these lengthy proceedings, the Immigration Judge has conducted the hearings in a generally fair way, and we see no persuasive evidence of bias on the part of the Immigration Judge, such as to require a remand to a different Immigration Judge. See Matter of G-, 20 I&N Dec. 764, 781 (BIA 1993). We note that, to the extent that the respondent complains of testimony that was stricken by the Immigration Judge (see Tr. at 94), the respondent is free to present an affidavit or other proffer of evidence to the Immigration Judge detailing the stricken testimony, and is also free to move that a new evidentiary hearing be allowed.
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4) (new evidence submitted on appeal may be construed as a motion to remand).

2007 WL 3318684 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SILVERIO ROJAS-VELAZCO A.K.A. SILVERIO ROJAS-VELASCO
FILE: AXX XX3 735 - HOUSTON, TX
September 19, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michael R. Leppala
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The appeal is dismissed. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the June 20, 2007, decision of the Immigration Judge. The respondent contests the Immigration Judge’s determination that he has been convicted of an aggravated felony and is ineligible for relief from removal. The record reflects that the respondent has a Texas state conviction for burglary with intent to commit other felonies, to wit, assault in a habitation under Texas Penal Code Ann. § 30.02 (Vernon 2003). We uphold the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G) (I.J. at 2; Exh. 2). We support the Immigration Judge’s determination as consistent with Taylor v. United States, 495 U.S. 575, 598 (1990) (“the generic, contemporary meaning of burglary contains at least the following elements: an unlawful or unprivileged entry into, or remaining in, a building or other structure, with the intent to commit a crime.”) See also United States v. Ortega-Gonzaga, 490 F.3d 393 (5th Cir. 2007) (definition of state burglary conviction for purposes of federal sentencing guidelines); cf. Shepard v. United States, 544 U.S. 13 (2005) (modified categorical approach applicable if a divisible statute).
 
We have considered the respondent’s additional arguments on appeal and the evidence regarding the citizenship status of his family members. However, we do not find that he has demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel with respect to his removal proceedings, and we cannot consider the competence of his representation during his criminal case. Further, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established his eligibility for relief from removal, and so there is no basis to remand.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 3318684 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 4699795 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LYNDON LINCOLN JORDAN A.K.A. LYNDON L. JORDAN A.K.A. LINDEN JORDON A.K.A. LYNDON JORDON
FILE: AXX XX1 680-OAKDALE, LA
September 17, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert B. Weir
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B)) (not sustained)
 
Sec.237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Ineffective assistance of counsel; cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s May 21, 2007, decision. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, g U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), for having been convicted of a controlled substance violation. The Immigration Judge, incorporating his November 8, 2004, decision, also denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(a), as a of discretion, and ordered him removed from the United States. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The Board reviews findings of facts by an Immigration Judge, including those as to the credibility of testimony, to determine whether the findings are “clearly erroneous.” $ C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i)¿FN1º.The Board may review all questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues on appeal from decisions of the Immigration Judge “de novo.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iii).
 
The record shows that on November 2, 2002, the respondent was convicted in the Criminal Court of New York, New York, for the offense of criminal sale of marihuana in the fourth degree, in violation of section 221.40 of the New York Penal Code. The respondent’s attorney, at a hearing conducted on May 21,2007, conceded that the respondent was removable for having been convicted of a controlled substance violation. The respondent argues on appeal that the professional negligence of the attorney representing him in Immigration Court deprived him of due process. The respondent avers that the ineffective assistance of counsel was reflected by the counsel’s concession that the respondent was removable (Tr. at 4). Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, the Board has found that certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities.  of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra).
 
*2 The United States Court.of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this case arises, held in Miranda-Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994) that an alien seeking to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must show not only that counsel was ineffective, but also that the alien suffered “substantial prejudice” as a result of counsel’s errors. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553,558 (BIA 2003) (finding that the respondent had failed to show the requisite prejudice to warrant consideration of his late appeal on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel). While the respondent argues that, but for his attorney’s neglect, he would have prevailed as evidence of removability was not submitted, the record shows that respondent’s previous counsel in no way prejudiced the respondent.
 
The record, as noted above, reflects that the respondent was convicted in the Criminal Court of New York, New York, for the offense of criminal sale of marihuana in the fourth degree. Section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act provides that any alien who at any time after admission has been convicted of a violation relating to a controlled substance, other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of 30 grams or less of marihuana, is deportable. We fred that, as the record showed that the respondent’s November 2002 conviction involved the criminal sale of marihuana, this conviction rendered him removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i). The fact that there was no showing of either the mount of marihuana or whether the sale was for remuneration is of no consequence as the respondent’s offense does not fall within the personal use exception. Accordingly, he has not identified how the actions ofhls previous counsel resulted in “substantial prejudice” to him, and his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is without merit. Absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Thus, the respondent is now precluded from contesting the Immigration Judge’s determination that he is removable based on that conviction.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Ouyana, and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, further argues that the Immigration Judge erred when he denied the respendent’s application for cancellation of removal as a matter of discretion. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent in this case does not merit, as a matter of discretion, a grant of cancellatiun of removal. In adjudicating an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, the adverse factors evidencing the respondent’s undesirability as a permanent resident must be balanced with the favorable factors and social and humane considerations presented in his behalf to determine whether the grant of cancellation of removal is in the best interests of this country. Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998); Matter of Marin, 16 I&N Dec. 581 (BIA 1978). Although the respondent established that he had lived in the United States since 1981, as a lawful permanent resident, and has family ties here, we find that his continued criminal history in this country outweighs the favorable factors of record.
 
*3 Among the factors deemed adverse to an alien are the nature and underlying circumstances of the removability ground at issue, the presence of additional significant violations of this country’s immigration laws, the existence of a criminal record and, if so, its nature, recency, and seriousness, and the presence of other evidence indicative of a respundent’s bad character or undesirability as a permanent resident of this country. Favorable considerations include such factors as family ties within the United States, residence of long duration in this country (particularly when the inception of residence occurred while the respondent was of young age), and evidence of hardship to the respondent and family if deportation occurs. Additional favorable factors include service in this country’s Armed Forces, a history of employment, the existence of property or business ties, evidence of value and service to the community, proof of genuine rehabilitation if a criminal record exists, and other evidence attesting to a respondent’s good character (e.g., affidavits from family, friends, and responsible eomrnunity representatives).
 
We conclude that significant negative factors militate against a favorable exercise of discretion in this instance. His lengthy period of criminal activity includes a June 1988 conviction for unlawful possession of marihuana, an August 1989 conviction for criminal sale of marihuana in the fourth degree, a February 1992 conviction for criminal sale of marihuana in the fourth degree, a November 2002 conviction for criminal sale of marihuana in the fourth degree, and a May 2004 conviction for criminal possession of marihuana in the fifth degree (EOIR-42A). We also note that the respondent has testified that he has been smoking marihuana habitually for some 5 to 6 years, and has never voluntarily entered a drug treatment program (Tr. at 32).
 
The respondent has also adduced a number of countervailing equities. The respondent entered the United States in 1981 as a lawful permanent resident, although he has been involved with the criminal justice system for a substantial period of time. The respondent, who has never been married, was briefly employed for periods on 1988, 1996, and 2000 (Tr. at 12-13, 17-18). He has one United States citizen child, age 18, who lives in Miami and who the respondent has had no contact with for some 4 to 5 years (Tr. at 14-15). His mother, brother, and sister live in the United States, and the respondent, who has always lived with his mother, owns no property, and has no money or other assets (Tr. at 26-33). He has not served in the armed forces, and does not appear to have been a person of any particular value to his community at large. His application for relief is not supported by testimony, or affidavits, from family.
 
Turning to the issue of rehabilitation, we consider it to be an important factor in light of the nature of the respondent’s criminal record. The Immigration Judge, after delineating the respondent’s criminal activity, found no evidence of genuine rehabilitation, noting the lack of affidavits from family or friends attesting to the respondent’s good character (2004 LJ. Dec. at 5).
 
*4 In balancing the various factors in the respondent’s case, we take note of his favorable equities. However, when we weigh these equities against the adverse factors of his extensive history of criminal conduct, and his substance abuse, and our lack of confidence as to his rehabilitation, we must conclude that a favorable exercise of discretion would not be in the best interest of the United States in tlais instance.
 
• Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

This regulation applies to a Notice of Appeal filed after September 25, 2002. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(f); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MAURICIO SALGADO PORTILLO
FILE: AXX XX2 367 - HOUSTON, TX
September 17, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Andrea W. Kelly, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James Edward Manning
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 3, 2007, decision. The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of his request for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred in denying him a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act as a matter of discretion. In evaluating whether relief under section 212(h) is warranted, we balance the adverse factors evidencing an alien’s undesirability as a permanent resident with the social and humane considerations presented on his behalf. See Matter of Mendez, 21 I&N Dec. 296 (BIA 1996). The respondent contends that the Immigration Judge did not give sufficient weight to his claim of rehabilitation and hardship to his spouse.¿FN1º We agree with the Immigration Judge that even considering the hardship to his wife and four children, it is not in the best interest of the United States for the respondent to remain in this country (I.J. at 9-11). The respondent has an extensive and recent criminal record. For example, in 2000, he was convicted of resisting arrest (Exh. 2B). In 2002, he was convicted of burglary of a vehicle and sentenced to 100 days’ confinement (I.J. at 1; Exh. 2B). In 2003, he was again convicted of burglary of a vehicle and sentenced to 60 days’ imprisonment (I.J. at 2; Exhs. 2B, 9D). He was convicted on three occasions, including once in 2003 and 2005, of driving while intoxicated (Exh. 9D; Tr. at 45-46; I.J. at 5). Based on the foregoing, we find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s discretionary denial of a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
The respondent also claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The record of proceedings includes a motion to reopen and reconsider filed with the Immigration Court, in which the respondent claims that his prior counsel was ineffective. The Immigration Court lacked jurisdiction to consider the motion because the respondent’s appeal had already been entered with the Board. In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires; (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. The motion filed with the Immigration Court contains a grievance form but does not indicate that the respondent’s prior counsel was notified of the allegations. Therefore, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Furthermore, he has not shown that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s alleged misconduct. See Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent has not shown that the outcome of his case would have been different but for his prior counsel’s alleged misconduct. Based on the foregoing, the respondent has not established a due process violation resulting from his prior counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent also contends that the Immigration Judge erred in not allowing sufficient testimony regarding his rehabilitation. However, the respondent does not cite examples of any instances in which the Immigration Judge prevented him from presenting such testimony. Furthermore, the respondent does not indicate on appeal how he has rehabilitated.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ADDIL GIBBS
FILE: AXX-XX7-549 - MIAMI, FL
September 14, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Georgia B. Gillett, Esquire¿FN1º
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s May 11, 2007, decision ordering the respondent removed to Trinidad and Tobago. The Notice of Appeal was filed on June 11, 2007. While the appeal has been pending, on August 9, 2007, the respondent’s counsel filed a Motion to Withdraw Appeal. The motion indicated the respondent wants to be removed as soon as possible.
 
However, on August 22, 2007, the respondent filed a note to the court indicating that he never requested his attorney to withdraw his appeal. Included in the note is a copy of a letter to the respondent’s attorney requesting the case be mailed to his mother, and stating the attorney’s services are no longer needed. The letter appears to be dated in July, but the day and year are not clear from the copy. There is also a copy of a June 20, 2007, letter to the attorney from the respondent’s mother indicating that services are no longer required and, inter alia, requesting all documents to be sent to a Mr. Sampson. There is also an August 8, 2007, letter from the attorney to the respondent indicating that, pursuant to his request, the Motion to Withdraw Appeal was filed, and documents related to an application for adjustment of status via the respondent’s wife are being forwarded, as well as other documents and the Board’s appeal briefing schedule and transcript.
 
There is no allegation of, or compliance with the procedures to establish, an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, so the documents will be considered in the most logical light. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). It appears that the respondent terminated the services of the attorney by July 2007, at the latest. The appeal brief was due on August 10, 2007. The Motion to Withdraw the appeal was not filed until August 8, 2007 and this motion also indicates the respondent’s entire immigration file was forwarded to the respondent on that date. The August 8, 2007, letter from the attorney forwarding material to the respondent does not indicate, as stated in the motion, that the entire immigration file was forwarded to the respondent; just that certain documents were sent to the respondent. Notwithstanding, the respondent’s letter notifying the Board that he never intended to withdraw his appeal was timely filed, although after the due date for the brief. Given the inconsistencies in these documents, the attorney’s motion to withdraw the appeal will be denied.
 
While the motion filed by the attorney does not indicate that she wishes to withdraw from the case, in light of the respondent’s August 22, 2007, note to the Board and supporting letters, we will construe the motion as such. The motion to withdraw from the case by counsel will be granted.
 
*2 There has been ample time during which the respondent was represented by counsel to file an appeal brief, yet none was filed. No request for an extension has been received. There is no allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel. Consequently, we also consider the merits of the appeal.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that he should be allowed to apply for adjustment of status via his wife because he is eligible for a section 212(h) waiver for his criminal offenses. However, the June 16, 2006, drug trafficking conviction is an aggravated felony and a 212(h) waiver is therefore not available. The respondent mentions that he is challenging the conviction in criminal court because he was not informed of the immigration consequences of accepting a plea in that case. However, there is no claim that the motion to vacate has been granted. Consequently, we are still bound by the underlying conviction. See Section 101(a)(48)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(48)(A); Resendez-Alcaraz v. United States Attorney General, 383 F.3d 1262 (11th Cir. 2004); Matter of Punu, 22 I&N Dec. 224 (BIA 1998); cf. Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006) (conviction vacated for failure of the trial court to give the alien defendant the statutorily required advisal of the possible immigration consequences of a guilty plea is no longer a conviction for immigration purposes).
 
Accordingly, the motion to withdraw the appeal is denied. The motion to withdraw as counsel is granted. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

As this order allows the withdrawal of counsel, a copy of the order will be mailed directly to the respondent as well as the attorney.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BENNY ESPINOSA
FILE: AXX XX2 220 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
September 5, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rodel E. Rodis, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reissuance of Board decision
 
*1 This case was initially before us on March 2, 2005, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision which found the respondent removable from the United States. On December 27, 2005, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen that was rejected due to filing defects. On January 19, 2006, the respondent filed a corrected motion to reopen. On February 23, 2006, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s March 2, 2005, final order was entered. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
The respondent subsequently filed a petition for review of our denial of his motion to reopen with the Ninth Circuit. The government filed an unopposed motion to remand, which the Ninth Circuit granted. In its motion, the government requested that the case be remanded so that the Board may be given an opportunity to reevaluate our February 23, 2006, decision, in light of the Ninth Circuit’s recently issued decision in Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582 (9th Cir. 2006) (finding that ineffective assistance of counsel entitled alien to tolling of filing deadline for motion to reopen), and to consider whether tolling of the motion to reopen deadline is appropriate in this case.¿FN1º
 
Upon reconsideration of our February 23, 2006, decision, we find that we must still deny the respondent’s motion to reopen, as it was untimely filed, and he has not shown that he should be exempt from the 90-day deadline for filing such a motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent requested that we reopen his case and reissue our March 2, 2005, decision, so that he may timely file a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit. He claims that his attorney, Ted Laguatan (“Laguatan”), was out of the country when the Board decision was issued, and Laguatan did not inform him of the decision until after the expiration of the deadline for filing a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit.¿FN2º
 
To the extent that the respondent has claimed that reopening and reissuance of his decision is warranted due to Laguatan’s ineffective assistance, the respondent has not attempted to comply with any of the procedural requirements when raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and approved by the Ninth Circuit. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582 (9th Cir. 2006). In Ray v. Gonzales, supra, the Ninth Circuit has acknowledged that the procedural requirements in Lozada serve important policy goals of ensuring the legitimacy of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and in developing an adequate factual basis to adjudicate such a claim. The Ninth Circuit further recognized that there are cases when the requirements of Lozada need not be rigidly applied, such as when there is an adequate basis in the record for an ineffective assistance complaint and when the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one. See id. and cases cited therein. In support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent’s sole submission consisted of an unsworn letter alleging, in general terms, that Laguatan gave him notice of the Board’s decision only after the expiration of the deadline for filing a petition of review with the circuit court. The respondent’s minimal submission in his motion does not satisfy the policy goals underlying the threshold procedural requirements in Lozada. The respondent likewise has not demonstrated that circumstances exist that would justify non-compliance with any of these requirements. Accordingly, we are unable to consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance claim against previous counsel.
 
*2 Because the respondent has not met the procedural requirements in Lozada, we need not consider whether equitable tolling of the 90-day filing deadline is warranted due to ineffective assistance of counsel. However, in light of the language in the government’s motion to remand, we will further address the issue of equitable tolling. Even assuming arguendo that we can consider the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel despite his failure to comply with the Lozada requirements, we have not been provided with a sufficient basis upon which to conclude that equitable tolling should be applied in his case. The respondent has not identified, either in his motion letter or on remand, what period of time he seeks to toll. His letter does not indicate when he was made aware of the Board’s decision, and/or Laguatan’s alleged negligence. The respondent has neither claimed nor demonstrated that he exercised due diligence in filing the present motion to reopen as required to establish eligibility for equitable tolling. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d at 589, fn. 5 (finding that alien who acted with diligence in filing his claim after learning of counsel’s ineffectiveness was entitled to equitable tolling of the filing deadlines); see also Valeriano v. Gonzales, 474 F.3d 669 (9th Cir. 2007); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003).
 
After reevaluating the respondent’s motion to reopen in light of the Ninth Circuit’s case in Ray v. Gonzalez, supra, and the government’s motion to remand, we are unable to conclude that the respondent has adequately demonstrated that his untimely motion to reopen should be granted. We likewise note that the respondent has not argued nor has he adequately shown that exceptional circumstances warrant the exercise of our limited authority to sua sponte reopen his proceedings. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

DISSENTING OPINION:
Roger A. Pauley
*1 I respectfully dissent. I would find that the respondent is bound by the concession of his attorney that the battery offense was a crime involving moral turpitude. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N dec. 377, 382-3 (BIA 1986). The fact that, in retrospect, the concession may be shown to have been ill-advised does not mean that the respondent can escape its consequences, particularly in the absence of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).

2007 WL 2825162 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OLGA ACOSTA DE SCHAPER A.K.A. OLGA SCHAPER
FILE: AXX XX3 299 - NEW YORK, NY
September 5, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Adam P. Feller
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on June 19, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b, as a matter of discretion. In addition, we concluded that the respondent is ineligible for Temporary Protected Status (“TPS”) because of her criminal record. See section 244(c)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254a(c)(2)(B)(i). We also rejected the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim finding that the respondent the had not sufficiently shown patent error or complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Zheng v. United States Dep’t of Justice, 409 F.3d 43, 47 (2d Cir. 2005). Finally, we noted that the respondent’s newly offered evidence on appeal was not sufficient to warrant a remand. The respondent has timely filed a motion requesting reopening and reconsideration. The respondent has not identified any error of fact or law in our previous detailed decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2007); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24-I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing the requirements for motions to reconsider). Furthermore, the respondent’s motion fails to comply with both the statutory and regulatory requirements for reopening. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent has not submitted or identified additional evidence demonstrating that further hearings are warranted. See Matter of S-V-, 22 I&N Dec. 1306, 1307-1308 (BIA 2000); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied and, as a result, the respondent is ordered removed to El Salvador.¿FN1º
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s appellate fee waiver request is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3).

2007 WL 2825159 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIRZA MASOOD AHMED A.K.A. MASOOD MIRZA AHMED
FILE: AXX XX1 385 - BALTIMORE, MD
September 5, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert L. Oswald, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
The respondent appeals the February 14, 2006, decision of an Immigration Judge finding that the respondent had been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude and of an aggravated felony, under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F), that he, therefore, was removable, as charged, and denying the motion to terminate. The appeal will be sustained.
 
The respondent is a 61-year-old native of India and citizen of Pakistan, who was admitted to the United States on or about June 9, 1992, as a lawful permanent resident. The respondent’s record of conviction, admitted into evidence below, demonstrates that on February 9, 1998, the respondent was convicted on a plea of guilty of battery committed on May 17, 1997, in the Circuit Court of Mercer County, West Virginia, and was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of 12 months for that offense (Exh. 2). The execution of the sentence was suspended and he was placed on probation. He was released from probation and the remainder of the sentence was suspended on August 6, 1998. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) charged the respondent with being subject to removal from the United States based upon that conviction, asserting that the respondent is removable under sections 237(a)(2)(A)(i) and (iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1237(a)(2)(A)(i) and (iii), as an alien who has been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude committed within 5 years after the date of admission and of an aggravated felony at any time after admission into the United States. The DHS alleged that the respondent’s conviction constitutes an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, because it constitutes a crime of violence for which the respondent was sentenced to at least 1 year in prison. The Immigration Judge ruled that because the respondent was convicted of a battery that involved the intentional infliction of harm, it constituted an aggravated felony and that the charging documents demonstrated he had committed a crime involving moral turpitude.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that battery in the State of West Virginia under W.Va. Code § 61-2-9(c) is a misdemeanor, and as such cannot be an aggravated felony under section 16(b) of title 18 United States Code. The Immigration Judge found he was not convicted of an aggravated felony under title 18 section 16(b) of the United States Code. The respondent further argues that his conviction is not for a crime of violence under title 18 section 16(a), because it does not involve the use of force as an element of the crime. One commits the crime of battery under West Virginia Code § 61-1-9(c): “¿iºf any person unlawfully and intentionally makes physical contact of an insulting or provoking nature with the person of another or unlawfully and intentionally causes physical harm to anther person, …” The United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit, in which this case arises, has held that when a definition of a crime is ambiguous and will not necessarily provide the answer as to whether a particular conviction is for a “crime of violence,” it is permissible to look beyond the definition of the crime to examine the facts contained in the charging document on which the defendant was convicted. See United States v. Kirksey, 138 F.3d 120, 124 (4th Cir.), cert denied, 525 U.S. 849 (1998).
 
*2 Because the intentional employment of physical force against a person or thing is not an “element” of this offense, the offense is not a crime of violence under 18 U.S.C. § 16(a). Even if the respondent had pled guilty to the portion of the statute criminalizing unlawfully and intentionally causing physical harm to another person, he would not necessarily have used force to cause that harm. See Leocal v. INS, 543 U.S. 1 (2004). Moreover, the statement of charges does not refer to any degree of harm such as pain or bruising and it is not clear from the record that the respondent was not found guilty of “physical contact of an insulting or provoking nature.” Accordingly, he was not convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
The Immigration Judge found that battery in this case was a crime of moral turpitude. The respondent admitted through counsel that he was convicted of a crime of moral turpitude (Tr. at 8); however, the Immigration Judge relied on the record of conviction to find the respondent was convicted of a crime of moral turpitude and the respondent has disputed that finding on appeal. The respondent has not claimed ineffective assistance of counsel in regard to the admission made by counsel. Nevertheless, as this issue was adjudicated below, we will consider whether the record supports the Immigration Judge’s finding.
 
“It is the inherent nature of the crime as defined by statute and interpreted by the courts and as limited and described by the record of conviction which determines whether the offense is one involving moral turpitude.” Matter of Short, 20 I&N Dec. 136, 137 (BIA 1989). When the statute includes some offenses which involve moral turpitude and some which do not, we look to the record of conviction (indictment, plea, verdict, and sentence) to determine the offense of which he was convicted. Id. He was originally charged with first degree sexual assault. The conduct described in the criminal complaint would readily meet the definition of crime involving moral turpitude. In this case, the respondent pled guilty to the lesser included offense of battery and the recitation of facts included in the criminal complaint in support of another charge cannot be used to demonstrates that the respondent was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude. See Fernandez-Ruiz v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 1159 (9th Cir. 2006). Therefore, we cannot agree that the respondent was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude on the record before us.
 
Based on the foregoing, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained; the respondent is found not removable.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The order of removal is vacated and proceedings are terminated.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2825159 (BIA)

2007 WL 2825216 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GONZALO EMILIO TAVERA URIBE
FILE: AXX XX1 337 - MIAMI, FL
August 29, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mayra Joli, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on October 10, 2006, because he failed to appear for a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on March 30, 2007. Therein, the respondent argued that he did not receive the Notice of Hearing. We note that he was personally served with the Notice to Appear that advised him of the requirement to provide his most updated address with the Immigration Court. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on May 16, 2007. The Immigration Judge noted that the hearing notice was mailed to the address reflected on the Notice to Appear, 11521 NW 10 Street, Pembroke Pines, Florida 33026, and the respondent had provided no evidence to establish that he did not receive the notice. On May 18, 2007, the respondent requested reconsideration before the Immigration Judge. Therein, the respondent indicated that he had filed a complaint against his former counsel, and counsel’s negligence caused his failure to appear for the hearing. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on June 21, 2007, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
A motion to reconsider is a request that the Board or an Immigration Judge reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked. Matter of Ramos, 23 I&N Dec. 336, 338 (BIA 2002) (quoting Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.2 (BIA 1991)). A motion to reconsider challenges the original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Matter of Cerna, supra, at 402. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, as opposed to a motion to reopen, which seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. Id. at 403; see also section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that it was correct to deny reconsideration based on the record that existed when the motion to reopen was adjudicated. The Notice of Hearing was mailed to the respondent’s last known address, and the respondent failed to establish in the reconsideration request that the Immigration Judge erred in this determination. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny reopening.
 
We further note that the respondent argued in his motion to reconsider that attorney negligence may have caused his failure to appear. We characterize this argument as a request for reopening based on exceptional circumstances rather than an argument pertaining to lack of notice. A request for rescission of an order of removal based on exceptional circumstances must be filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. Because a request for reopening based on exceptional circumstances was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of removal, it is barred from consideration. In addition, we characterize the respondent’s motion to reconsider as a second request for reopening. Under the regulation, an alien may only file one motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii).
 
*2 We would also deny reopening notwithstanding the above bars. Initially, we note that it is unclear if the respondent notified his prior counsel that he had accused him of ineffective assistance of counsel, and allowed him an opportunity to respond to the motion. Dakane v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 399 F.3d 1269 (11th Cir. 2005); Gbaya v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 342 F.3d 1219 (11th Cir. 2003) (noting that it is required that an alien establish substantial if not exact compliance with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988)). The respondent indicates that his current attorney advised former counsel of the accusation, but we note no evidence that clearly indicates that such notice was given. Even if the procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel were established, we cannot find that counsel was negligent in these proceedings. The respondent states in his declaration that former counsel advised him that he need not appear in court until he received the Notice of Hearing. Such advice appears to be proper. We cannot ascertain from the record any evidence that former counsel suggested that the respondent need not appear for the hearing after he received the notice. The respondent presumably received the Notice of Hearing, and he was under an obligation to appear. His failure to do so has not been shown to be the result of attorney negligence.
 
We further cannot grant the respondent’s motion to reopen that was filed with the Board on August 20, 2007. Therein, the respondent has submitted an order from the criminal court that shows that his motion to vacate his plea on the charge of possession of cannabis was granted on August 6, 2007. It is unclear whether the criminal court did so for substantive or rehabilitative reasons. Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (if a court vacates an alien’s conviction for reasons solely related to rehabilitation or immigration hardships, rather than on the basis of a procedural or substantive defect in the underlying criminal proceedings, the conviction is not eliminated for immigration purposes). Even if the respondent’s conviction was vacated for substantive reasons, he remains convicted of attempted burglary of a structure that rendered him removable from the United States under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act. Inasmuch as the respondent is a removable alien, we see no reason to terminate these proceedings or remand the record to determine the reason why the criminal court vacated his possession of cannabis conviction. The motion is denied.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2825216 (BIA)
End of Document

2007 WL 2825213 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAGDA AIDA GARCIA-TAX
FILE: AXX XX8 268 - LOS ANGELES, CA
August 29, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas A. Lappin, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered deported from the United States on March 3, 1998, because she failed to appear for her hearing. She filed a motion to reopen on April 23, 2007, but the Immigration Judge denied it on May 23, 2007. This appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she was not adequately notified of the hearing. However, as noted by the Immigration Judge and the respondent on page 2 of her brief, a Notice of Hearing sent to an alien’s attorney of record is proper notice. See 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5; Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (1985). In these proceedings, the respondent received personal service of the Order to Show Cause and her former attorney was sent the hearing notice via certified mail. It also appears that an individual received the Notice of Hearing on behalf of the respondent’s counsel as evidenced by the signature on the Form 3811. The signature is illegible, but we will presume that it was received by former counsel or someone acting on his behalf. Inasmuch as the respondent received the charging document and her counsel received the hearing notice, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent was properly notified of the hearing.
 
The Immigration Judge further found that reopening of these proceedings based on exceptional circumstances was inappropriate because the motion was filed untimely, and the respondent had not pursued with due diligence reopening after learning that she was a possible victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Albillo-DeLeon v. Gonzales, 410 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir.2005) (the limitations period is tolled until the petitioner “definitively learns”of counsel’s fraud) ;Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). The Board will generally presume that an alien learns of counsel’s negligence on the day alleged in the alien’s statement or affidavit. See Ghahremani v. Gonzales, —F.3d—, 2007 WL 2332069 (9th Cir. 2007) (noting that the Board must accept an alien’s statement regarding when he learned that he may have been the victim of attorney negligence unless the representation is unbelievable). Herein, the respondent indicates in her statement that she learned in 2000 that she was subject to an order of deportation when she attempted to renew her application for a work permit. According to her statement, she was confused by the information because she did not know that an Immigration Judge had ordered her deportation. She indicated that she consulted some attorneys, but was advised to just wait and see what happened. The respondent further indicated that she consulted her former attorney, and he could not explain why she was not notified of her hearing.
 
*2 Based on this scenario, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not seek reopening with due diligence. Singh v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1090 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent consulted with counsel in 2000 and was aware that she was subject to an order of deportation. She apparently chose to ignore her immigration status possibly under the hope that the government would not seek to deport her from the United States. The Immigration Judge correctly refrained from equitably tolling the period for seeking reopening. The Immigration Judge was also correct to find that the request for reopening based on exceptional circumstances was untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2825213 (BIA)

2007 WL 2825132 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALVARO DAVID DELGADILLO
FILE: AXX XX9 548 - DENVER, CO
August 28, 2007
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laura L. Lichter, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Special motion to reopen to seek 212(c) relief
 
*1 This case was last before us on April 30, 1998, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent ineligible for relief from deportation. On May 14, 2007, the respondent filed the instant “Special Motion to Reopen to Seek 212(c) Relief.” The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not submitted a response to the respondent’s motion. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
As recognized by the respondent, his present motion is untimely, as a special motion to reopen to file for 212(c) relief must have been filed on or before April 26, 2005. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(h). The respondent seeks to overcome the untimeliness of his motion by claiming that the ineffective assistance of prior counsel, L. Ari Weitzhandler (“Weitzhandler”), aggravated by his unavailing consultations with immigration attorneys, Philip Alterman (“Alterman”) and Jeff Joseph (“Joseph”), caused the delay in the filing of his present motion to reopen. We are unable to consider the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as he has not complied with the procedural prerequisites as outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA). While the respondent submitted a printout which purports to show that a letter was sent to an “Ari” via e-mail, we do not find that this is sufficient indication that Weitzhandler was properly notified of the allegations leveled against him by the respondent. The respondent, for instance, has not provided any evidence linking the generically-named e-mail to Weitzhandler, or any evidence that Weitzhandler received this e-mail or any notification. Likewise, there is no evidence, apart from counsel’s bare assertion in the motion, that a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Id. We note that statements of counsel regarding whether a bar complaint was filed, and whether former counsel was notified of the allegations, are not evidence (Motion at 4-5). See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980).
 
Moreover, to the extent that the respondent has implied that Alterman’s and Joseph’s conduct prevented him from becoming aware of Weitzhandler’s alleged misfeasance and contributed to the delay in the filing of the present motion to reopen, we note that the respondent has not filed a claim of ineffective assistance against these attorneys pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra. Absent such a claim, and in the absence of any other evidence that would otherwise corroborate the respondent’s assertions with regard to these meetings, the nature and the substance of his conversations with these attorneys, including what these attorneys may have represented to him that “lulled ¿himº into a sense of security,” we do not find there is a sufficient reliable basis upon which we can conclude that the respondent’s actions following these alleged meetings with these attorneys were reasonable under the circumstances.
 
*2 In addition to his failure to comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, we are also unable to find that the respondent has shown that he exercised due diligence as required to warrant equitable tolling of the April 26, 2005, filing deadline of this motion. Although the respondent claimed that he began to be concerned about Weitzhandler’s unavailability as early as September 2004, he did not retain another attorney to take his case until 2007. The respondent claims that he contacted attorney Alterman and told him that Weitzhandler was not returning his phone calls. Despite the respondent’s difficulties in reaching Weitzhandler, Alterman apparently succeeded in contacting Weitzhandler himself. The respondent alleged that Alterman told him, after speaking to Weitzhandler, that “it sounded like the motion was simply pending” and that if the respondent wished that he take his case, the respondent needed to get his file from Weitzhandler. The respondent claimed to have gone to Weitzhandler’s office multiple times, but the office was always locked. In reviewing the respondent’s affidavit, we are left with the question as to why, knowing that Alterman had the means and the ability to reach Weitzhandler, the respondent did not press upon Alterman to contact Weitzhandler in order to obtain his file. It is likewise unclear as to why, although he called Weitzhandler’s office “15-20 times” throughout 2005 without receiving any response, and given Weitzhandler’s alleged behavior in September of 2004, the respondent did not retain the services of another attorney to handle his case in 2005, or after he was informed that Weizhandler was “no longer practicing law” in 2006.
 
As such, because the respondent has neither complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, for claims involving ineffective assistance of counsel, nor has he established that equitable tolling should be applied in his case, we will deny as untimely filed the respondent’s special motion to reopen for 212(c) relief. See Infanzon v. Ashcroft, 386 F.3d 1359 (10th Cir. 2004). In view of the foregoing, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
Gereld S. Hurwitz
FOR THE BOARD
2007 WL 2825132 (BIA)
